THE RED PAGE.
The Return to the Native.
KIPLING’S Kim, as a book, is better than
the maga.-fragments promised : possibly
it is Kipling’s best book : certainly it is
the best finished, completest. For sug-
gesting otherwise, pardon ! “ Children should
not see a carpet on the loom till the pattern is
made plain ” says Kim’s friend Mahbub Ali, the
horse-coper. Nor (though the precaution with
which the local painter turns his pot-boiler be
singularly superfluous) critics. “George Eliot”
knew that; steadfastly (in her latter days) refusing
serial profits for the sake of Art and the
Work and the impression totus, teres . . But
Kipling is not Mary Ann Evans.* Com-
mercially and literarily, she had the virtue of
subordinating the Parts to the Whole. Literarily
and commercially, he has the vice adverse.
Yet Kim does open tamely. Later you are
charmed and absorbed. And the book closes with
the old effect of brilliancy, dexterity, et prceterea
not very much. Why cannot Kipling master the
architectonics of the novel ? Why are his short-
stories so much more satisfactory, in the way of
Art ? Because (for a working hypothesis) he was,
is, and will be a precocious Indian child, whose
art stopped short in the Gazette office at Lahore,
and who cannot add a man’s stature to his boyish
cubit. He can see; but he cannot combine things
seen, cannot induce and deduce. (Dorrington is
his local analogue). His clear vision—it is the
child’s; his bright, clean touch—it is the clever
school-boy’s; his fresh, unjaded wonder, his per-
petual interest in the spectacle of life f —these are
the prerogative of the narrow, undeveloped brain,
with energy massed in a torrent, not spreading
in a pool. For the hypothesis’ t buttresses, con-
sider the child and the.savage, (that Other.child)
seen throughput Kipling’s work. Captains
Courageous—a boys’ story-book simply. The
Jungle Books— children’s familiar stories of
animals that talk. Wee Willie Winkie—
stories of children so well remembered, there-
fore so well told. Stalky and Co. —another boys’
story-book. In the short-stories note the domin-
ance of the ghost and the soldier- —superstition and
war —the things that terrify and enchant the child
and the savage. Love enters rarely, and then less
as a thing felt than as a tiling seen. The Light
that Failed missed its mark because Kipling
lacks synthetic power; lie could, see the great
motive, but not in its proportions and relations.
All Kipling’s prose has that panoramic effect—the
effect of a succession of isolated impressions,
detached objects ; and it is so that a child-savage
sees life : he cannot fuse and deduce. And
in Kipling’s verse and other prose; the fond-
ness for machinery—what is it but Budge’s
yearning “to see the wheels go wound”? The
disproportionate stress on the Flag of England,
the contempt for other flags : these are the tribal
traditions of the savage. So on. Survey Kipling’s
work with this idea in mind, and you find con-
firmation everywhere.
Now comes Kim —a story for boys, a panorama
of India. We can like it because *we are all
children of a larger growth, as Kipling is a child
of arrested growth. The boy-“hero,” Kim, is a
bey all through : there are no Passions in the
book; and motley India passes before us in a
succession of pictures, just as Kim passes before
us in a succession of episodes. There is in the
book no inevitable continuity, no vital rotundity :
any chapter could be lopped off and replaced by a
few tag-words without bringing sense of loss.
That is the way in which the child-Kipling sees
Life—as a series of disconnected impressions. And
the essence of Art is to unite impressions in the
Whole of Life—to say why, to show whither.
(Finishes the explication of Kipling with con-
gratulations on having done without -bj-ct-v- and
s-bj-ct-v-: continues the exposition of Earn.)
Have you read On Our Selection ? Well,
Kim is an Indian On Our Selection —with the
difference that India is not Australia, that India
is a larger selection, and that Kipling writes
better than Arthur Davis writes, —so that his
work seems good outside its own country.
Kim is the most difficult thing Kipling has
attempted; and his success is an intellec-
tual feat. Of course, India is beyond any
canvas ; but to put even a glimpse of a little bit of
India on canvas—merely to suggest that mar-
vellous world’s-midden of races and religions—is
one of the most arduous tasks to which a writer
can address himself. And Kipling has compassed
the task. Possibly no man knows India all, the
races and the tongues and the subtleties of minds
inheriting the lore of a thousand generations ; and
only an Indian can judge the measure of
Kipling’s success. At this distance it seems won-
derfully great. My ! how the man has ransacked
his memories ! how he has manipulated his know-
ledge ! how he has sweated for his local colour !
Looking closely, you see Kipling is a locomotive’s
headlight, flashing brilliantly over a small area
as it speeds, and making more intense the gloom
beyond ;—but what is a man to do ? A book is
only a book ; and India is—lndia. Within its
area, nothing too small for the light to illuminate.
Hindu proverbs ; cant phrases polished almost to
epigrams (did S. M. Herald print Mahbub Ali’s
boast: “When I was fifteen, I had shot my
man and begot my man!”); scraps of scenery;
variants of caste and of character;—every-
thing Kipling knows is kneaded in. Even far
-Australia pays him tribute. Surely the Wes-
tralian willy-willy suggested “the wandering
wulhe-wa got up to dance.” (335). ’Tis Cup-
favourite Revenue’s odds that Hurree Chunder’s
“ adventitious aids ” (232) was prigged from P. J.
• Holdsworth’s Bulletin story of Parkes and
Curnow’s oysters. (,“ Hi don’t need those had-
ventitious haids, Mister Cumow !”) And when
Kim commenced in S. M. Herald somebody
having authority commented on this page regard-
ing the, misunderstood Dogra “of lower caste than
a Sikh.” Kipling bows to authority ; and we read
no'v that “a Dogra is of other caste than a Sikh.”
(41).
lor the rest: Kipling’s notion of thq Indian
Secret Service is attractively displayed (he has a
child’s love of the cabalistic Secret). The Secret
Service brings dramatic interest; there is splen-
dent character-interest throughout—men and
women. The Baboo is a marvel, though his con-
trasts are forced ; he wants side-tones and sub-
aspects, but Kipling does not deal in these; a
child does not see them.
At end : after all: a passenger on the road of
Literature, say the pundits; not a landmark.
You remember as you remember scenes passed
in a railway carriage—trees, houses, people
seen for a moment. But Destiny does not
travel by express, and nothing great in
Literature was ever accomplished without re-
*®r®nce tbe Great Motive of Destiny.
Children know nothing of Destiny, and Kipling
never gets out of the railway-carriage. He began
by being clever—inordinately clever, and interest-
ing—exceptionally interesting. He will end by
being interesting—inordinately interesting, and
clever exceptionally clever. Here’s a place for
gratitude, and Kipling in his place.
The Fatal Opulence of Bishops is the title of a
book by a London vicar, just into a second
edition.
“‘The Hunting of the’ Boer” appears in
Lond. Onlooker. Some verses—
They mobilised columns the foe to disband ;
They pursued him with haste and with kits ;
They threatened his life with a Bord semi-grand,
And crushed him with musical “ hits.”
They sought him with Tommies, they sought him with
chefs.
They pursued him with shells and entries ;
They menaced his freedom with musical clefs,
And marvelled at British delays.
They sought him with comforts, they sought him with
crates,
A wild and pursuing C.P. ;
Yet the foe still evades the most tardy of fates,
For a Boer has no luggage, you see !
Some of H. B. Marriott Watson’s newly-re-
printed sketches, The Skirts of Happy Chance,
were pleasant when read singly ; but the‘collection
tastes thin and monotonous.
Pinero’s new play, Iris, at Bond. Garrick
Theatre, is described as his “longest and most
ambitious.” It is -a study of a woman—weak,
romantic, sensual—to the tune of Villon’s refrain,
Always the man with money scores.
The psychology seems, at this distance, too good
to have been palatable to critics with the Chrisfc-
mas-picture mind ; and the triumph of the Spanish
Jew does n’t suit British prejudices on the stage,
though accepted in fact. The Jew is a full-
blooded, scornful person in whose mouth these
words are put—
After all, England is a paradise only for the puritan and
the hypocrite. ( His spirits visin']. ) Ha, ha ! Farewell,
England! Land of lean women and smug men, of the
drooping eyelid and the sanctimonious drawl ! Land of
money-worship, of cant and Pharisaism, of false senti-
ment and namby-pamby ideals—in every department of
life, the suburb of the universe ! Ha, ha, ha ! England,
farewell!
This is not essentially novel or notable—Cunujng-
ham Graham and many others have expressed the
sentiment; George Moore’s “Brixton Empire”
suggested the phrase. But launched on the
English stage, and by Pinero, it is almost
momentous.
Puck: Has it been printed that the favourite
push has Shakespearian authority in Macbeth, v, 3 ?
This push
Will cheer me ever or disseat me now.
The “push” here are the English invaders in
Birnam wood. In Julius Ccesar, i, 2, you find the
similar use of press.
Who is it in the press that calls on me?
The following “dictation paper ” was set at last
exam, of candidate-cadets for Sydney Water and
Sewerage Board. And nobody passed !
The academician versed in chronological data and geolo-
gical antiquities, expert in synthesis and the construction
of exegesis, to whom the differential calculus was arithmeti-
cal simplicity personified, who could startle philosophy with
subtle theses on occult and,abstruse sciences or involve a
tortured labyrinth in philological polemics illustrated by
simile and metaphor and phrased with vocabulistic plenti-
tude and grandiloquent exuberance ; in short,, a veritable
peripatetic lexicon, one whose mathematical disquisitions
on hydrostatic forces and the laws of pneumatics were
quoted throughout Christendom as irrefragable and inde-
feasible. Such a man, I say, with this stupendous learning
may be unable to earn his living in the industrial centres
like Sydney, where common senseis the main factor guid-
ing the hazardous ventures of commercial pursuits and
manufactures to efficient financial issues.
“To whom the differential calculus was (!) arith-
metical simplicity personified (\) ” “Stupendous
learning” and “earn his living” (!)—“common
sense ” (!) and unconscious humour! Who needs to
be able to spell such words as “vocabulistic”
without a dictionary ? Who needs to spell them
at all, when the use of them, unless with a specific
aim, is a proof of incapacity to write good English ?
The height of the W. and S. Board’s satire on its
own ignorance is reached when you remember that
A-less Jacob Garrard is the Board’s chairman !
A. G. Alanson, a sometime Bulletin contribu-
tor, writes the chief serial story in the new
Chatterbox— “ The Diggers of Black Rock Hill.”
It is well written ; but the English illustrator
should be given to soldier-ants.
In The Wouldbegoods (T. Fisher Unwin,
London) Edith Nesbit imitates The Golden Age
and omits Graham’s humour. Some of the illus-
trations by Arthur Buckland are charming.
“Michael O’Brien and his wife Bridget were
old and infirm,” says the first sentence of Virtue and,
Repentance, by the Rev. W. Hampson, a 94 pp. booklet
published by Will. B. Tyndale, Southern Cross, W.A.
The last sentence is : “But Pat O’Brien, before his un-
timely death, had always journeyed in the right direction
to the gaol,* swerving neither to the right nor left, and
heeding not the attractions that sought to divert him
from it.” The rest is between the Rev. W. Hampson and
his Conscience.
Swain and Co., booksellers and Stationers, of
Moore-street, Sydney,. issue a Souvenir of New South
Wales (50 photographic views), which is an excellent speci-
men of the class. But the class grows wearisome. Instead
of reproducing Post Office and Markets ad nauseam, why
not reproduce pictures of bush life and scenery—typical
Australian pictures made by brush, pen, or camera—and
try the public with Art and Nature instead of stucco ?
Boko: In that creditable magazine, The Common-
wealth, N. Lindsay illustrates Ogilvie’s verses, “ Bells
along Macquarie,” and in the small sketch at bottom of
page depicts two men sitting smoking by their camp-fire
with the belled horses. Near them lies a saddle, flaps
down—as no bushman would think of placing it; it
should be placed on end, knee-pads down ; even if it were
to be used as a pillow it would be laid on its back, flaps
in the air. The belled horses standing at the rear are
what heralds term “regardant,” instead of eating as
hungry horses should ; but this position is plainly neces-
sary on account of the kind of bells they wear. These are
fastened by a long strap over the withers, and lie on the
chest, so that if the horse bent his head to feed, the
strap would fall over his head—or, supposing it would
stay firm, the bell would not ring. Horse-bells are
always buckled by a broad strap round the throat. Is n’t
it time we had a few artists with “ bush experience ’’ ?
“ The old feud about mixed classes before nude
models still rages in Paris art-schools ; but the disputants
have new names—the Fig-leaf party and the Don’t-care-a-
flg-leaf party.
“ Also works by Goya, Gericault, Delacroix
Ingres, Daumier, Millet, Puvis de Ohavannes, Rodin,
Alfred Stevens, Legros, Strang, Conder, Rothenstein, and
others."—An advfc. by Garfax and Go., print-sellers,
London. An Australian in good company.

Copyright.
SENATOR KEATING proposes to intro-
duce a Commonwealth Bill to consolidate
and improve the State laws of copyright.
We need it.
With the exception of Westralia, whose more
recent constitution has permitted advantage to be
taken of more modern ideas, the Australian
States are governed by copyright legislation Which
is antiquated and inefficient. In the case of
Westralia the legislation is somewhat less anti-
quated, somewhat less inefficient. Conies and
authors are feeble folk, and State politicians are
usually too busy in self-aggrandisement to consider
legislation not influentially demanded. It is a
chief blessing of national union that we may hope
to see national questions better handled by more
competent legislators, paying less regard to per-
sonal profit.
Copyright is a national question. Literature,
painting, sculpture, music, the drama, and other
arts which help to make, preserve, and civilise a
nation have the very greatest value to us. We
wish to encourage these all. We wish to protect
these all. One way of encouragement and protec-
tion is to give the artist legal property in the
result of his labours. A man who writes a book
has as much right to the profit of the work of his
brain as a man who builds a house has to the
profit of the work of his hands. Property in a
work of art should Sndure as long as property in
a work of handicraft. That is, copyright should
be perpetual.
Australian copyright legislation should put Aus-
tralia first. International copyright is practically
of no value to us. We gain nothing by it, except
the right to lie in a backwash of the artistic
■ stream. What we want is the American system,
by which any foreigner who claims copyright in
books, for example, has to bring the book to
America first, and print' it there with local
labour, from local materials—at least as soon as it
is printed elsewhere. America thus gains in two
main ways. First, she protects her industries.
The foreigner finds himself compelled to pay
American type-founders, paper-makers, oloth-
makers, type-setters, machinists, and other allied
artisans. Then America comes first, not last, in
the matter of literature. Many of the' best
English books, for example, are published and
read in America before they are read in England.
Many English publishers have an American office.
And if an author does not choose to do thus,
America can reprint him.
This works both ways, of course; and that will
be perfectly satisfactory to us in Australia. If
we have a good book, we are quite content to take
it to England and America and print it there.
Remember that the Naissance of Australian
literature is taking place. But how heavily are
authors and publishers handicapped ! It costs
usually 40 per cent, more to print and bind an
•edition of a book in Australia than it costs in
England. Why ? Because we have to pay more
for labour, and we have to import our material and
pay duty. Let the Commonwealth protect the
author as well as the artisans who live by him.
Let the Commonwealth protect the artisans who
live by him in protecting the author. Save us
from cheap-labour prints and reprints, and give
us a chance to employ well-paid labour here. We
can found our own type, make our own paper.
Give us a chance to compete with the foreigner
on equal terms.
The same with the drama. If a foreigner wants
stage rights, let him bring his drama here and
stage it here at least as soon as elsewhere. Why
should we be compelled to live by cast-offs of the
European stage ? Australia first, —now and
always first—as far as laws can compass it. For
the rest: trust the people.

WOMEN AND
WARNER’S
SAFE CURE.
The Health of women is of supreme im-
portance. The daughter of to-day becomes
the wife of to-morrow, and it is abso-
lutely essential to the welfare of the race
that good health should be hers; good
health which brings happiness and en-
sures vigour in posterity. A woman’s
nervous system is very highly strung.
Her mechanism is far more delicate and
complex than that of a man, and there
is little to wonder at when it breaks down
under the continuous high pressure of
modern life.
Most women require some aid to enable
them to support the daily stress and
strain, and this aid is found in WARNER’S
SAFE CURE —a medicine wonderfully
adapted to their delicate organisation—
which has for twenty years secured the
grateful endorsement of women in all
walks of life in all parts of the civilised
world.
Read the following Letters:
“ Charlton,” George Street,
Norwood, S.A.
October 1, 1900.
Dear Sirs, —
About seven years ago I contracted a severe
internal complaint, and was in a very critical
state. The doctor attending me told me that if I
lived I should be a sufferer all my lifetime. His
treatment was quite useless in giving me relief.
After two or three years I got tired of paying
doctors’ fees for no benefit, and hearingof Warner’s
Safe Cure I gave it a trial. I took, in all, five
bottles, and am thankful to state I am perfectly
cured. The medical man was genuinely surprised
at such a wonderful result. lam now as well as
it is possible to be, and give Warner’s Safe Cure
the whole credit for accomplishing what doctors
deemed impossible. Hoping that ladies, suffer-
ing as I did, will profit, I am writing to you now.
My mother considered that her life was saved by
the use of Warner’s Safe Cure in a somewhat
similar complaint.
(Mrs ) ANNIE HARRIS.

A Brisbane Appetite.
Nobody can tell the following story better than
the writer tells it himself.
“In 1891,” he says, “I first experienced the
miseries of acute indigestion. I was obliged to
knock off work, and suffered agonies for two
months. Then I felt slightly better, and went
back to my post at the North British Fire Brigade
Station, and stayed there until the great flood in
1893.
“ Then I had a terribly severe relapse which
laid me down for two months, seven weeks of it
being spent in the hospital.
“ Several doctors attended me, but they failed
to effect a cure.
“ Yet at the end of ten months I felt somewhat
better, and being of an energetic disposition I
started in to work again.
“I managed to keep at it for six months and
then collapsed completely, and resigned my posi-
tion in the Brigade.
“On returning home several of my friends
urged me to put my faith in Seigel’s Syrup, and
in that only, saying they had known it to cure
cases as bad as mine. ‘ Don’t be down-hearted,’
they said.
“On this I began using Seigel’s Syrup (and
Seigel’s Pills occasionally) and gradually got
better. When I had taken about twelve bottles
in all. I was so much better, I accepted a position
as mate in a ship going to trade in the Islands—
taking a supply of the Syrup with me.
“ I must mention that I kept on using Seigel’s
Syrup until we got to Sydney, and then I was
quite restored to health, and have been perfectly
well ever since.
“My health is now splendid ; my appetite is
excellent, and all the bad symptoms I once had
are gone like an ugly dream after morning breaks.
“ My wife uses the medicine for a milder form
of indigestion than mine was, and is as firm a
believer in it as I am. ,
“ I will name a few of the peculiarities of the
aggravating and dangerous disease as it affected
me. There was always a bad taste in my mouth,
my breath vvas bad, and I used to retch frequently
after eating ; I belched continually, lost flesh, and
had thin, lantern jaws.
“ Scores of friends and acquaintances can tes-
tify to the truth of what I have said. It i 3 certain
that Mother Seigel’s Syrup cured me when all
other medicines and forms of treatment proved to
be no good, and I have to thank the Syrup only
for the fact that I am now a strong, healthy man.
I have now been nearly three years in the em-
ployment of the Queensport Meat Company, but
m/home is at the address given below.” A. C.
B.ickmann, Myrtle Villa, George-street, Kan-
garoo Point, Brisbane, Q. Sept, 6th, 1900.
Various Verses.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ballad of Penitence.
We’ve had our fling, and the dance is done,
And the gold of our pleasures fades fast to grey;
We’ve scoffed at Prudence and lived for Fun,
And now there’s the devil an’ all to pay !
We’ve footed a measure right blithe and gay,
And laughed at Care with a rollicking crew . . .
The crew’s a bit shaky and sick to-day;
But we still may laugh for a year or two 1
We’ve spoiled the Egyptian and drenched his dun;
We’ve slept with the swine in the good old way;
We’ve played high stakes—and we have not won,
And now there’s the devil an’ all to pay !
We’re reaping the whirlwind, the good folks say
(The thing sounds big, but it’s mostly true):
It’s a dullish game, and we’ve come to stay ;
But we still may laugh for a year or two !
Our friends drop away from us, one by one :
Our loves have been legion ; but where are
they ?
Those vows?—you might measure them by the
tun . . .
And now there’s the devil an’ all to pay !
Poor asses; we dreamt ’twould be always May,
With scent of blossom and skies of blue :
As it is . . . we must shortly lie low and pray;
But—we still may laugh for a year or two !
Lad 1 Hope has crumbled in slow decay,
And now there's the devil an’ all to pay!
Bid there's warm blood yet in me and you,
And we still may laugh for a year or two !
TAS.
FRANK MORTON.
Out of Season.
A serenade ! Nay, Sweetheart, that’s not
possible ;
It’s out of season, like an old-time bonnet ;
And out of season, too, the mode to tell
Your charm, my rapture, in a Petrarch sonnet.
It might be, ere my song was midway done, and I
Stood trembling with the love-thrill of my num-
bers,
Your next-door neighbors would awake and cry
A malison on him who broke their slumbers.
Nay, love was meant for two—’tis yours and mine
alone;
A third might laugh at it, might mar its glory :
Those Veronese had whispered had they known
A poet listened to their witching story.
The old, old way to tell the old, old story still
Is surely best ; and love that looks for aiding—
E’en though for music’s aid—is love that will
Die all too soon in spite of serenading.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Red West Road.
Off-shore I hear the great propellers thunder,
And throb and thrash so steadily and slow;
Their booming cadence tells of seas that plunder—
Of Love’s moon-seas and- brave hearts thrown
asunder.
Of hot, red lips and battles, blow for blow ;
And as they sing my heart is filled with wonder,
Though why—l scarcely know.'
Perhaps it is because they tell a story,
And lift a deep storm-measure as they come—
A song of old-time love and battles gory,
When men dared Hell and sailed through sunset’s
glory
With pealing trumpet tuned to rolling drum,
To hunt and loot and sink the jewelled quarry
In seas too deep to plumb.
I only know I watch the steamers going
Along the Red West Road, with heavy heart,
And when the night comes, look for head-lights
showing,
And mark their speed—the ebb tide or the
flowing,
For loth am I to see them slew and start
Adown that path ; and every deep call blowing
Cuts keen and leaves a smart.
The blazing West to me is always calling,
For in the West there burns my brightest star ..
Oh, God ! to hear the anchor-winches hauling,
And feel her speeding, soaring high and falling
With steady swing across the brawling bar —
To hear the stem-struck rollers tumble sprawling,
And watch the lights afar !
To South and East and North the screws are
singing,
So steadily and tunefully and slow,
But on the Western Track they thunder, flinging
Their wake a-foam, and by their roar and ringing—
By laughter sweet, deep in my heart, I know,
That down that Red West Road with big blades
swinging
Some day I’ll go.
M. L.
QUILP N.
An old man over 90 years of age, and over
60 in Australia, cannot get an old-age pension for
his few remaining years because he has not lived
continuously for 25 years in N.S.W. Residing
near the Queensland border, he has spent some of
his time on one side of the imaginary line
separating the two States, and some on the other.
Wanted badly—the Australian Old Age Pension
law.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 30, 1901.
New Guinea—and After?
Last week the Commonwealth Parliament
affirmed its readiness to join in measures
for acceptance of British New Guinea as a
territory of the Commonwealth, and its
readiness to vote £20,000 a year for five
years towards expenses of government.
Considering the importance of this step, the
matter received from Parliament very little
consideration. The question involved is by
far the most difficult which the Common-
wealth has yet discussed. In regard to
Alien Exclusion and the Tariff the principle
of action was clear. It was clear that, if
Australia is to he preserved for a white race,
the Colored Alien must go. It was clear
that revenue duties should embody as
far as possible the idea of Protec-
tion for Home Industry. These ques-
tions were keenly debated. But the
question which national alternative to
choose—to devote Australian energy wholly
to the development of Australia, or to com-
mit the Commonwealth to a policy of exter-
nal expansion, the end of which none can
foresee—obtained hardly more consideration
than if t'he choice had been a mere matter
of form. Fortunately, the discussion and
the settlement are not final ; and, when
the Enabling Bill is framed, it may be
hoped that Parliament will recognise its
grave responsibility.
* * * *
Superficially considered, the idea of
“annexing New Guinea” is attractive.
There is an Australian sentiment which
chimes with the Monroe doctrine of America,
and wishes to place our outlying islands
beyond the reach of foreign aggression. But
it is open to doubt—many Americans doubt
it_whether the acquisition of Cuba
has profited the United States. It
is almost certain that the U.S. acquisi-
tion of the Philippines has been, and will
continue to be, unprofitable. And the U.S. A.
have 80 millions of population ; we have
four millions. The U.S.A. have spread
settlement over nearly the whole of their
continental territory ; we have settled only
the fringe of ours. If it be admitted that
the U.S. A. are only now ripe to pursue an
annexation policy, then we will not be ripe
for a hundred years at least. Why should
Australia be earth-hungry, when the earth
we have is so little developed ? Instead of
sp-'nding Australian taxes to govern and
develop tropical New Guinea, is it not
better to spend them in developing tropical
Australia ? Why fritter energy, when our
energy is so scant and our territory already
so vast 1
9 9 • •
“Annexing New Guinea.” But it is not
annexing New Guinea ; it is only annexing
the S.E. division. The Dutch own the
eastern half; the Germans the N.E. divi-
sion. These possible enemies are now dis-
tant ; it is proposed that we should go and
settle right alongside them. Upon those
who advocate annexation the onus lies to
show how it is going to pay Australia to
transform the S.E. division from a British
dependency into an Australian dependency.
There is one way in which it might pay us,
if other things were equal, and that is by
allowing Australian advocates of colored
labor to go to New Guinea and use their
colored labor on the spot. Instead of bring-
ing colored labor to the sugar industry,
we could send the sugar industry to
colored labor—or such part of the industry
as wished to go. Considered as a refuge for
the Philps and Cowleys, the Sargoops and
McEacharns, and all that ilk, New Guinea
looks indeed a rosy appendage to Australian
policy. But the rose-glow fades at sound of
the one word Tariff. If colored-labor in-
dustries are established in New Guinea, it
will be difficult for tbem to prosper unless
they can find a market in Australia. But if
the product of New Guinea colored labor is
admitted duty-free, to compete on equal
terms with the product of white labor, then
we might almost as well bring back colored
labor to the Commonwealth. And you can’t
fly in the face of facts, and say that New
Guinea, with its vast colored population, is
to become a white-labor province. So if
the Commonwealth annexes British New
Guinea, the tariff wall must shut the colored-
labor product out; that is certain.
* • * *
The whole argument that Australia must
annex outlying islands for reasons of defence
seems very weak when it is critically con-
sidered. How can occupation of New
Guinea protect Australia 1 The Germans are
already established round the corner ; the
Dutch are on the other side ; the French
are on the East. A few. ocean-miles more or
less make no difference nowadays : what is
essential is the base, the coaling-station ; and
these the foreigners already have, and amply.
The real truth is that, instead of New Guinea
being a source of strength in war, it will be
a source of weakness. It is a detached out-
post; and we shall be called upon to defend
the outpost as well as the fort. Besides, if an
enemy really wishes to attack from the North,
he can land on the Australian mainland
anywhere—if that were worth while. Of
course, it is not worth while, unless he comes
to stay and settle ; for all the vital purposes
of war, for the purposes of doing harm and
getting profit, an enemy must strike at the
rich South, and gain the railways. He would
simply pass by New Guinea ; what does New
Guinea matter 1 But if Australia annexes
British New Guinea, she undertakes the
obligation of defending it—and in the event
of any serious attack during the next 100
years we will be hard put to it to defend
Australia.
* * * •
These considerations may not be conclu-
sive ; but they are to the point. Upon those
who would alter the existing state of things
is thrown the duty of showing that the
alteration will pay. Now, how will it pay
Australia to annex the British fragment of
New Guinea ?
Life Assurance Set to Music.
One of this country’s most recent visitors has been
a certain Okonhyatekha, who came all the way
from Canada to lecture and otherwise demonstrate
on behalf of that blatant and brassy life assurance
institution, the Independent Order of Foresters.
Okonhyatekha was heralded by long newspaper
puffs telling how, in addition to being head and
chief boss, and principal responsible party of the
institution above mentioned, he was a chief of the
Iroquois Indians, one of the most slippery and
unreliable races, by the way, ever forcibly disguised
by any white-man Government with a thin varnish
of civilisation. It is a curious circumstance and
illustrative of the strange ways of human nature
that the management should deliberately set
forth this matter as an advertisement cal-
culated to help the 1.0. F. along and
yet it probably does help it along. The
distant native looks green as well as the distant
hill, and while it would only cover a local life
assurance office with ridicule if it advertised that
it was managed by a local aboriginal chief belong-
ing to a tribe of the' very worst antecedents, the
same announcement, coming from the other side
of the world, tends rather to arouse a feeling of
friendly interest. Okonhyatekha, like his asso-
ciates, lectured and otherwise held forth about the
glories of his office as a place where a man could
insure his life with advantage. He expounded in
a hall—the hire of which, presumably, has to come
out of the funds. Music and songs were
supplied by a number of artists, for the 1.0. F.
is a brass band variety of life assurance office,
and it would be interesting to know out of
what funds these artists were paid. Certain
well-known local gentlemen were present to
set forth, either verbally, or by the silent assent
conveyed by their presence, that they were so
deeply affected at the beauty, solvency, and un-
rivalled advantages of the 1.0. F., and its
brotherliness, and its Christian principle, and its
Oronhyatekha, that they were absolutely com-
pelled to come and supply their disinterested
testimony, sse they would have burst. But
the public possibly grows a trifle weary of seeing
the same gang turn up in disinterested enthusiasm
every time, and might be pleased if the 1.0. F.
would get a fresh lot.
* * * *
The 1.0. F. is the only assurance institution
within this writer’s knowledge which has
at its disposal funds with which it can hire
singers and lecturers and brass bands and
the other appurtenances of the cheap-
jack variety of life assurance. The A.M.P. or
National Mutual or Equitable, or any other office,
charges a premium only, and accounts for all
premiums received in its balance-sheet. There-
fore, as it can hardly put down music and song
and refreshments anjtong its expenses, it doesn’t
have these things. The 7/6 per quarter which the
A.M.P. charges to a man of 20 for £lOO of
insurance all goes to pay for insurance, barring
the fraction reserved for necessary expenses, and
the nature of these expenses is fully explained.
But the 1.0. F. charges the same citizen 8/lid. for
the same policy, in addition to other charges
arising out of his membership of the blessed Order
—but it only accounts in the published balance-
sheet of its life assurance department for about
ss. of this. And, presumably, the hall-hire, the
musicians, the song-and-dance aspect of things, and
all the other ridiculous ham frill, come out of the
other 3s. l?,d. or more. The members of the insti-
tution pay all expenses, anyhow—there is no one
else who can pay them unless the Indian chief
at the head of the concern is philanthropic enough
to provide the coin out of the revenue of his
domains.
» » * *
The case against the 1.0. F., and its circus or
patent-medicine method of doing business, and its
many tomfooleries is just this: To the small
insurer it charges more for a £lOO policy than a
good Australian office does—though it professes to
charge less, because it calls only part of the charge
“premium,” and the rest “ court dues.” It gives
vastly worse security than any large Australian
office, because, though the person aged 20 pays
the 1.0. F. Bs. per quarter for his £lOO policy,
only ss. of this (less necessary expenses) is set
aside to meet the policy at maturity, while the
7s. 6d. he would pay to the A.M.P. is all set aside
(less necessary expenses) to meet the policy at
maturity. In addition to charging the small
insurer more, and giving him incomparably worse
security, it gives no surrender values, and it
reserves the right to increase the premium. When
it issues a policy for so much, with a £2
premium payable thereon, it doesn’t thereby
pledge itself to pay the policy when it falls
due provided the £2 premium has been
met regularly. It only pledges itself to
pay the policy provided the £2 premium is met
regularly, as well as any further premium which
may be demanded, however large that further
demand may be. And if, when the policy-holder
is growing old and has paid his premiums regu-
larly for many years, he is unable to pay any fur-
ther sum which may be demanded, he simply loses
his policy, the provision he thought he had made
for his wife and family, the premiums he has paid
—everything. In Canada, where the 1.0. F. has
its head-quarters, it is not allowed to pose as an
ordinary life office. There, where it is better
known than it is in Australia, it has to print a
clear intimation on its documents that it is an
“assessment ” office—one of those which reserves
the right to increase premiums and to throw out,
without a penny of compensation, the member
who can’t pay the increase. In Australia this
ghastly fact is kept very much in the background.
• * * *
The 1.0. F. started in 1874. By 1881 it is
frankly admitted that it was in a perishing condi-
tion. Then it looked up a little, and now the
people who lecture about it, with music and
orchestral selections in between, allege that,
having lasted 20 years since its re-organisation, its
stability must be beyond all question. This is the
silly kind of argument which appeals to the
ignorant —the class that the 1.0. F. and its satel-
lites apparently eater for. The most hopelessly
insolvent life assurance office in England is 35
years old. The Bank of Van Diemen’s Land was
68 years old when it pegged out, and Australia is
full of similar records. Neither the age'of a life
assurance office nor the amount of its funds is the
least guarantee of solvency ; if its funds are large
and its liabilities under its policies are larger, then
it is insolvent despite its apparent wealth. And,
as for its age, the most insolvent, equally with
the most solvent, institution, can keep going
as long as plenty of premiums come in, and the
getting of them depends mostly on the vigor and
the brazen assurance of its conductors. But the
1.0.F.’s solvency or otherwise is not the only
feature in the case. Even if it is solvent it
offers to the small policy-holder (who is
also the most numerous policy - holder) a
policy that is ridiculously expensive com-
pared to what he can get from strong
Australian institutions. Its alleged cheapness,
when the charges that areadded on to its premiums
are considered, is one of the silliest lies ever
passed on to an ignorant community. Its
“surplus,” so-called, is only a surplus if the
whole of its liabilities under its policies are
ignored; on the same basis the A. M.P. has
a “surplus” of over £17,000,000. Also, its pre-
tensions to some special charm, or security, or
sanctity by reason of its “brotherhood ” are merely
the patter of the racecourse mountebank. Every
mutual life assurance institution is a brotherhood,
even if the manager is not described as Brother
Teece or Brother Remington. Indeed, the latter
and their kind in Australia are much better
brethren than the huge Red Indian gentleman
from Canada. If a member fails, after 10 or 20
years of regular payment, to meet his premium
when it falls due, the Australian manager, who
doesn’t call himself a brother, keeps his £lOO
policy in force for two or three or more years out
of the surrender-value; while Brother Oronhya-
tekha, of the fraternal 1.0. F., who has taken
more money from him in Lis better days, just
throws him out right there and forfeits his policy,
there being no surrender value. The brother who
throws one out so promptly at the first sign of
poverty isn’t this paper’s idea of brotherhood,
anyhow.
m • *' *
The 1.0. F., with its silly literature and its
ridiculous calculations and its wildly-original
“surplus,” is a splendid example of the policy of
bounce. As a casual specimen of its general atti-
tude, one of its regular followers broke forth thus
at the Oronhyatekha conversazione
The doctor (Oronhyatekha) came in contact with the
Order 20 years ago. He recognised in it the germ of a
great idea, the idea of giving people the means of pro-
viding for the women and children when the breadwinner
was gone.
That be it observed, was about the year 1880. At
that date, when the A.M.P. Society was already
31 years old, and eleven other Australian life
offices were in full working order, and when life
assurance had been going strong for a century or so,
Okonhyatekha recognised, in the 1.U.i,, tne
germ of the great idea of insuring peop e s
lives! This painful concern actually pipses as if
it had invented life assurance. All allusion to its
high charges, its dubious security, its misleading
calculations and the like, is loftily set aside as the
result of envy and grudging unchan tableness.
And, after all, it is only a blatant offshoot or varia-
tion of the very old, discredited, cheap and uast\
assessment system, whose wrecks have filled
America for vears past. It isn t anything
new, and it hasn’t discovered anything new.
It appears to be largely the work of men whose
knowledge of life assurance is very small, aided by
men whose personal assurance is amazingly large
It is vaguely suggestive of the circus, and the pill
which will cure everything, and the cheap-sale
draper. And when the Federal Parliament starts
to supply Australia with a decent life assurance
law it is to be hoped that its first act will be to
give the 1.0. F. and its Oronhyatkkha per-
emptory notice to quit, and that it will send some
responsible, able-bodied person to see them off
and make dead sure that they are gone.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Bitter Brown Baron.
The troubles of the Federal Parliament on the
subject of Alien Immigration are not diminishing.
The latest news is that Baron Something or-
Other, Jap. ambassador in London, objects, not
only to direct exclusion as an insult to his inflated
brown nation, but also to any educational test,
unless it is so devised that it will let the Jap. in
freely. He explains, in fact, that the Jap.
Government “ cannot acquiesce” in any exclusion
policy unless it puts the Jap. on the same level as
the European. It is a cold day for the British
empire when any small brown nation stands on
twopence to reach up to its knocker, and bangs at
the portal insisting on admittance with veiled
threats of what may happen if it is refused ; but
strange things happen nowadays. Still it seems
unlikely that anything bad will result this time
unless the Federal Ministry takes the advice of
certain badly-disguised enemies and makes a
hideous mistake. Under the circumstances its
course is obviously to go right on with its Educa-
tional Test for the Jap’s exclusion, and knock at
Bull’s door even more loudly demanding assent.
Under the Salisbury regime he who bangs most
vociferously has the best chance to get in. I lie
Sydney Telegraph— always ready, and more than
ready, to sacrifice the interests of Australia to
anything that offers a prospect of getting rid of
the Barton Ministry and replacing it with a
Calico Jimmy Government of foreign importers—
makes out of the Baron’s upheaval an opportunity
to demand that the Educational Test shall be
abandoned and direct exclusion substituted.
That would discredit the Barton Government
in the first place, for it would be a definite backing
down before its opponents. Then when the direct
exclusion Bill was vetoed by the British Govern-
ment (as it certainly would be, seeing that not
only is Japan hostile to it, but the British
Ministry has declared itself hostile also), that
would discredit the Barton Ministry a second
time. The British Government has declared its
willingness to accept the Educational Test for the
exclusion of aliens, and it can’t reject it without
doing a servile and conspicuous crawl-down at the
orders of Japan. It now reiterates its willingness
to accept the Educational Test, which is a good
sign. Therefore, it is a matter of choosing
between a measure which can be made effective,
and which —barring some more convincing protest
from the infuriated Baron—is sure of acceptance,
and one which, we have already been informed,
is practically certain to be rejected. The
Telegraph virtually proposes that the Barton
Ministry should abandon its Bill because the
brown ambassador at the other end of the world
objects to it. If Australia is ever going to be a
solid and durable white nation, it will have to
tread on the feelings of a good many brown
ambassadors in the process, and the present one
seems brown enough to start with.
The Fading' Peacock.
Victoria’s sorrowful Peacock Government is in a
tight place over its proposed popularly-elected
Convention for the drafting of a new State Con-
stitution. It was returned to power at last election
on its promise that it would nail its half-mast flag
on the outer battlements, and stand by this issue
to the last. Many of its supporters were luke-
warm in the matter, but the country was solid
enough, and even the gentlemen who feared lest
they might lose their seats when Parliament was
reduced and reconstructed had to affect enthu-
siasm. Peacock himself had to affect enthusiasm,
whether he had it or not. He and his party had
the country behind them when they probably
didn’t want it, and the country being behind them
they had to make a pretence of being in front of
the country. The Bill passed its second reading by
a small majority, which would probably have been a
much larger majority if Peacock had put his
sloping shoulder to the wheel in anything like real
earnest. Then the matter was dropped. Peacock
says he dropped it because he saw it was useless
to push the matter further ; therefore he is in the
extraordinary position of dropping the scheme
which is backed (unwillingly) by a small majority
in the Assembly and (willingly) by a large majority
in the country, to take up something else which is
backed, apparently, by a minority in the Assembly
and about which the country hasn’t been con-
sulted at all. There never was a better chance for
a strong man, with real fighting capacity, to dis-
tinguish himself. If Peacock had declared that
he and the Ministry and the Convention Bill
would all perish together if need be, and that if
it was lost he would go to the country again
on the matter, and raise Cain and other
vegetables, he could have carried the measure
to a certainty. Then he could have fought the
Upper House on it if there was any fight in him,
and could have fallen like a man if it was neces-
sary to fall. Peacock’s political fate is obviously
to fade away rather than fall. He is departing by
ineffective instalments, like the color out of an
old grey coat that has been too long in the sun.
A weak tendency to hang up things that are diffi-
cult, to drop things that are large and potter with
things that are small, to try to please everybody,
to apologise to everybody, to promise anything,
and to hope that something will turn up, is what
seems to be the matter with Peacock.

Some Old Age Pensioners.
“Nina Jeane” to The Bulletin on the blessings
of the old age pension :
I found the old age pensioner in a Woolloomooloo slum.
She said she was 68, and she looked it. Her face was a
dark unpolished cedar-color. Her hair was iron-grey,
and the face, seared and furrowed by the corrosive years,
was covered with dark patches that in her youth had
been freckles and beauty-spots. To see what she was like
in her youth ’twas only necessary to glance at the face of
her grand-daughter, a bright little child of nine, very


plainly and insufficiently' clad in a coarse white frock that
had once been pink flannellette. As for her home, it was
destitute of everything that we consider absolutely
necessary for our own grandmothers. She bustled round
making up my washing, and apologising that she had no
brown paper to wrap it in, and assuring me that
Molly would run round with it. As she bustled
I noticed her wrists. They were quite brown,
and gnarled and twisted like the roots of the tree
that grew awry. “ You suffer from rheumatism,” I
said. “ Yes, Miss, very bad in the arms and wrists.
The washing do make them very bad, too ; but there’s
rent to pay and food to buy, and clothes for Molly. She
must have boots or they won’t let her go to school, and
she’s gettin’ on very well with her lamin’ now. The
teacher’s quite proud of her. Oh, yes ! I get the pen-
sion. We’re quite well off now, since we got that.
I ask the Blessed Virgin to bless the gentleman who
brought that in every night. It pay’s the rent, and what
I make at the tub keeps us in food and something to
cover our nakedness. Afore the pension it was awful.
We never had enough to eat. It took all I could
earn to pay- the landlord, and we was always flirtin’
in debt, so that I’m afeared to go down the street for
that I might meet some of the landlords we’d slipped away
from. I’d like to pay ’em, but there’s no chance of ever
bein’ able to. Now, with the pension we just live —just
pay our way'. Without we starve, and rob the landlord,
too.” In the same slum I found another. He was a white-
haired old cripple, and he sat on the narrow pavement
just outside the door. My washerwoman introduced me.
He looked up, and I noticed that he had a pair of braces
jn his hand. Yes, he had the pension. It was a great
idea. He was comfortable now. With the pension he
could pay his rent, get three meals a day, and have a
little bit of tobacco on Saturday nights. He lived with
strangers, and made a few extra shillings per week by
working this “embroidery” on to braces. If he had a
wheel chair he’d go to the Domain and sit under the
trees and do the work there. He was slow at it, his
fingers were all thumbs, and he was frightened that the
paralysis which made his lower limbs useless to him
might eventually extend to his arms. That, he said,
would reduce him to the same abject misery in which he
existed and cursed his Maker before the O.A. Pension Act
passed. No, he’d never go to the Liverpool Home, he’d
known blokes that went there, and from what they told
him, he had made up his mind he’d commit suicide before
he would consent to be placed there.”

Black Labor in the Pacific.
Recent developments in Fiji throw some in-
teresting light on the colored-labor problems
which now agitate Australia. . In Fiji, until very
recently, the Colonial Sugar Co. employed cheap
Hindoo coolies as field laborers, but mechanics’
work—that of engineers, carpenters, blacksmiths,
&c. —was kept in the hands of whites. In July
last, however, the Co. imported a cargo of cheap
Parsee mechanics, and set them to work alongside
the whites at wages averaging ss. 3d. per day.
(The whites’ wages averaged 13s. per day.) The
whites threatened to resign in a body, and as the
co. found that the dusky mechanics were incompe-
tent, it informed the Europeans that they
were only imported as superior helps—this, appar-
ently, by way of letting the Asiatics have
time to learn their business. But as the old
bob-a-day coolie-helps were giving satisfaction, the
extravagant kindness of the Co. was suspicious.
Five white mechanics eventually resigned, and
the remainder are expecting the sack next season,
if the Parsees are a success. But, whether they
are or not, the fact remains that the monopolistic
Sugar Co. has flattened-out the argument about
the inability of the whites to work in hot climates,
and has shown nakedly that black labor is desired
only for its cheapness. The effect of coolie labor
on the moral condition of Fiji is also worth
noting. The coolie has an utter disregard for
human life—his own included. He will
calmly carve his woman’s head off, and
go to the gallows like a wooden man.
Local statistics show that the coolie-popu-
lation commits, in proportion, by far the
largest number of crimes—all the most glaring
offences, such as murders and rapes, having to be
laid to their account. The coolie’s evil influence
on the moral condition of the Fijian was observed
as early as ’97 by the Wesleyan Western Mission
authorities, and, as the coolie’s elevation was con-
sidered impossible, he and his vice were confined
to the plantations. But, as soon as the indentured
coolie gets his liberty, he goes through the land,
carrying with him all the concentrated vice,
obscenity, and disease of the plantation-camps.
If it be true, as asserted, that the natives of the
South Sea Islands are gradually dying out, then
the Australian Commonwealth is subjected to a
deadly peril by the British Government’s policy of
allowing all islands where cheap labor is scarce to
be supplied with our “fellow-subjects” from
India. Thus, Northern Australia, from Mary-
borough right round to Westralia, will, in the
future, be surrounded by a belt of low-living
Asiatics, &c., and the inhabitants of North-Coast
Queensland will have their evening sea-breezes
strongly impregnated with the taint of the East.
No exclusion laws can be made too rigorous in
view of the imminence and seriousness of the
peril.
Another Voice from Groper Land.
As a matter of fairness, The Bulletin publishes
this from “R.G.G.” :
Re “Carisbrook” on Westralian slavery (B. 3/8/’01).
Should not we receive with much caution the statements
of a man who, by his own confession, calmly stood by
while two young gins were cruelly' horsewhipped for a
trivial offence committed in ignorance, and who, on
another occasion, allowed a bullock-driver to bind a black-
boy hand and foot and flog him till the blood flowed
freely? Also, is it credible that a teamster should
have confessed to such a man or to anyone—
that he had flogged his black-boy to death and
buried him 50 miles from civilisation ? It is
also grossly improbable that a hand on the station
indicated should have confessed that, after wounding
a native with a rifle-bullet in the thigh, he had finished
him with a revolver shot. “ Oarisbrook’s ” excuse for
non-interference —“ the native sympathisers are few, and
it is a case of those few keeping their mouths closed if
they wish to live in that part of the State ” —is pure rot,
as is the statement: “ The tyranny exercised by squatters
(who are generally J’s.P.) over native sympathisers makes
life not worth living in that part.” My own experience,
based upon four years’ residence in, and travel through-
out, the N.W., is that the squatters are considered very
small potatoes by the community at large. The bulk
of the population are miners and men dependent
upon the mining industry, and the average miner is
hardly the man to care a straw about the lessee
of a few hundred square miles of spinifex-country.
Also, in cases that come before the courts the juries are
composed, not of station-hands, but of miners or trades-
people in the towns. The police don’t send out to a
station for a juryman when they can get him at the
nearest pub. Can “Carisbrook” prove any definite
case of running natives in from the desert and flogging
them into submission, which has occurred during the
past four years ? Will he name any station where white
men are kept for the purpose of flogging lazy' niggers ?
That the indenture-system has been abused is undeniable,
and some gross cases of cruelty have occurred within the
past few years, but these have been limited to one or two
stations and are not likely to be repeated. The natives
know perfectly' well that the Resident Magistrate, who is
not “ one of the good old Groper sort,” will strictly in-
vestigate any cases of alleged cruelty, and are not (slow
to tell their employers that they do know it. Did
“ Carisbrook ” ever contemplate the alternative
to the indenture-system ? The natives, many of
whom have been bred upon the stations, have
practically lost the art of hunting, and would much
prefer to loaf upon the white man for tucker.
Fancy' some thousands of them, with their gins, let loose
upon the mining settlements of the Nor’-West! The re-
port of the Chi A Inspector of Prisons contains the alarm-
ing .statement that over 9) per cent of the prisoners sent
to Fremantle from the Nor’-West suffer from diseases
contracted from the contaminated native women who in-
fest the camps. What then would happen should the
station-hands be turned adrift? As things are, the
natives are mostly well treated. I have known a squatter
near here pay £3 3s. per week for maintenance and medi-
cal attendance in Marble Bar Hospital for one of his black
boys; and the priest in charge of the district has stated
publicly that, “ next to the New Norcia and Beagle Bay
missions, the best Samaritans going the road were the
proprietors of such stations as Mundabullangana and
Croydon.” The natives won’t kill themselves with hard
work, and white men are not employed to flog them when
they are lazy (as they usually are). Neither has the
mining community any desire to relieve the squatters of
the cost of their keep and add to its own reputation for
immorality.

A Kynoch Cabinet.
Lord Rosebery, who lias a genius for babbling
pretentious inanities, lately called for a Cabinet
of “business-men” to run the sorely-perplexed
British Empire into a haven of peace. He forgets
that it is the business-man influence in the British
Cabinet which has worked the present war-havoc.
Joseph, the screw-maker, and Joseph’s brethren,
the sellers of ammunition; Cecil Rhodes, the
scheming millionaire; Beit and Co., the Ger-
man financiers —are these not all business-men of
the shrewdest business character ? And have they
not ridden rough-shod over the very “ unbusi-
nesslike ” members of the Cabinet; over the
sleepy pessimist, Salisbury, and the prinking
philosopher, Balfour ? The history of the Boer
war in brief is a history of business enterprise.
Rhodes, Barnato, Beit and others found the
thorough exploitation of the Rand difficult
under existing conditions. They approached
Kruger to assist them, and were pre-
pared (as Rhodes’ speeches most clearly
show) to go straight against the British
Empire, and for an Afrikander South Africa.
Kruger could not be induced to trust the gang,
nor, seemingly, could they make him “listen to
reason.” So they turned, on strict business prin-
ciples, from the Boer power (o the British power
in South Africa to help them, and the British
Cabinet was more foolish than the rude, unlettered,
pastoral President. A Cabinet of business-men !
It is the business-men, the mongrels of all races
and nations, who, flocking to London and domin-
ating England, are ruining her in body and soul,
corrupting her councils and her society, dispos-
sessing her peasants and squires of their heritage,
making Britain, not a nation, but a gambling
stock-exchange, with pleasure-gardens and deer-
parks attached. What England needs is power
to shake off these business-men, the Beits,
Rhodeses, Chamberlains, Astors, Morgans,
Carnegies, Hooleys— and with them, too, the
dangerous prattlers of the Rosebery type.
For the Encouragement of Intending
Cohentingenters.
Junes (N.S.W.) Southern Cross publishes this
letter received from three brothers who volun-
teered from that locality for service in S’ Africa :
Dear Sir, —According to promise, I am sending you a
few lines to let you know how we who volunteered, of
our free will, to fight and risk our lives for old England’s
sake, are treated by those who are over us. In the first
place, on the open veldt (where we have been constantly
since we landed, for we are with Remington’s Flying
Column) one never sees a single tree, although we have
travelled thousands of miles, unless it may be at a Boer
farm, where there have been a few willow or fruit trees
planted ; so that you can imagine the scarcity of fire-
wood. The only way that we can possibly get enough
wood to boil our quart pots to make tea is by breaking up
the deserted houses of the Boers and carrying away such
pieces of doors, flooring, or furniture as we can get in the
general scramble, for there will always be a rush for scraps
of fuel made by perhaps two or three hundred at a time ;
it is a case of everyone for himself and the Lord help the
hindmost. It is quite a common occurrence to see men
boiling their pots on a fire made from pianos, organs, &c.,
but of late an order has been issued that we, the Aus-
tralians, must carry no wood on our horses. That is a
hard knock to us, seeing that the Hussars and Innis-
killing Dragoons—part of our column —were not for-
bidden to carry wood on the front of their saddles. In
those regiments each squadron has cooks told off to cook
for the men, whereas the Australians have to draw their
rations, and every man be his own cook. Therefore, not
being allowed to carry firewood on approaching a camp
is rather hard. Now, the officers who are responsible for
issuing such an order have an orderly, or, in other words,
a man-servant, and a Gape cart loaded with firewood
for their comfort. There is another matter worth
mentioning, and one which almost caused a
mutiny. We had been allowed three blankets per man,
having no tents, and, sleeping on the veldt, where it was
constantly blowing cold, three were not a bit too much ;
but the other day an order came out that every man was
to hand in one blanket each. This would leave him but
one for under and one over him. A great many of the
men flatly refused to obey the order for a long time, until
it was explained that now they were soldiers they must
obey, even if they were ordered to give up every blanket
they possessed ; if they'refused, they could be tried by
court-martial for mutiny and get five years’ penal servi-
tude. The reason given for taking our blankets was that
they wanted to lighten the loads on the waggon, so that
we might be able to do forced marches. But while they
take away one blanket out of three from the men, the
officers have waggon-loads of comforts and all sorts of
rubbish as curios that they have collected. No wonder
the officers don’t wish the war to finish, for they are
getting big pay', and are having a regular picnic, while
the troopers are getting all the hard knocks and the
roughest usage. The scale of rations per day per man
is five small hard biscuits, or, in lieu of that, two panni-
kins of flour, two spoonfuls of sugar, one ounce of
tea, one pound of meat (which each one may cook the
best he may). General Remington does little else but
night marching, and on the day following a whole night
march, when one would reasonably suppose that we
could sleep, we have to feed horses in the morning, and,
after breakfast, take horses out to graze till dinner time,
then feed once more, and, after dinner, graze again, and
so on. To tell the truth, Ido not think he would halt at
all for the men’s sake, but simply to save the horses. The
Kaffir drivers and the Boer refugees are all treated better
than we are, and I can safely say that, out of a thousand
men from New South Wales with this column, if they
could leave their job as they can in Australia when it
does not suit him, there would not be a man left in the
morning. They would all sling it. It is a regular thing
to hear men say, any minute of the day, that they would
never volunteer again, even if England was in real danger.
All their loyalty has left them, from the treatment they
receive.
The Evangel of Eight.
Grattan Guinness, “medical evangelist,” de-
livered an address to men only at Melbourne Town
Hall, the other night, and, as a matter of course,
the subject of the Guinness shriek was “ Vice ”
—the Vice which needs both sexes to make it, and
is largely dependent on Mrs. Grundy for its
mischievousness. Guinness, seemingly, has
walked around Melbourne at night, looking for
shocks. He found them. “He shudders when
he thinks of the banks of the Yarra, of the Fitzroy
Gardens, and all other Gardens,” as they appear
after dark to the citizen who wants material for
addresses on Vice. He reckons that darkness and
public reserves are the parents of Vice; therefore
he would turn a strong electric-light upon all the
gardens and close them at night, “as is done in
London,” —which, considering that Guinness
instances London as the wickedest of all cities
since Time was, doesn’t sound like a very logical
suggestion for a remedy. Guinness, being a
fervent evangelist, must talk nonsense. The dim
endearments of those couples (to whom every-
thing in the Gardens is lovely) have got
to happen somewhere, and the fact that
they happen where they do is accounted for
by Guinnessism of all descriptions. The
environments of “Vice” are just what Purity
Leaguers, etc., choose to make them. Those
? saintly souls are shocked at the thought of human
animal nature having rational allowances made
for it; consequently every city that regulates
public morality by the pecksniffian British code
compels a state of things which seems ridiculous
to heathen Japanese. The Law’s humbug is
responsible for the presence of the actual prowling
prostitute within public gardens at night. The
Law says that Traviata has no Vice Quarter of
the city where she can always be found, so she
haunts the gardens in order to get accidentally
discovered by chance customers. Surely Guinness
doesn’t expect to electric-light the woman off the
face of a social-evilled earth ! As for the other
couples whose sweethearting, of a sentimental but
ardent character, leads to matrimony much more
often than it leads to “unmarried ruin,” they have
just as much right to be in the public gardens at 10
p.m. as any couple at a vice-regal ball have to the
privacy of His Excellency’s reserve at 2 a.m.
What the raging Guinness practically pleads for
is an abolition of all shady places where a man and
a girl can be together unobserved. He would
turn a limelight upon every paddock, and put a
10ft. fence around the Australian bush. And all
because there are certain dangers to public health
against which the Government d-eclines to take
proper precautions, the backwardness of the
Government being due to the snufffasome scruples
of the Evangelicalism that keeps Guiness going.
The defeat of the Leake Government, in W.A.
after five months of suspense, came as a relief to
all parties in that State. From the moment the
Ministry assumed office it was evident that its
party was in a minority, and business was blocked
by the Opposition every time. The old Forrest
party, at the general elections, was in such bad
odor that some Forrestites ran as independent
candidates, and repeatedly avowed their intention
of giving the Leake Government a fair chance.
This accounts for the five months’ delay in the
tabling of the no-confidence motion ; it was a con-
cession by Piesse to save the faces of the so-called
Independents. Truth to tell, the Leake Govern-
ment was somewhat disappointing, but it must be
recollected that it had the arrears of ten years of
Forrestism to clear up, and that it was
consistently -blocked by the Opposition majority.
There has been a tremendous stirring up
of the dry bones, and many shady transactions
have been laid bare to the public gaze. The air
has now been cleared, and the prospects for the
future are decidedly brighter, for it is realised by
all that the autocratic methods of Forrest are
things of the past, and that constitutional govern-
ment is now to take its proper place. The
Democrats have been taught a severe lesson, and
are likely to profit by it. Steps are now being
taken to secure an alliance of the two wings of
the party to avoid splitting votes in the future.
The Liberals have an enormous majority in the
country, and it seems likely that a dissolution
cannot be long delayed. At the polls they should
triumph easily.

G. H. Reid’s wild yell of “ How are we to
manufacture cheaply and compete with the cheap
labor of other countries —how are we going to
% compete with these underpaid, sweated coun-
tries ? ” was about the biggest indictment of Free-
trade that ever burst from that cavern of winds.
There is only one way in which it can be done—
by erecting an iron-clad tariff wall right round
the continent, and strict Government control of
the remuneration for and the conditions of labor.
The Dry Dog Man seems to think that we can pro-
duce raw material better and more cheaply than any
other country in the world —produce it under such
favorable circumstances as to be able to pay good
wages. And it is all rats ! The most wretchedly-
paid toiler on this continent is the producer of
raw material. The man on the land makes mighty
little, but the unfortunate wretch who works for
the man on the land is beyond hope. In a few
years more Morocco, South Africa, Uganda, the
valley of the Nile with improved irrigation, and
the Argentine will become still keener rivals in
the production of raw material, and the “natural
industries ” will pay worse wages than ever

“ J.D.” writes :
Current comment labels the acumen of the Manly ferry-
boat employees in the recent dispute as “ remarkable.”
Under cover of a left-handed compliment it merely echoes
what the average ill-informed daily newspaper is always
saying in regard to the proceedings of labor-unions in
general, i.e., that they' are incompetently managed.
It considers not the arduous climbing of organised
labor from the small, irregular and inarticulate
protests of a dozen years ago up to? the possession
of a Labor Party strong enough to compel Arbi-
tration and Navigation Acts at the hands of Parliament.
During the past decade there have been numerous
instances of successful generalship on the part of Union
leaders, beside which the little ferry-boat success is a
mere circumstance hardly worth considering. The old-
time gibe at the toilers for having corns on their brains
as well as on their hands is not too fair in the circum-
stances. If the material they had to work with in their
poverty, the opposition to be overcome, the bribes to be
refused, were all known and considered fairly, the won-
der would be that anything whatever had been accom-
plished by the Union organisers.

One of Lord Ketchener’s latest general orders
(No. 380) to the Army in S’Africa :
The Commander-in-Chief in South Africa desires to im-
press on officers in command of mobile columns that the
object of such columns is mobility ; that he has learned
that such forces carried about with them furniture,
kitchen-ranges, pianos, and harmoniums, which nullify
that object; that these articles must be handed over to
the nearest stores, &c.
What humorists ! After two years of war this
kind of discovery is only just being made, and we
can now guess what must have been the state of
things in 1899, when the British dandies
thought the campaign would be a beautiful
picnic. A mobile column carrying furniture,
kitchen-ranges, and even harmoniums, for the
officahs, whilst the common soldiers at the front
were half-starved on a biscuit a day ! The world
wondered at De Wet’s escapes, but it couldn’t
have been very hard to circumvent the “ flying ”
column commanded by Majah Reginald Perot
Cholmondeley with 500 men and a cargo of
pianos, harmoniums, and cooking-ranges.

The Englishman (Calcutta) puts in a plea for the
free admission of Hindoos to Australia:
It is surely right that loyal subjects of the Crown should
receive more consideration at the hands of the Colonial
authorities than foreigners, however friendly.
Not much ! The most hostile Frenchman, German
or Russian on earth may come here and spend his
spare time cursing the British Crown with all the
language he knows ; but his son will wander after
the local girl as all sons do ; and his grandson will
probably be undistinguishable from any other
Australian. And the most loyal Hindoo that ever
grovelled to the Crown may come here, and if his
son marries the local girl (Heaven help her !) his
grandson will simply be a mongrel named Ram
something or other. The German, Dane and
Swede are our brothers, come of the same stock,
and even if they burned London to the ground
they would be material out of which good
Australians can be made all the same. And even if
the Hindoo saved the Empire from destruction he
wouldn't. The Lord so fixed things that the
Anglo-German cross is a good white citizen and
the Anglo-Hindoo isn’t, and the Loud knows a
great deal more than the British Crown. Though
in this matter the British Crown also has some
sense. When England’s royal family leaves off
intermarrying with the hostile Germans and
Russians, and takes to finding its husbands and
wives from among the loyal Hindoos, then Aus-
tralia will re-consider its race prejudice!

The See Ministry assumed office in N.S.W. as a
“ Country ” Ministry, and it took to its bosom
“ the man on the land,” and promised to kill for
him many fatted beeves. It hasn’t. It promised
a Rabbit Bill with cheap wire-netting, an amended
Land Bill to redress some of the glaring anomalies
of present Acts, a consolidation of the 17 Land Acts
now in existence, a Closer Settlement Bill, a Local
Government Bill, and hard roads to every cockey’s
gate. It sent the O’Sullivan to promise railway
lines to everybody and in every direction.
Within a few months the See Ministry has even
forgotten its adopted name, and hasn’t made a
serious pretence of redeeming one bucolic pro-
mise. The bush Israelites have many Moseses,
but never a Joshua.

The annual report of New South Wales State
Children Relief Board shows that, since the
inauguration of the boarding-out system in 1891,
17,380 children have passed through the Board’s
hands. Of that number, 6975 are still under con-
trol, 10,405 have been either reclaimed by their
relatives, adopted by their foster-parents, or gone
out into the world to earn their own living. Only
a small percentage have died, while a few have
been transferred to other public institutions. For
the past year but £306 2s. lOd. has been paid for
medicines and doctors, and the death-rate among
the children absolutely separated from their
parents was only 4 per 1000. Compare this with
the frightful mortality among the illegitimates !
The sooner that beneficent Board gets more
power and a bigger staff the better for the
murdered illegitimates of N.S.W.
4
The Federal Government should at the earliest
opportunity give some consideration to the ques-
tion of encouraging—by Protective duties on
canned fish, and, perhaps, by bonuses —a fishing
industry in Australia. At present, there is prac-
tically no effort made to gather in the harvests of
the sea. An odd harbor or lake is scraped
by a few fisherfolk, whose gleanings give a
scanty and dear supply of fresh fish, but
a greater part of the fish eaten in Aus-
tralia is imported in cans from England and
America. No reason exists why we should not
supply our own demands and build up an export
trade. The fish are here and await catching and
canning. In Great Britain the fishing industry
finds employment for many thousands, and cheap,
wholesome food for millions. But the canning
and curing is carried on under frightful sweating
conditions. Fish-curing factories are exempted
from the English Factories Act, and at Yarmouth
women and children work from daybreak until
midnight at gutting, salting, and packing
herrings. The work is done in the open
air, or in miserable sheds which give no pro-
tection against wind or rain, and girls aged
from 11 to 15 years have to labor under these con-
ditions 16 hours a day. The thought of this does
not make the English herring very appetising,
and affords another reason why Australia should
make haste to establish a fish-curing industry of
its own under fair labor conditions.

“Kiwi” sends this bitter curse from the other
side of the water :
Why “crack up” the Maoriland land policy, or, more
correctly, landless policy'? The graduated land-tax was
designed by the Ballance Govt, £6 compel the “cutting
up of large estates,” and great was the howl among the
rich fraternity in consequence. Before the tax got a
chance to do its much-needed work, however, along came
the M‘Kenzie-cmjr-Seddon system of land-purchase, with
the result that every territorial grabber is now hanging
on, quite content to pay the land-tax in the hope of
Government buying him out. In plain English, we are
borrowing money to buy land for settlement, which has
been made worth buying chiefly on account of the spend-
ing of other borrowed m \ey on public works ; and now
the two systems of borrowing and spending are proceed-
ing merrily side by sid •. The State, insteail of getting
by taxation the enhanced value due to the spending of
money on public works, is allowing the landowner
to pocket the lot, and then the landowner is in
turn being bought out with other borrowed money !
Needless to say, the system suits ihe squatter
crowd admirably—they naturally prefer being bought out
to being taxed out. This is the reason the late John
McKenzie died such a popular man. The great unin-
formed crowd has a hazy notion that the policy means
putting a few score small monopolists in the place of one
large- one, and they conclude that all is well. The
selectors who want land like the system because they get
land, and they don’t trouble to look deeper than the
surface of things ; the organised workers are of opinion
that the question doesn’t directly concern them, and
they are satisfied with eight-hours legislation and
kindred measures. Last of all, the squatter likes the
system for the reasons above stated. Small wonder
that Seddon now gets support from both the fat and
the lean man 1 But there is worse to follow : The pur-
chased land, instead of being leased with periodical re-
valuations, is given away in 200 and 300-acre lots on pay-
ment of 5 per cent annual rent for 999 .years without re-
valuation. The rent goes to pay interest on the purchase-
money plus roading, surveying, etc.,‘ and the land, being
technically Crown land, pays no land-tax. Therefore the
State gets no direct r-evenue from the land, and, as there
is no provision for a sinking-fund, the principal bids fair
to become a debt in perpetuity. Small wonder, there-
fore, that leases-in-perpetuity are now being gambled in
as freeholds were a few years ago. Then, again, we have
not abolished the curse of the freehold. Our Crown land
is rapidly becoming alienated under this criminal system.
True, very little of the land is disposed of as freehold in
the first instance, but it is settled on perpetual lease
(which must not be confounded with lease in perpetuity)
and deferred payments, under both of which there is pro-
vision for ultimate purchase. Now we have in vogue a
system of State money-lending, and the largest advances
are, of course, made on freehold. Result : everybody
who can is converting his lease into freehold to take
advantage of the -new borrowing facilities. The real
remedy for the land evil is direct taxation of the unearned
increment.

Melbourne Customs authorities have again made
a stir by confiscating a consignment of Zola’s
works. Zola certainly wrote some horrible stuff
(his “La Terre” drew forth a formal protest from
the leading French writers of the realistic school),
but in the average English translations his boobs
are not such as to deserve to be damned by a
tide-waiter custodian of public morals. Some
definite policy on this matter of forbidden literary
fruit should be promptly decided on. As to
books which are flagrantly filthy, and nothing
but filthy, there need be no question. But
the books which may harm an innocent mind, the
works of Shakespeare, Spenser, Chaucer, Swift,
Dryden, Fielding, Smollett, Meredith, prac-
tically all the Greek and Latin classics, nine-
tenths of continental literature, the Bible, and
other more or less notable works—what about
them ? If all that can rub the bloom off
the peach of maiden innocence is excluded,
we must live in a literary wilderness. And to
exclude, on a silly system inspired by British
prejudice, or by sheer ignorance, some books.

which, besides its injustice, has the danger that it
might lead in time to a Customs censorship of

sensible system is to leave to the individual citizen
