cases, fail, and a child get from the Bible, or from
Shakespeare, or from Zola, some moral harm.

alternative is prohibition of all the world’s best
literature.
SUNDRY SHOWS.

“The Sign of the Cross” has prospered
exceedingly during the week at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney (in spite of the severe competition of
the Simultaneous Mission). Besides convey-
ing to many thousands a vivid impression
of the traditional idea of the wrongs of
the Catacomb Christian, the play has brought
some few to a full sympathy with the feelings of
that little girl who, shown a picture of wild
animals devouring martyrs in the Roman amphi-
theatre, burst into tears, at the thought that one
noble and sorrowful-looking lion bad no Early
Christian. If this play correctly delineates the
heterodox people of Nero’s day—well, there’s a
good deal to be said for Nero. On Saturday (Nov.
30) “Silver King,” the finest melodrama ever
written, will be staged, with Mr. Barrett and
Miss M'Carthy in the chief parts.
** * *
At Sydney Royal the last nights of Lady
Isabel are announced. This version of the drama,
as before mentioned, is rather an improvement on
some others ; nevertheless there is badly wanted
an up-to-date rendering of “East Lynne” in
which that impulsive lady shall come to a less
melancholy end. It is noticeable that the dressing
for this mid-century tale is invariably up-to-date,
save that Cornelia Carlyle occasionally goes right
back to the fashions of old times. Nobody goes
back, save Cornelia. Her skirts are scanty and
her curls corkscrewed, but Lady Isabel, who lived
in the same ungraceful and poke-bonnetted period,
is a dream in modern clothes and modern grace.
It is like Saul in ancient Hebrew robes conferring
with Jonathan in evening dress and a gleaming
shirt-front on Mount Gilboa, or the spectacle of
eleven apostles in ancient Syrian garb watching
the twelfth as he ran after his blown-off top-hat in
Galilee.
** * *
That somewhat tired Siamese-American
monarch, “Wang,” left Sydney Criterion stage
last Friday, and “Fra Diavolo” Vas staged on
Saturday. In this piece, as before remarked, the
Josephine Stanton company shows itself at its
best. A particularly good orchestra does full
justice to the music, and if there is no very great
strength of solo singers, the general brightness of
the chorus and smartness of the acting all round
atone for that defect. A new tenor, Mr. J.
Clarence Harvey, has strengthened the co. since
last production of “Diavolo.” The opera was
warmly received on Saturday, and its production
will be continued until Nov. 30, when “Dorcas”
is to be staged, assuming that lady has got her
clothes ready and is otherwise fit.
** * *
“ The Ladder of Life ” is drawing to a
close at Sydney Lyceum, and that interesting
military drama, in which the Highlanders win all
England’s glory for her, will shortly give way to
something that sounds instructive and elevating—
“Honour Thy Father.” After the smoke and
powder of Dargai Heights, and the frequent rush-
ing of excited Highlanders across the stage, the
new play will probably prove restful, even if the
father in question is killed off in the first act, and
after that the play is the usual melodrama.
* *
“ Jack ” Crain, whose connection with the
Australasian stage dates back to ’79, will shortly
be the recipient of a benefit, prior to his depar-
ture from Sydney. This is his first appeal to the
public, although his services have always been
freely at the disposal of others. A big bill is in
preparation.
*# * *
The Bernard Espinasse matinee last week,
at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, showed the desire of
the public and of the dramatic profession to give
the young dramatist a good send-off. The pro-
gramme was full of attractions, and the audience
large and enthusiastic.
** * *
The variety form of entertainment, which
had been a rather neglected quantity in Mel-
bourne for a good many weeks, regained its popu-
larity with a rush last Saturday night. At the
Opera House, where the new patent punkah
arrangements came as a breezy blessing in sur-
prise, every seat was filled or booked long before
the curtain went up, and for half-an-hour there-
after the firm but polite refusal of hard
cash proceeded steadily until the world in
general knew that Rickards was himself
again. The time had arrived for Rickards
to buck up, so he bucked. Saturday’s pro-
gramme at the Opera House consisted of
a first part that didn’t drag, and a second part in
which one good turn deserved another, and almost
invariably got it. Those admirable song-and-
dancers, the Allisons, scored a roar of applause
early in the evening. A gaudy ballet, with Dora
Taylor as the heroine of a pantomime story, was
only a little too long. Charles Pastor, a new
arrival, produced an infectious laugh to justify his-
importation; and Peggy Pryde seemed to be
accepted as the only woman they had ever
loved. Peggy, this time, looks about 22 in
the limelight, and fully equal to another
success. Melbourne is going to let Peggy
rattle along as before with the tongue that works
on a swivel, and if she occasionally lapses into a
sentimental ballad concerning the honeysuckle and
the bee, her staunch admirers will overlook the
indiscretion. All the same, they don’t ask for any
honeysuckle sentiment from their humorous little
friend. She has brought some up-to-date patter,
and as often as she cares to mention the
lodger’s loin-chops that used to hum in his
bedroom cupboard, Peggy will hum like-
wise. After the festive little comedian came
the Craggs, who are seemingly some of
the same people whom George Musgrove brought
to Australia ten years ago, though two of them,
anyway, must have been horn in the meantime.
The Craggs are considerably more than clever
acrobats in the style which was first identified
with their “Family ” name. They are wonderful.
Other troupes are said to have surpassed the swift
and graceful Craggs’ performance, but Australia,
not knowing those other people, remains sceptical
on the point.
** « *
At the Bijou the “ World’s Entertainers ”
began their season of “Polite Vaudeville” on
Saturday, and a crowded house found it not too
polite to be pleasant. Lee and Riall’s vaudeville
differs from Pickards’ variety show at the outset
in not having a first-part scene, but otherwise there
is a cousinly resemblance between the two, each of
them concluding with a biograph under a different
name. Fifteen Entertainers in all supply 11 turns.
The principal feature of the bill appears to be
Henry Lee, who impersonates six great men, in
addition to himself, and has somewhat improved
upon his “turn” of years agone by changing his
make-up in view of the audience, instead of
sandwiching his impersonations between dull
slabs of darkness. Henry Lee of yore sent
no _ thrills of admiration through the present
scribe. His performance didn’t look quite worth
the introductory remarks and the time spent in
dressing his parts behind the curtain. Now that
Lee reveals the mysteries of his toilette, the turn
has lost its early heaviness, without gaining a
particle of humor. Charles Sweet, as the Musical
Burglar, is quite as bright as the departed Musical
Tramp, or even brighter. Also Arthur Nelstone
has a droll pair of legs and an air of freshness,
and the dancing generally is quaint and slick.
The entertainers are disposed to be mild and soft
in two or three instances, especially the instance
of the handsome lady who sings long narrative
songs to a photographic accompaniment, but
every turn has some sort of smartness to re-
commend it, and the stage-management is netice-
ably keen throughout. American showmen have
discovered, the importance of a strict attention to
small details in stage business.
** * *
Six nights of “ The Magistrate ” are hap-
pening at Melb. Princess’s. One must admit that
the bulk of Pinero’s light-hearted comedy was
more effectively played once, or twice, or thrice
upon a time than it appears this week. But, seeing
that Brough is a Mr. Posket of 16 yvars’ experi-
ence, and the comedy depends solely on his co.
for whatever production it can get, and Australia
wouldn’t be happy if it never got it, the compara-
tive shortcomings of this performance will be
generally received with thanks. In fact, they are
not worth mentioning, except for the sake of
argument. Moreover, the difference between
Titheradge and Cecil Ward as the gallant Colonel
who gets forcibly washed, is of less consequence
than an alteration in Kingston’s tariff. And
people will go to “The Magistrate” to get tariff
out of their ears and minds. The above remarks
are intended to lead up to an announcement that
Brough’s season at the Princess’s will inaugurate
its farewell fortnight on Saturday with the pro-
duction of “ Mrs. Dane’s Defence.”
#* * *
This is the fifth week of “Hearts are
Trumps ” at the Royal, and the health of Bland
Holt’s mighty spectacle remains robust.
** * *
Fitzgerald’s Circus, likewise, is going
strong and amassing riches for the brethren, who
erected a temple of iron on a stretch of wilderness
and defied the hailstones from on high. Said
hailstorm, by the way, reduced the Fitz.’s best
menagerie-tent to fragments, after which a plain,
ordinary tempest came and shattered their second-
best tent; but little troubles of that sort
seem to be the salt of circus-life. The Diving
Horses have now mastered their feelings, and they
drop down to their task eight times a week with
more alacrity than they showed on the first night.
The white horse is particularly loyal to the
management. On arriving at the point where it
is required to get into the tank somehow, the
animal looks around with a what-price-me ? ex-
pression on its honest, matronly face, and dives
with its forelegs outstretched in an attitude of
prayer.
** * *
“ A Chinese Honeymoon” is the name of
a successful farcical comedy lately staged at
London Strand. Presumably it is not quite
so realistic as the Honeymoon play produced
by the Chinese co. in Melbourne during its
winter season of 100 nights, or so. The Chow
comedy showed how a one-time married pair got
separated, and, supposing one another to be dead,
came together again in second marriage. The
custom of the country keeps the lady’s face veiled
from her lord until the bridal, so the discovery of
the startling truth in this case was reserved
for the last act. A big four-poster bed,
THE LATE HON. GEORGE THORNTON , M.L.C.
Photo, by J. Hubert Newman, Sydney.
with curtains all round it, was the sole
“ set ” for this final act. The bride came
forward, sang her little song of love and happiness,
and disappeared behind the drapings of the four-
poster. _ Then the bridegroom came and expressed
his sentiments, and sought the nuptial coucn, after
which the orchestra played honeymoon music of
an amusing character. Then the climax. Bride-
groom sprang into publicity from between the
curtains and told of his surprise, not unmingled
with satisfaction, on discovering that the Old
Wife and the New were one and the same girl.
After which the audience dispersed, smiling
celestially.
** * *
Polite Vaudeville did not do too well at
Adelaide Royal. Rickards has accustomed Ade-
laide people to 3-2-1 prices, and they did not fancy
going back to the 5-3-1 scale. Thus (except for
Saturdays and a holiday) only the cheaper
parts filled up well. Also the Dartos were simul-
taneously showing at the Tivoli. At one particu-
larly disastrous matinee, Sweet, the Musical
Burglar, asked the house not to trouble to
applaud. “ There are not enough of you to
make a noise, anyway 1 ”
** * *
“ Otello ” is a blaze and a crash of color,
light, noise, and fury, horrifying rather than
touching, more passionate than pathetic. Its
music excites the listener at the moment of hear-
ing and masters his imagination, but the opera
seeme short of little themes, which, heard on
Saturday night, haunt a hearer’s ear and memory,
keeping him awake in church next morning. A
catchy exception is Besdemona’s swan-song,
which, beginning with a quaint, churchy recitatif,
merges into the plaintive “ Ave Maria” sung by
the “fev” mourner while she listens to “Death’s
cold, sullen stream ” lapping at her feet. Another
popular bit is a stirring thing, drummed out by
Othello —“Farewell to Glorious War,” the writer
fancies.
* » * *
The Moor, as interpreted by composer
Verdi, librettist Boito and singer Larizza, is
Shakespeare’s hero seen with one eye. He’s the
defrauded husband yelling “Blood, lago, blood !”
rather than the heart-wrung lover, lamenting “0
lago, the pity of it, lago ! ” Shakespeare, by the
way, built his revised and improved “Othello” on
an Italian story of a jealous Moor, who looked on
while his wife was beaten to death by a minion
with a stocking filled with sand, and then
pulled down the house to hide her corpse.
Larizza, discarding character complexities and
contradictions, takes us back to this original
corner-stone and shows us a straight-goer, who,
when once he’s angered, hates like a lunatic, digs
Desdemona brutally with his elbows, and batters
her whenever he gets the chance. Musical
authorities testify that it would baffie even a
Wagner to translate an all-round Shakespearean
“ Othello ” into opera, so, failing the whole hog,
Verdi’s “Otello” certainly chooses the strongest
part in presenting the Moor’s robust, revengeful
side, rather than his sentimental streak. A plain-
tive, puling Othello would be an absurdity.
• • • •
An enormous first - night audience ap-
plauded “ Otello ” wildly, though a cynical few
sniggered when the hero dug his poor wife
savagely in the ribs, and again when he burst into
her beautiful old-Venetian chamber, with his
melodramatic eyeballs, black face, and swaying
blunderbuss. The operatic Desdemona is a tame,
ill-used warbler, though, as hubby testifies,
“qualified to sing the savageness out of a bear.”
Marchi, as Emilia, suggested a stately Italian pic-
ture come to life. Bozzoli, slim and dandified
of appearance as lago, was artistio as usual, and
added to his popularity with local women, which
has been growing ever since the fact of there
being a Mrs. Dani made itself felt.
* * * *
As a spectacle, “ Otello ” is gorgeous and
convincing. As an object lesson of the desira-
bility of a White Australia, it is invaluable.
Sydney Associated Laundries’ advance of
25 per cent, in prices is a most impudent “try-
on.” Original excuse for the rise was the tariff;
argued out of their corner, the owners took refuge
behind the preposterous allegation that the
laundry employees would shortly demand in-
creased wages, therefore, &c., &c. As a method
of compelling the public to contribute to a bosses’
strike-fund, which may be used to successfully
reduce the wages of the laundry workers, this
takes the starch right out of the stiffest trick ever
previously heard of in these parts. Writer is
flad to hear of one laundry boss who admits that
e has already lost 40 out of 200 customers, so the
pubbc is evidently kicking.
Australian dailies last Tuesday published
two S’African cablegrams side by side. One
alluded to the Boers who are still, against
stupendous odds, holding up the last rag of the
banner of independence, as “rebels”; the other
described that handful of despicable traitors who
have offered to fight along with the British
against their own countrymen and their own
country’s independence as the “loyal Boers.”
And so they are loyal—in the same sense as
Benedict Arnold, and Judas Iscariot, and the
Irish informer in the melodrama, and all other
snch characters. The question for every Aus-
tralian : Had you been born a Boer which would
you rather be —the man who fought to the last for
his country or the thing that took pav from its
foes?
It took 20 days to get through the big Tas.
case wherein the awful V.D.L. Co. unsuccessfully
sued the Govt, for trespass. Senator Symon,
addressing the jury, stressed the point that a
verdict for the co. would be a disaster to Tas-
mania ; but as the grab-all Co. has been raising a
V.D.L. of an unpleasant smell in the island for
the last half-century or more, the K.C.’s appeal
to the jury’s parochialism was quite unnecessary.
As the V.D.L. is disgustingly wealthy, it may
change the venue to the Privy Council, or Federal
High Court, for no less than 150 miles of foreshore
are involved.
The tailoresses’ strike in Sydney continues
though most of the employers have come to
terms with the Union. Hordern’s girls are still
out. Mr. Sam Hordern’s declaration that he
would prefer to close his factory rather than allow
a widow employee who wouldn’t join the union
to resign, is, so far, the best joke of the
dispute. The idea of the astute Mr. Hordern
voluntarily surrendering very big profits weekly
when for forty shillings a week he could
pension this particular employee is rather too
absurd for credence. Public sympathy is still
with the tailoresses. Last Saturday’s collections
m the street, and contributions from other unions,
are said to have made the week’s strike-pay about
17s. 6d. per head—which is more than the normal
wages of the girls.

The Bank of N.S.W. is about as good a barometer of the
country’s (and especially of N.S.W.’s) financial state as
could be found. It shows that interest rates are higher,
the demand for money greater, and the money available
rather less. The decline in deposits and note circulation
are probably the least promising features in the situation.

Maori land is borrowing £1,500,000 at 3 per
cent, in London, at a fixed price of £94. From this there
must be deducted £1 per cent, for underwriting, also
expenses and accrued interest, so the net price is likely to
be about £92. At this rate Maoriland will incur
£1,500,000 of debt, and receive only £1,380,000. The other
£120,000 is interest paid out of ioans—in other words
Maoriland’s credit is good enough to borrow at between
and 3£ per cent, and it borrows at 3 per cent., the
difference being added to the principal of the debt. It is

Vanning Tables. —The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

Gentleman having only a limited time at their
disposal can obtain the practical instruction in assaying
and metallurgical work, necessary to qualify them for
obtaining good positions, in the ore testing works of Gil-
fillan and McCreery, Stock Exchange Buildings, Melb.*

A very successful trial of the Gardner Electric
Drill took place at the works of the Austral Otis Com-
pany, South Melbourne, a few days ago. The drill,
although only temporarily fixed, bored 8£ inches in three
minutes through hard basalt. A number of prominent
mining men witnessed the test, and expressed themselves
as greatly pleased with the machine. The Austral Otis
Company have been appointed Sole Agents in Australasia
for the Gardner Electric Drill.*

* * * *
If further proof is needed that Luhrig Vannera
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Vanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work.*

Talisman Consolidated, M.L. : Curie, the well-
known'(EcoNOMiST) critic, said of this show last year that
it was the second in M.L., admitting that the Waihi was
far first. Ourle made some big mining mistakes in other
countries, and his opinion about this mine appears to
have been quite erroneous. Adjoining blocks were ac-
quired in the good old Bewick-Moreing way, by the issue
of shares. The old plant—apparently quite big enough
for the mine—was discarded, and a new 50-head mill with
cyanide erected. Crushing started over three months ago,
and the clean-up was postponed for all sorts of alleged
reasons. It took place the other day, and it leaked out
that the value of the ore is only 265. per ton, and the
tonnage much less than might reasonably be expected
from so large a battery running for so long a time. It is
also the result of picked ore, a number of hands having
been employed in sorting, so as to bring the
crushing up to expectations. Nathless it is a
bad disappointment. Shares went to 355. weeks ago
when the plates were “ looking well,” and fell to 21s. 6d.
as soon as the result of crushing went up. Average value
of ore as mined certainly not £1 a ton ; it could not pos-
sibly pay at that. Ore-reserves small, reefs of no size, and
not opened up to any extent, and the co., like many
another placed on the English market in boom-time, pro-
mises to fade away.

a foolish, disreputable swindle, but these States are doing
the same foolish, disreputable swindle all the time, and
they are so used to it that if anyone proposed to make
good the £120,000 loss out of revenue he would probably
be regarded as a person who was needlessly particular
and altogether too conscientious for this world.
If Ist July be taken as the beginning of the
financial year (which it is in five of the seven States) then
five months of the new year have elapsed, and in that
brief period the seven States of Australasia have borrowed
in London no less than £9,324,213, in addition to large
amounts raised locally. And being driven by their wild
boom policy to go on raising money, despite the utter
badness of the market, they have lost something not far
short of £1,000,000 on the transaction. Maoriland, selling
its loan at 93 for every £lOO, with flotation expenses to
come out of that, which will probably reduce it below £92,
makes the best bargain of the lot, bad as that bargain
is. N. S. Wales got a little less, and Westralia and
Queensland were glad to get about £BB. Earlier in the
year Victoria had to renew a £3,000,000 loan, and got
about £9O. It is a very costly 'loan boom that these
States are inaugurating, and a very big one. And it differs
from the boom of the late ’Bo’s in this, that there are just
now very few really promising railways to construct, and
the money is being buried in non-productive works at a
rate never before heard of in Australia. Wherefore the
interest must needs be paid out of taxation, and more
revenue must shortly be found, unless some new swindle
can be devised to postpone the evil day.

The Colonial Mutual Fire Insurance Co.
(Melbourne) is memorable as one of the Australian
companies which spread its business recklessly to foreign
parts (the reason always assigned against State fire
insurance is that it would be purely local and couldn’t
have the blessed advantage of spreading to foreign parts),
went practically broke there as per usual, and yet had
the grit and ability to fully retrieve its position. This is
the record of its smash and its recovery :

1900.. 16,184 8 = 3,000 2000 39,747
1901.. 27,789 {Add. to capiunS} 61 ’ 036
The addition of £12,500 of the profits to capital makes
the latter £50,000 paid-up, so the total shareholders’
funds are now £101,036. As the shareholders
actually paid up only £37,500 their nominal 8 per
cent, dividend really represents £lO 13s. 4d. per
cent., which is a highly respectable rate. The
business shows remarkable expansion. For the
year ended September, 1898, the gross premium income
was £89,845, while for the year to September, 1901, it
reached £135,504. During the last eleven years the
Colonial Mutual Fire 00. has provided for £10,295 lost
through depreciation of securities, and has added
£83,447 out of profits to its shareholders’ funds. Its
investments (almost wholly State and municipal securities
and bank deposits) look very sound, and last year yielded
£2Blo—sufficient in itself to pay 7£ per cent, dividend on
the original capital. The Colonial Mutual hasn’t far to
go now till it becomes the leading Victorian fire company
—in fact, it may have attained that position already if it
was possible to make an accurate comparison; and
another ten years like the last might make it the leading
fire co. of Australia.

Harrison, Jones and Devlin reckons its reserves at
£66,097 (reserve fund, £52,500, and undivided profit,
£3597), but as £40,000 of this is represented only by the
paper asset of “goodwill” (an asset which should most
certainly be written off), the real reserves may more
fairly be set down at the £16,097 shown above.
The £lO,OOO written off this year is mainly to
cover losses arising through advances made on wool at
the inflated values of 1899. The other £28,013, written off
since 1895, is in diminution of the paper-value of the co.’s
freehold property, which has been reduced from £179,513
to £151,500. Whether there is any further depreciation
to be provided for under this heading it is impossible,
in the absence of any evidence, to say. But, considering
the uncertain position of things this year, it is a pity that
the dividend was noe cut down to 6 per cent., so as to
keep it within the earnings. The present isn’t a good
time to pay dividends that are not fully earned. The
co. staggers under a pretty heavy mortgage on its free-
hold, and though this liability has been reduced from
£97,000 in 1895 to £76,000 in 1901, it is still a consider-
able drain on the profits.

The North Queensland Insurance Co. has grown very
rapidly of late years. In 1896 its gross premium income was
£B2-252, which was a very large amount for an Australian
office, this being a land where fire insurance are
mostly small and humble. But since then things have
expanded after this fashion :
Gross Premiums.
1896 .. £82,252 1899 .. £116,227
1897 .. 102,014 1900 .. 151,567
1898 .. 107,712 1901 .. 160,770
One notable reason of the progress made by the North
Queensland Insurance Co. is its cheap management—less
than 15 per cent, of the gross income goes in expenses,
and it is almost the most cheaply-managed fire office m
Australasia. Moderate dividends, a resolution to build
up solid reserves, and careful investment of the funds in
State securities and good bank deposits have done the
rest.

The National Fire and Marine Insurance Co.
(Maoriland):

1899 20,028 124 „ „ 12,255 115,681

1901 24,600 12| .» ». 12,255 141,159
The reserves consist of reserve fund, £90,000 ; re-insur-
ance fund, £36,000 ; ,and undivided profit, £15,159. The
National’s business is growing steadily as well as its pro-
fits, the net premium income having risen from £91,998 in
1898 to £108,120 in 1901. The interest earnings are £9,862
(sufficient in itself to pay a 10 per cent, dividend), repre-
senting £4 Bs. sd. per cent, per annum on the total
investments as at the end of the year, which is good
enough to suggest that the money is carefully and safely
laid out. The National is only the third largest of
Maoriland’s fire and marine insurance institutions, yet
its solidity and progress are a remarkable contrast to
the majority of similar institutions this side of the water.
In Maoriland the people stand by their local offices
as a matter of preference ; in Australia, apparently,
they prefer to do business with any rather than with a
local office. As a result, Maoriland, with its less than
800,000 of population, has the three most solid fire insur-
ance companies in Australasia, while Victoria ancLN.S.W.
take a secondary place, with Tasmania third ; and
Queensland, South Australia and Westralia, with over
1,000,000 inhabitants, don’t possess a single fire office
among them.

The Perpetual Executors and Trustees Associa-
tion of Australia (Melbourne) completed a reasonably
good year on 30th September. This institution is fast
recovering from the severe shock which struck it some
years ago, when it was held to have wrongfully put a
client’s money on deposit in certain banks which came to
grief, and was ordered to take over the deposits and stand
the loss. The decision was always a doubtful one, con-
sidering the terms of the will under which the unlucky
investment was made. Recent doings of the Association
have been as follows :

The reserves consist of contingency account, £7710, and
undivided profit, £1254, and if the former proves to be all
required to provide for losses, then the real reserves are
only £1254. Considering that the co.’s total assets are
only £42,217, and that £ll,lll has been already written off,
£7710 seems a very large provision for contingencies, and
the process of writing off ought to be at least nearing an
end. The earnings are now about equal to 6 per cent, on
capital, but even if things improve it is to be hoped that
the Perpetual Executors and Trustees’ Association won’t
be beguiled beyond 5 per cent dividends until it has
built up a really strong reserve—not less than £lOO,OOO
should be aimed at. Also, it is to be hoped that the new
reserve will be invested in State securities —notin inflated
mortgages and boom banks. Large dividends, small
reserves and undue trustfulness in the investments made
the last trouble, and, though it has been surmounted, the
process was a painful one.

The Swan Brewery Co. is having a good time while Wes-
tralia’s gold boom and borrowing boom lasts. At the
same time, good as things are, it would be well advised
to reduce its 24 per cent, dividend somewhat and keep
an eye on the future. When the borrowing boom gives
oat, the probable slump in W.A. properties—hotel pro-
perties among the rest —is something too great for con-
templation or calculation. The Swan Co., with £76,087
of paid-up capital and £70,646 of reserves, is in a very
solid position, but the Westralian borrowing drunkenness
is, in proportion to population, leagues ahead of anything
Australia has previously witnessed, and when the bottom
falls out of it there is room for a slump compared with
which that of the eastern States in ’93 will be only a
speck on the horizon.

Melbourne Mont de Piite Co. reckons that it

heavy one. The fact that two-thirds of the ooncern were
made away with before the loss was detected suggests a
whole lot of things.

the youngest institution of its kind on this continent,
closed its third year on 30th September, 1901:

The little Institution is making a certain amount of pro-
gress, and has already £1276 of deposits and £3845 paid-
up capital. Probably it will get along faster now that
the preliminary debit is cleared off, but, all the same, the
payment of a 6 per cent, dividend in the very first year of
the institution’s existence looks a risky policy.
Out of the first Federal loan a goodly proportion
is for repairs and patches and other unproductive works,
or works that should assuredly be charged to revenue.
For replacing worn-out telegraph-poles (bought with loan
money and not paid for) £14,000 is allotted ; fortifications,
£9525; new switch-board in Melbourne post-office to
replace the old one that has become obsolete, £30,000;
machinery for Government Printing Office, £12,500 ; and
so on. One more loan swindle, added to the six State
loan swindles we had already.

The Executor, Trustee and Agency Co. of S.A. has a
curious habit of not charging the directors’ and auditors’
fees to the year to which they belong, but to the year
after; the shareholders vote £475, or some such amount,
at the annual meeting, instead of making the itemafixed

total reserves would appear, in each case, as somewhat
less than the amount shown above. A certain amount of
progress is being made, but it can’t be described as any-
thing very extensive up to now. Still, as the co. has
been 22 years in existence, it has apparently. done much
better in the last four years than in the previous 18.

British B.H. ought to advertise for its Western
lode-body. 4000 ft. of diamond-drilling at the 500 and
600 ft. failed to discover it; yet no mining man dares
deny that it is cruising about somewhere not far down
from where the drill pierced. The development at
Howell’s 500 ft. is a valuable one, but the mine wants
something more. One ore-body, unfortunately, doesn’t
make a mine, even if it is 65ft. wide.
* * * *
Cobar Chesney, N.S.W. : At a shareholders’
meeting held in Melbourne last week re the buying
option over which £5OOO deposit has been paid, the
chairman said the optioner was treating large quantities
of ore at a loss, but the cost of smelting was £2 per ton
at Lithgow and 50s. at Cobar. In reply to dissatisfied
shareholders director Malcolm M’Eacharn said the
optioner had a perfect right to test the mine as he
thought fit, that he was not looking for profits in these
tests, but to satisfy people in England that the mine was
worth the money--£lOO,OOO. No director was associated
with Mr. O’Gorman in the opposition although
it had been so rumored, and it was not likely
the mine would be depreciated in value because of the
large quantities of ore shipped a,way. This business is a
bit of a puzzle. In giving the right to take ore for treat-
ment during the option the Board surely could never
have contemplated that thousands of tons would be so
taken. Remarkable, too, that all the ore should have
gone to smelters owned by the Cobar Co.—to Lithgow,
where it would be of value in working-up iron slags be-
cause of its silicious character, and to the Cobar
furnaces where they also have to contend with an
excess of iron. Also, somewhat remarkable that some of
the other smelters did not get a chance to treat this ore ;
and, more remarkable still, that the optioner should send
away thousands of tons and put up with an
average loss of over £1 per ton, The optioner—
Mr. O’Gorman, recollect--is still sending away many
hundreds of tons of 4 per cent, ore, and complacently
putting up with a loss of over £1 a ton—to satisfy his
English people. The situation looks ridiculous.
* * * •
Sydney Harbor Collieries : After four years
weary sinking at enormous cost of one of the finest coal-
shafts in the world—lBft. in diameter, bricked all the way
—Sydney awoke one morning last week to find that coal
had been struck. The D.T. was a day late, the Herald
having got in first, but, as usual, it tried to make up for
its neglect by going much better than any other paper
next day. The thickness of the seam, it was explained,
could not be ascertained for some time, as if a bore
could not have been put through in a few hours, even if
the seam was 20ft. thick. Two days later the woeful
news gently leaked out that this particular seam was
only 2ft. 6in. thick, and it is admitted that “ everything
now depends on striking the main seam.” There is one
unanswered question : Was this small seam discovered in
the Cremorne bore ? If not, the outlook is not too cheerful.
However, the enterprise is a noble one and deserves infi-
nite reward. Should a bigger and better seam be struck
at a not much lower depth, the enterprise and Prof.
David, who has so strongly advocated it, will get full
credit, but if there is no seam there, or if the coal is
wholly or partially burnt, there will be trouble, and cal-
culations will be made as to how much money would have
been saved had these facts been, as they could have been,
established by drill-holes instead of by shaft-sinking. It
is also well to remember that before any extensive mining
for coal can take place another shaft must go down.

* • * •
Lake View Consols: Everything done in this
mine is boomed by newspaper wire infinitely beyond its
merits. The latest —that the main shaft is down over
1000 ft. ; that the north drive at that level is being pushed
ahead with great vigor ; that diamond drills are now in
active service at the lower levels at the mine, and bores
are to be put out in all directions ; that another shaft is
to be sunk, with the object of more effectually working
the northern end of the ore bodies ; that the development
work throughout the mine totals over 1000 ft. per month,
and the show is generally opening up splendidly.
Nothing is ever wrong with L.V.C. ; also the plant is
always in a satisfactory condition; the last 10 heads
“are running capitally, and the remaining 25
should be dropping early in the New Year.” The
fact of the shares falling £3 in a few weeks—a deprecia-
tion of £750,000 —is a mere detail not worth mentioning.
Perhaps if the shares had gone up £3 it would have been
a horse of another color. The consulting engineer has
told the board that the present output of 14,0000 z.
monthly could not be maintained for any length of time,
and that the preparation going forward to increase
the plant to bring it up to a gold production of
18,000 oz. per month was unwarranted either by ore
reserves or the development work going on in the mine.
The mining manager said just the opposite, and asked
the directors to have the mine reported on to see
who was right. Meanwhile the shares have fallen
from £9 to £6, so it is quite easy to see whom
the public believe. A little while ago it was announced
that chairman C. D. Rose and Kaufman were coming to
the mine to set things square ; since then the former has
resigned, and of the new chairman’s proposed visit, it is
said “ if he does come, it is doubtful if he will have the
benefit of any advice from Mr. Hamilton or Mr. Hewitson.”
The inference is either that these men have not been
called upon to make a report, or that they have declined
—the former is the more likely. The market tells the
tale. All the writer desires the public to remember is—
that Lake Views were carried to £2B and back to £ll in
a remarkably short time. Then to £l5 and back to £7
in a few days. A big mine controlled by men who saw
big money in working the public and acted accordingly.
• * • V
Northern Associated : The mine is treating
oxidised ore at the Boulder No. 1 battery, and another
1000 tons of high-grade sulphide ore will soon be sent to
the Fremantle smelter. “It is estimated that the
tonnage of smelting-grade ore in sight in this mine is
equivalent to three years’ supply. This estimate does not
include the immense quantity of 25dwt. to 30dwt. stone
blocked out in the workings or stacked at surface. From
4870 tons of ore already smelted, the co. has made a net
profit of £146,000. The whole of the ore smelted has
been produced solely from development work, not a stope
having as yet been opened in the mine.” Without expla-
nation, this reads remarkably well. Undoubtedly this
is a good mine, but not half so good as the
description implies. The mine has been sending high-
grade ore to Fremantle for the last 14 months, and
has only paid one ss. div. irv that time ; taking these
figures as correct, three years’ supply would only mean
15s. a share, or say £l, if ample allowance were made for
the oxidised ore. The yarn about not a stope having as
yet been opened in the mine we get with sickening
monotony. As a rule, the ore got from development
work in a mine does not represent a tenth of that ob-
tained from stoping, so why is it that this mine has only
three years’ supply ? The strike of the rich run of gold in
the Northern Associated is such that more ore is got by
this so-called development work than will be obtained in
stoping. Newspapermen are not allowed down the mine ;
if they were they would see that in some places the
development work, of which so much is said, consists of
drives 18ft. high, by which method of working most of
the ore is taken bodily away, and they would be im-
pressed with the idea that when all development work is
finished the mine will also be finished. Within the last
two months these shares have fallen from 80s. to 545.,
despite a div. of ss.—a depreciation of upwards of half-a-
million, which fact is, of course, not interesting enough
to be worth mentioning. Some day in the dim future
the S.M. Herald, Melbourne Argus, Sydney D.T., and
other Australian newspapers will, perhaps, realise the
extent to which they have been played with over the
Northern Associated.

SOCIETY

LIQUEUR
BRANDY
Lip-loyalty, the infectious disease that
gave rise to the Diamond Jubilee Infectious
Diseases Hospital at Melbourne, is quite cured for
the present. The hospital, the one sane item
remaining after the hollow frenzy of the
Queen Victoria Jubilee, is represented
by a great, empty, desolate, unfinished
shell of a building at Yarra Bend. Funds are
exhausted, and the Town Hall committee, which
is running the thing much as a snail might run the
Great Pyramid, in waiting for something to turn
up. Nothing turns up but infectious diseases,
however. Ditto the Queen’s National Statue
Fund—£sooo in hand, £lO,OOO wanted—and
nobody oares 2d., bar David Syme, who, having
thought he could whip up the money from the
public, occasionally gnashes his teeth in agony.
There is a sensible road out of the difficulty, and
that is to lump the moneys collected and use them
to complete the Infectious Diseases Hospital.
* * * *
Acting Supreme Court Judge Pring
(N.S.W.) established a record (for an acting
Judge) last week, when on one day he sentenced
two men to death—Kuhnell, the Redfern alleged
poisoner; and Joseph Campbell, the horrible
Queanbeyan sex lunatic. Possibly neither will
hang. There is room for a suspicion of doubt in
Kuhnell’s case, and his supposed victim did not
die. Campbell is palpably a maniac, and should
have been shut up irrevocably for life after his
first offence.
TEA AND COFFEE.
TEACHER OF INFANTS' CLASS: “What letter is this?”
ATENTIVE LITTLES BOY: “ T, Mise! ”
TEACHER : "Right! Now next, what’s this?”
INATTENTIVE BUT SHAKE LITTLE BOY: “ Coffee, Miss!”
Carl Kuhnell, found guilty at Central
Criminal Court, Sydney, of attempted poisoning and
sentenced to death, looked at his trial like a con-
fident plaintiff in a Court of Petty Sessions. His
interest in the proceedings never monopolised His
mind, for at times he appeared quite preoccupied.
He never once nervously twitched his moustache,
betrayed anxiety, showed restlessness,
or exhibited impatience. He wasn’t stolid or
stupid, but alternately alert and languid. In
build, a light-weight athlete ; in feature, regular
and unremarkable, eyes steady, bright, and
confident. Voice hard and clear, without a
tremor. Crown Prosecutor Wade cross-examined
Kuhnell sharply after an ugly bit of testimony,
and the impression that Kuhnell had more than
average intelligence melted away. He revealed
himself shallow and conceited. His answers were
absurdities, and he left the box with the
light step of one who felt and relished a victory.
When the acting-judge passed the death-sentence
Kuhnell went down to the dungeon with a light
laugh. He seemed like one who philosophically
loses a petty-sessions claim. It is unlikely that
theN.S.W. Executive will let him hang ; there
is some room for slight doubt as to his guilt, and
he did not kill anyone.
* * * *
Joseph Campbell, charged with an un-
speakable outrage at Queanbeyan, and the suspect
in the Ramsay’s Bush horror, was present when
Kuhnell was sentenced to death. The culprits
were in strong contrast. Kuhnell confident and
scarcely concerned, smart and well-dressed;
Campbell nervous, unkempt, scared, hopeless, as
pitiful a specimen of mean, miserable animalism as
was ever seen in a dock. His handkerchief was
frequently to his eyes and forehead, and he
couldn’t fix his legs comfortably.
Campbell will possibly be hanged (maniac
though he palpably is) by the Law as a rough-and-
ready way out of the difficulty, which its own
carelessness had caused. A previous conviction
proved him to be a sex maniac, yet, after a term
of imprisonment, he was let loose again, and in
his subsequent crimes the Law was guilty of con-
tributory negligence. People of this sort should
be shut up for ever, not let loose until they so out-
rage public sentiment as to palliate the act of kill-
ing a mentally-irresponsible creature, whose crimes
are the result of maniacal impulse.
• * * *
Acting-Justice Pring, in sentencing Joseph
Campbell to death for that brutal crime, the
Queanbeyan outrage, went out of his way to ap-
prove of capital punishment for such crimes “ina
young country.” The age of the country does not
come into the question. Such crimes are as pre-
valent in London or Berlin as in Queanbeyan or
Ramsay’s Bush. The Bulletin, in fact, much
doubts whether offences of violence against women
and children are proportionately as prevalent in
Australia as in England.

On a Sydney wharf, t’other day, I was watching a steve-
dore selecting men out of a gang of 50 wharf-laborers.
Ten negroes, who were in the gang, were all taken on to
the exclusion of as many capable-looking whites, one of
whom told me that niggers are favored by most steve-
dores. Surely, in all this legislation to ensure a White
Australia, there should be some provision to enforce the
preference of white over black labor-other things being
equal.

* * * *
A lucrative study in Australia just now is
the Art of How to Escape Stieking-up. Hardly
a day passes without a case of robbery with vio-
lence being reported from somewhere (generally
m a populous suburb), and, rightly or wrongly,
people begin to propose importing a'few Boers for
instructive purposes. A spiked belt round the
back, just where the garrotter plants his knee, is a
useful thing, and a bull-pup for a walking com-
panion is also conducive to safety. When stroll-
ing out to see the silvery moon, one has to avoid
the man that comes swinging up from behind,
and when two or three are gathered together it is
high time to get the sand-bag cleared for action.
I he recent sticking-up case at Melbourne’s
most fashionable suburb, Toorak, inspired a Vic.
law-maker to tell of an experience of his own, to
the truth of which he swears. The legislator was
going home late one night, carrying the usual
tempting black bag, when lie was waylaid in a
quiet place by a man who wanted his money or
his life. “I had a passion for both,” said the
legislator, “and instead of parting I dashed
my bag in his face, and we closed. I know
something of wrestling as it was practised at
Newcastle in my early days, and I gave my man
a fall that shook all the villainy out of him. While
I sat astride his binge.y he pleaded with me
piteously, said he was weeks behind with his rent,
and was only trying to get the money together to
save his wife being turned into the street.” “And
you let him go?” said a newspaper man, in amaze-
ment. “ Well, yes,” said the M.P. j “you see, I
was his landlord ! ”

An item which S.M. Herald did not re-
port in the debate on the disfigurement of the
harbor frontages. E. M. Clark, speaking on the

Go to Milson’s Point, .and you will see a splendid wall
there, three storeys high, painted blue, and on that blue
ground you will discern Che words “ Read the Sydney
Morning Herald, one penny.” That is overlooking the
harbor. Who is the owner of that wall ? It is the

for advertising purposes.”
* * * *
Many Vic. churches were grievously
damaged by the hailstone cyclone that struck that
State the other week, but only one pub. sustained
any serious injury. The Devil’s activity in look-
ing after his own is a good example too much neg-
lected by the higher powers. A R.O. preacher
invites attention to the fact that all the churches
overthrown by the gale were Prodistan. The
stability of the average R.C. church building is
certainly a monumental proof of the R.C. opinion
that Omnipotence is liable to be rather careless in
its handling of the weather.

reached someone remembered that two children
from the State Children’s Relief Department were
handy. They were watched, and soon detected
It is generally a State Relief Dept. kid. Is mys-
terious stone-throwing part of the curriculum in
State institutions for children ?

tunate two-year-old child was savagely worried by

suburbs.

There were six, not five, supers in the
Melb. “ Theatre Royal Syndicate ” which bought
one ss. ticket in a Tatt.’s Melb. Cup sweep
—and drew Revenue. Each man’s share was Is.,
including the cost of stamps and registration.
And, of course, there was the usual tragedy of ill-
luck connected with that ticket. One of the
original six supers who plotted the joint invest-
ment happened to be in an unfinancial state
when the day came for parting up his “bob.”
Not having the shilling in Fiis pocket, he turned to
a companion and suggested that he should take
the sixth chance. So the other took the twelve
pen’orth of speculation and won £llOO.

An Adelaide alderman, discussing Mayor
Kissy Ware, C.M.G., and his alleged disinclina-
tion to “shout ” up to the proper limit:
Though he was not born to be a chairman, thev could
overlook his failings in that respect, but they were
entitled to protest against the miserable and niggardly
way in which he had filled his position.
In a word, Incompetence in a Mayor doesn’t
matter; but, from the aldermanic standpoint,
any reluctance to fill ’em up again freely and
frequently is a capital crime.
» * * *
Australia has seen much of the man
tramping around the world “ for a wager; ” now
it has got the wager-man who tramps per ocean.
The voyagers (now at Sydney) per the boat
Tilikum, who are “striving to beat Capt.
Slocum’s record,” are. not likely to arouse
any wild enthusiasm. Slocum’s ' craft Spray
was a 13-tonner, the Tilikum has a tonnage
of only 4; but what chiefly made Slocum’s
voyage wonderful was that he took his trip alone,
whilst the Tilikum has a crew of two.

ness of the Manly ferry-hands in pushing their

impossible for her to use the ancient gag that
“ the time is inopportune for a strike.”

generous employer and adverse to the “defiant”

advertisers would allow the daily press to give
much latitude to the strikers, or to arouse much
public sympathy on their behalf.

It does not often happen, as in a N.S.W.
divorce case, last week, that a mother forgets the
number of her “interesting events.” “How
many children have you had ? ” asked the Judge
of petitioner. Witness replied that there were so
many she could not tell the number. “Do you
mean to say you do not know?” “There were
several premature—” said witness. “ Never mind
them.” “Well, about 19,” stammered witness,
after consideration.

“N.E.C.” : On that liquor argument
Editor Bulletin. — l don’t think “E.F.,” who writes
from Melbourne, Percy Malby, and Robt. H. Lemon, sec.
Vic. L.V.A., have quite finished their law-and-liquor edu-
cation. I know a young woman behind a Sydney bar
who, though she doesn’t profess to have passed all her
exams., can settle the spurious-spirit argument without
the aid of f of a Bulletin column. She recently served
three young men for three hours with alleged whisky,
and when the time came for the publican to balance up,
he worked it out' that the damsel had made 18s. profit on
the bottle. She told me this herself, and on several occa-
sions I have known her to speak the truth. Didn’t ask
her how she performed this wonderful commercial feat,
but I heard one of the young men remark next morning
that “ the three of us drank about seven bottles of
whisky last night, and it must have been good stuff,
because none of us were drunk ! ”
* • * #
In a Sydney smoke-nuisance prosecution,
the other day, a photo, of the factory-stack sending
forth black smoke was accepted as evidence for
the prosecution. Apart from the fact that no
photographer would dream of accepting such
evidence as conclusive of the blackness of the
smoke, it is by no means clear that smoke that is
not black is not a nuisance. A Sydney tobacco-
factory’s municipally impeccable smoke-stack, for
instance, fills the air with strong tobacco reek
over half-a-mile radius. This may be a good
disinfectant, but folks with heart-trouble, com-
pelled to dwell in the vicinity, are urged to
commit suicide.

A REAL WOMAN.
TRAM-CONDUOTOR : “ Where to, Miss?” MISS: “ Eh!”
CONDUCTOR: “ Where to?” MISS: “Oh!—to my sister’s to lunch.”
The late watchman at a big city lodging-
for males only tells a woeful story of mis-
placed confidence. Shortly aftermidnight, recently,
he was interviewed by an agitated young thing,
carrying two parcels, and having the ingenuous
air of the daughter of a clergyman who
has mislaid her address. She had also a
sympathetic mouth and a kindly eye. This
interesting little lady had missed her last
train, and wanted a bed for the night; but, as a
malignant fate would have it, she had spent her
last sixpence, and didn’t know whatever would
become of her. The watchman was touched, but
explained that she could not possibly be accom-
modated there, as the establishment was for men
only, and was conducted on strictly Presbyterian
cash principles. At this the lone, lorn
damsel gave way to floods of tears, and moaned
about pa and ma and Aunt Jane, and
made distracting references to the bed of the
Yarra. Her sufferings so worked upon the sus-
ceptible guardian that, in a fit of emotion, he
offered the poor creature the use of his own room,
which he would not need till 6 o’clock in the
morning, stipulating only that the unhappy young
lady should up and off, bright and early, before
the Scotchbyterian authorities were afoot'. So it
was arranged, and the lady fled shortly after
dawn. The watchman began to miss his portable
valuables later. His pockets had been cleaned
out, and his chest-of-drawers completely gutted,
and he regarded himself as cut off from the con-
solations of complaint by his invidious position,
until sternly called upon by a censorious board of
Scotch extraction and Calvinistic morals to ex-
plain the presence of a “young person” in his
room at 5 o’clock “ i’ th’ morn.” Presumably the
board had been at the keyhole. The ex-watchman
is now looking out for a new boss—a Pagan.

“ Lex ” to The Bulletin :—
Melbourne University graduates teaching in Victorian
secondary schools are wishing that these institutions
could be brought under the Factories Act. Writer was
recently offered a senior non-resident mastership in a
much-advertised school at a salary of £100 per annum,
and when, in a flourishing country grammar school con-
ducted by joint principals—one of whom is a parson- a
temporary vacancy occurred through the illness of one of
the principals, this position, with three nights per w 7 eek
duty added, was offered at a salary of £2 per week ! Also,
the way of the private teacher is made hard by the
connivance of the Education Dept, at the violation of the
Public Service Act’s provision that State-paid employees
shall not take outside work. State school teachers are
permitted to undertake private tuition, and, though the
Education Minister has explained that this permission is
conditional on the tuition being confined to pupil teachers
preparing for departmental examinations, the condition
is consistently ignored. In an} 7 case, it is hard to see why
the dispensation should be granted, seeing that the duty
of preparing pupil-teachers for exams, is imposed by law
on the head-teacher of the school in which the p.t. is
employed. Not long ago a post-office employee w 7 as told
that he would be “ sacked ” if his wife continued to hold
an hotel license. But then “ Bung” is a political force,
and the poor skull-filler doesn’t count 1
The Bulletin is sure that Colonial Secre-
tary See has only to learn that rubber-tyres are
sadly needed on the Little Bay Hospital ambu-
lances to have them ordered at once. It is a long
journey from Sydney to Little Bay, and the
people who have to face it in an ambulance are
dften in dreadful pain all the way.

A letter from Private (name given),
calmly published by a Maitland (N.S.W.) paper:
At Belmont the Boers buried their dead by throwing a
few stones on their bodies, leaving legs and arms exposed.
Some of our chaps put bits of biscuit in their mouths,
pinned the British colors on them, cut their fingers off if
they had a ring worth while ; and one chap having a bad
pair of boots came across a dead Boer with a good pair,
and quietly exchanged.
And Bennett Burleigh (cable, Nov. 17) talks about
“ the callous robbery of British dead by Boers ” !

Ed. Bulletin. —l should like to see you
use your influence to kill the racial racket in Aus-
tralia. Eschew the words English, Scotch, Irish,

“Kobbi,” re Queensland Jlarly Closing
Act: —
Though shopkeepers are kept well up to the mark,
there is no restriction upon the Assyrian hawker, or if
there is, he ignores it, and sells when he pleases, Sundays
included. These fellows prosper exceedingly—thanks to
the big grab-all shopkeepers, who would rather see these
dusky devils get the cash (to send out of the country)
than the widow-shopkeeper who depends for her living
on what business she can do after the big shops are
closed. One Assyrian in C.Q. before the Bill was passed
was a common hawker—with a pack. Afterwards, he
bought a horse, followed this up with a horse and sulky,
and now he drives four good horses. And most of his
cash came from goods sold on Saturday afternoons —when
the farmers, &c., cannot buy from anyone hut the foreign
hawker.
[This doesn’t mean that the E.C. Act should be
abolished pit only shows the necessity for Barton’s
Alien Exclusion Bill and the suppression of the
alien hawker.—Ed. B.]

Editor Bulletin. —It isn’t absurd (“An-
swers to Correspondents,” 23/11/’01) to fight the
forewoman question out with Sam. Hordern.
Every other forewoman is in the union. Let
Jlordern win on his case, and the thin end of the
f wedge goes into the union. Other forewomen will
rat, and hands will follow them, and the girls in
the union who understand this know they are
fighting for the life of their union; not merely
against Hordern.—X.O.
[The question hinges on the definition of “ forewoman.”
If the “forewoman” is a sort of deputy boss, a sub-
manager over a factory, it seems to The Bulletin she has
the right to stay outside the Union because her position
implies thaSt she is to look after her employer’s interests
primarily in keeping employees up to the mark, and
so on. To the forewoman, who is just an employee of a
slightly higher grade, the argument does not apply, and
there should not be allowed more than one non-Union
forewoman to each factory or large division of a factory.
Ed. B.J

CONFESSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hush ! I’m writing my soul to you !
Turn the page, and my heart beats through
Publish abroad in black and white
I love, love, love my love, to-night!
P’r’aps you’ll read in a Sydney street,
’Mid the bustle of noisy feet,
Where the trams with clang and light
Leap to the swallowing gulf of night.
Or you’re back in the old Domain
Under shade of the figs again ;
Every time that a ’Bay fig drops
’Member we kissed ? —“ to mark the stops.”
Or you watch where the harbor dreams :
“ How that ferryboat twinks and gleams ! ”
“ Grand old ships . . . Hear the sailor’s call 1 ”
“ Last boat’s gone—Good-night! ” —that’s all.
Hush l I’m writing my soul to you !
Turn the page and my heart beats through :
Publish abroad in black and white
1 love l love l love my love, to-night.

THE YOUNG 'UN.
THE WEARINESS OF NATURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How very tired the Sun must be of shining !
The earth of turning must be very weary;
The wind sometimes must find itself inclining
To cease from blowing, feeling none too cheery.
The tide must tire of daily ebb and flowing ;
’Tis lassitude, I fear we must consent,
That makes a tree sometimes to leave off growing,
That makes a star drop from the firmament.
O priest! you understand not your own preaching ,
Heaven is in truth'nought but eternal peace ;
Your dreaded hell is but a vain beseeching
That work shall at some far last moment cease.
I cannot think suns shine, earths keep revolving,
Winds ever blowing, with eternal zest;
I do not want to see all things dissolving,
But they would profit by a little rest.
THE BILLY.
I WISH...
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wish I had died
That day when we rode with our bridles free,
And never a shadow ’twixt you and me ;
When the earth was so glad and the sky so blue,
And you kissed me hotly and I kissed you,
I wish I had died.
To-day I can see
The great brown plain advancing to meet the sky,
And the plovers flit past with a whirring cry,—
The tank and the crows with their croaking loud,
The sheep that moved like an earth-born cloud,
The birrah and box and the brigalow trees,
The coliarene scenting the summer breeze, —
The earth was so bright and the sky so blue
And the world held only us two—us two .. .
I wish I had died.
. N.S.W.
NELLIE M. FIELD.

Criterion Hieatre (Sydney) has been orow'ded
this week by persons who just love the music- of
Aubers romantic opera, “Fra Diavolo ” and by
people who think Mr. Henry Hallam a perfect

crawl into bed on her hands and knees, feminine-

goes to sleep under strong lime-light, and sings in
her sleep like a drowsy bird.
There was a picturesque moonlight fete at New-
ington College (Sydney) last week. After the
last notes of the concert had melted into the mani-
fold sounds of the night, the sham-fight com-
menced It was a glorious night. Against the
starred dome, the distant hill about to be assaulted
silhouetted blackly. Around, in the shadows
below, there were tiny camp-fires. A bugle
wailed. A searchlight darted round. A sudden
burst of rifle shots. Then crashing volleys.
Tongues of waving fire ran up and dowm parallel
lines in the darkness. More lights flashed round.
A tremendous roar from the hill—torrents of
sparks, wild cheering, and the ambulance corps
went flitting about, and there was a spirit of
desperate effort abroad till the bugles wailed some
more, and the rifle rattle died away and was lost
in the strains of Gorsave.

The image of Buddha, part of the loot “ ac-
quired by purchase and otherwise,” by Mr.
v\ ynne, and lent to the Society of Artists’
show, shouldn’t be passed by as a mere Joss.
A silver robe half-clothes the golden body; the
hands are clasped, with index fingers slightly
raised. It sits “as holy statues sit” in “The
Bight of Asia ” cross-legged, soles of feet up-
turned. The face is of strange charm. It has
an expression calm, benignant, passionless, with
a trace of a mysterious smile. The god whom
that image represents ought to be a very decent
reflow. 1 can imagine that the smile came after
he had been “acquired ” by Mr. Wynne.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The deep thinkers of Sassiety and the in-
fluential congregated at ‘Cliveden’ on Thursday
to discuss the necessity of being in the Conti-
nental swing with a branch of the ‘lnternational
Council of Women. Lady Braddon and Mrs.
t-Ai SO t —heads of the Association in the
J-ignt Little Island—were billed to propound the
7i! e 'u S of and to let Melb. aspirants reap
tfle benefit of their experience and superior know-
ledge. A very rustling, fussy gathering circled
about a business-like table in the drawing-room,
and collected the pearls of light and learning
dropped by the two Tasrnaniennes. Lady Brad-
don, nervously, and in very shaky tones, set forth
j * 16 Council after the gorgeously-
attired Dowager had run up the curtain and de-
livered the prelude. The speaker’s cheeks grew
pink as the roses in her bonnet as she read her care-
fully-compiled notes to the assemblage. A muffled
clapping of gloved hands followed her full stop.
Ihen Mrs. Dobson, the Senator’s wife, s':ood up,
and calmly, stolidly, elucidated the workings and
operations of the Council. Practice at taking the
floor has made Mrs. Dobson a delightfully lucid
orator, bhe enunciates clearly, dramatises her
sentiment without any theatrical effect, and takes
honest delight in steering cleverly and adroitly
through the shoals of speech. Not many Senators
or public spouters could orate with the lucidity
and precision of this lady. There are no
fascinating ripples in her stream of talk, no play
of sunny wit, but cold, clear wisdom shines on
the surface of her utterance. She convinced
nearly all present of the desirability of joining the
The alleged business of the Association seems
to be promoting the happiness and general good
of human kind in a literary and controversial way
—a way eminently conducive to both egotism and
altruism. You discuss, debate, and write up in
pungent style, the troublesome grievances of your
human kith and kin. The papers are read at the
International Congress to the World of Thought
and much personal glory is attained thereby.
1 here is good in it of a certainty, and a beneficial
employment for those women of leisure who are
given to occasional ideas or charitable impulses.
Among the mildly excited and generally interested
assemblage Mrs. Claude Macdonald (at present
domiciled at “ Cliveden ”) was the most fluffy and
dressed personage. She wore a cream voile with
a satin-jacket scrolled in Honiton braid and black
chenille, and the skirt ornamented with musical
staves. An immense ruff and a sparkling jet
toque helped the effect.

Kitty Grindlay, one of the few non-brunette

“ The certainty with which fish swarm into
Hobson s Bay whenever a meat-famine descends
upon Melbourne is enough to convert the most
ardent church-goer into a belief in Divine Provi-
dence. For weeks the bay has simply been thick
with barracouta, flathead, and pilchards, so thick
that Melbourne can be recognised miles off by its
fishy smell, wind and weather permitting, and
two-thirds of the inhabitants of its suburbs spend
their leisure plucking ’couta out of the waters,
and towing long, clammy masses of that vicious
fish home for breakfast.
“Even our sex here has taken giddily to fishing,
and in the ‘ swaggah ’ seaside suburbs, Middle
Park and St. Kilda, ‘sassiety’ fishing parties
have been quite the thing for a fortnight past.
Dudes, dudelets, and dudines, appropriately
garbed, have gathered and giggled on the piers,
whilst hooking fish and covering themselves with
scales, ooze, and piscatory odors. The ’couta have
been so thick that they could be dipped up in
buckets, and they are so voracious that they worry
the fair hand that succors them from the cruel
hook.
“ A recent bazaar, that scooped in some thou-
sands, was rather rich in its supply of quarrels
among the stall-holders. The bickerings in several
cases reached an alarming stage, and hard words
were used. Some were charged with pricing an
article at an exorbitant sum, and, when no buyer
was found, knocking it down at a trifle to them-
selves. One lady, accused of this offence, was
also charged with sending in an excessive account
for her expenses.
“A sporting dandy respectfully solicits all and
sundry to withdraw, cancel, or contradict the
report of his engagement to the handsome and
dressy daughter of a Melb. printer. The report
had got into many society papers.
“Toorak is talking of the Forbes affair, and the
news of the lady’s millinery venture in the Big
Smoke. Captain Forbes was an amiable member
of the former Hopetoun suite, and married pretty
Miss Maude Cockburn, daughter of Mrs. Young-
husband, of Oakleigh Hall, out Windsor way.
Miss Cockburn was a petted but very sweet
specimen of Victorian beauty in the Lady Teazle
style. The wedding was a colossal affair—an un-
usually smart one—and the collection of presents
quite eclipsed any Collins-st. jewellery show. So
Captain Forbes and his pretty bride went off on
their trip to Europe under brilliant auspices.
Since then there have been reverses and untoward
happenings, and now the lady is in the hat busi-
ness in London under the title of ‘Madame
Kinska.’ Good luck to her —and to every other
plucky woman !
“This week (Wednesday), the chubby little
daughter of Colonel Tom Price is to be married
to Mr. Ainslie Mills —a long, lean, bronze chappie
from your side. Next month (17th), the swagger
daughter of Mr. J. Pratt will be hitched up to a
returned warrior, Mr. Lindsay Inglis. This is to
be a slap-up smart knot-tying, although the domes-
tic hearth is to be the locale of the ceremony.
“In Essingdon they do things seldom, but they
do these few well. An ‘ old maids’ ’ dance was
the last freak. The self-styled back-numbers put
out their invitations in the form of an entreaty
somewhat in this fashion : ‘ We’re going to have
a dance. Will you come ? Ah, do ! ’ The majority
of males did come, and had a good time of
it. Many novelties were sprung upon them. One
was styled the ‘Noah’s Ark Dance.’ For this, all
the men were packed out of the room, and the
girls went out for them, one after another, and
marched in like the animals of the old rhyme,
two-by-two. Another novelty was a card dance.
For this, the cards (two packs) were shuffled, and
the men dealt out one lot and the ‘ old maids ’ the
other. Then the couples whose cards corresponded
danced together. And the spinsters rejoiced
exceedingly.
“ At a recent gathering a smart dame wore an
extra large medallion from her chain, and this
enclosed two chubby children’s heads. Bairnless
mithers find themselves nowadays out in the cold.
To be in the fashion, they have to borrow
portraits of farmed-out babies and sport them as
a god-child, or a God-send, or a protege.
“Recent events recall a story. Something had
hopelessly smashed in the engine-room, and the
ship was straying helplessly on the face of the
yeasty sea. The passengers were all in a pale blue
funk, everyone expecting each moment to be his
last, but- nearly all made a sickly show of bearing
up brightly, and the men wore a dejected bilious
grin to reassure the ladies. The most abject
collapse took the female half of a picturesque
partnership in the early proceedings. The little
lady gave way to an agony of terror, and
spent two days in a condition that made her an
object of pity to all beholders. Then a party of
matrons took it upon themselves to try and talk
the sufferer into a more rational and courageous
frame of mind. ‘Why give way?’ said the
spokeswoman. ‘ Nothing really serious may
happen, after all. Look at us—we do not despair.’
‘No,’ gurgled the stricken one, ‘ but—but it’s
quite different. It isn’t your fault. You aren’t
running away with somebody else’s husband !
Boo —hoo—hoo ! ’ Then, as luck would have it, a
rescue was effected within the hour.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
