PERSONAL ITEMS.
“Charlie” Yarley, an Adelaide lawyer,
who was “ admitted ” to the N.S.W. Bar the other
day, is a double of B. R. Wise—in personal
appearance.
Seddon, an inveterate smoker for years,
has discontinued the habit. There are other indi-
cations that the M.L. Premier is losing his old
nerve. He now weighs 20st.
Premier John See declares he can go
through all his business throughout the year
without swearing at an employee. John uses
worse arguments than swear-words.
Premier Jenkins is already declaring that
if he goes to the King’s coronation next year he
will not accept a knighthood. He was born a
U.S. citizen, and, though naturalised as a Briton,
retains his republican instincts.
John Forrest is still actual Premier of
Westralia. He did not work harder in the
formation of any of his Governments than he did
by letter-writing and wiring in the crisis which
has led up to the Morgans Ministry.
H. C. Dangar, M.L.C., recalling the fact
that payment of Assembly members passed
N.S.W. Leg. Council by one vote only—that of
John Davies —avows now that but for an accident
he would have attended and blocked the measure.
The desire of little S.A. Chief Justice
Way to be top-sawyer in everything has induced
him to accept the post of circuit steward in a
Methodist church. He has more offices of varied
kinds than any other person in the Commoft-
wealth.
The death of Mr. Charles Potter leaves
one N.S.W. ex-Government printer still living,
Mr. William Hanson, who held the office away in
the fifties, and who is now nearing his 90th year.
He was once partner in The Empire with the late
Samuel Bennett.
Touching Australian artists abroad. A.
H. Fullwood writes from London, where he seems
to have got a foothold : ‘ ‘ Saw Minns a few
days ago, and was rejoiced to find him looking
ever so much better and stronger. He was going
to winter in Brittany.”
B. R. Wise has, it is said, just completed
sufficient sales of his “Industrial Freedom” to
cover expenses of production. It has taken 10
years to achieve this, but economic works travel
up-hill to the British brain. The sale of the work
in Australia was almost nil.
George Thornton, who died last week at
82 years, prided himself on having held lighted
candles to the printer who worked-off the first
number of what is now the S. M. Herald. The
old Herald office was in Lower George-street,
very close to the present Bulletin office.
J. C. Davison, proprietor of Carcoar
(N.S.W.) Chronicle, is dead. He was for years
in the service of printer John Sands, of Sydney.
His uncle was the Simpson Davison who flourished
in the early gold-days, and wrote the one time
popular work “Gold Discoveries in Australia.”
Of the beginnings of Deakin. In 1878, a
deputation of West Bourke (Vic.) electors waited
upon David Syme, to ask him to contest that Con-
servative stronghold. The Dictator declined, but
“he had a young man in his office who would
make a suitable candidate.” He then introduced
Alfred to the deputation ; he stood, and was de-
feated by only 18 votes.
AT THE N.S.W. ARTISTS' EXHIBITION.—
EXPLANATORY.
MR. CAMELHAIR (the artist): “Rather a good
subject. When the show’s over, and I have
it in the studio again, Til paint it up and
finish it. Ought to make a neat thing.”
King Seddon is rapidly losing the gift
of keeping his temper. In replying to an
M.P. lately he sneered at him as a Sunday-school
teacher. Another M.P., an ex-parson, he referred
to as a “ Bible-thumper. ” Both turned the other
cheek by reminding Seddon that he was an ex-
publican.
If N.S.W. loses Railway-Commissioner
Kirkcaldie to Victoria, as seems probable, she
will lose the most practical of her triumvirate of
railway-bosses. In fact, Mr. Kirkcaldie is the
only trained railway-man of the three. It is now
just about Secretary M‘Laohlan’s turn for a Com-
missionership.
South Australia pays its Chief Justice as
much as the Chief Justice of the U.S. and £4OO a
year more than the Chief Justice of Canada.
There is an agitation to reduce the number of
Supreme Court Judges in S.A. from three to two,
and future occupants of the position will probably
receive less pay.
In reply to Major Rupert Carrington’s
wail from S’Africa for 150 more men for “my
regiment,” Mr. J. S. Horsfall, of Widgiewa
station, has offered £lOOO towards expenses. The
Bulletin does not blame Mr. Horsfall. The
thing is a family matter : The Majah is Mr. Hors-
fall’s son-in-law.
“ Banjo ” Paterson writes to S.M.
Herald from Chefoo, China, that he was not
allowed to travel on the Siberian railway. Route
was blocked, and miles and miles of the road had
given way, the Russian Consul explained. Yet
the Czar has just been boasting of the line as a
completed work.
William M'Millan, fiery, Radical William,
as acting-leader of the Opposition, promised to
the Barton Government last week that he would
give no quarter, would fight them step by step,
and force them to the country. Since John Norton
vacated his throne, curious what a number of
people aspire to fill it !
George Reid has been trotting round
Groom, the Vic. M.H.R., and exhibiting him at
meetings as his proselyte. George is naturally
very proud of his only convert, and, after boasting
of him, says: “Step forward, Mr. Groom, and
show yourself,” and Mr. Groom steps forward like
the Wild Man from Borneo.
Despite all the assurances about the excel-
lent state of the King’s health, it costs £lO 10s.
per cent, to insure his life for one year now. A
large number of policies have been taken out by
people who will lose heavily if the coronation
doesn’t happen, and the rate shows that London
doesn’t regard H. M. as a very good risk.
The advent of Governor Clarke will pro-
bably avert an open rupture between Waxellency
Madden and the Peacock. Madden is much cut
up over recent events, particularly his failure to
get the permanent vice-regal billet. One mark of
the trouble is that Peacock has of late left Execu-
tive meetings entirely to Sam Gillott and the
beautifully-tailored Gurr.
Hector Macdonald need not be more of a
snob than most human beings are to resent the
wearisome reiteration of the fact that he rose
from being a “common soldier.” Granted that
he has reason to be proud of the fact, there is no
reason why it should be “chucked” at him 100
times a day by pompous politicians, and cordial
mayors, and patronising newspapers.
Melbourne pressmen, accustomed to ap-
proach with freedom State politicians, growl
fiercely at having to dance attendance on Federal
top-notchers. As a rule it is impossible to get
hold of Premier Barton and certain other Ministers
until past midnight, and frequently the reporters
have to cool their heels on the doormat for hours
while the eminent ones are genially supping.
Chief-Justice Darley referred to ex-solici-
tor Rofe in court, the other day, always as a
“gentleman.” When he became a solicitor, Mr.
Rofe was a gentleman by Act of Parliament.
When convicted of conspiracy, he ceased to be a
solicitor, but evidently remained in the judicial
eye a gentleman. Perhaps what an Act of Parlia-
ment has done, only an Act of Parliament can
undo. .
Gov. Ranfurly tells St. James’ Gazette
that the royal visit to Australia has been of
inestimable value. It has, indeed. It has de-
monstrated to Australians how un-royal and
commonplace a thing Royalty is. Once upon a
time, the title “Prince of Wales’’might have
been conjured with here. What does the average
Australian who has seen the Duke of York think
of it now ?
How many mournful wrecks does one meet
in Sydney —artistic, poetic, jour-
nalistic wrecks, who hold as a
dear possession a tattered pass-
port to fame endorsed “ \.\ B.
Dailey ! ” Almost anybody who
wrote verses or daubed in
colors, and sent a sample of his
wares to Dailey, got a letter in
reply enrolling him among the
famous and inviting him to a
little fish dinner. This beauti-
ful and well-meant blarney was
the cause of spoiling many a
good navvy or stonemason by
sending him straying among the
bogs that encircle Parnassus.
A man can be careless, if he has
a good right arm, as to whom
he abuses. He should be deadly
particular as to whom he
praises. One really good-
natured, over-appreciative soul
like Dailey can do more harm
in the world than a dozen
assassins.
Prime Minister Barton :
The Commonwealth has the absolute right to enforce
the deportation clauses of the Kanaka Bill, and 1 await
with confidence the decision of the High Court, when
constituted, on the subject.
If Mr. Barton were going shortly to take the
Chief-Justiceship, would he make announcements
of that sort ?
The Australian betting-ring is losing sub-
stance every year. The death of fat Alf. Joseph
took £30,000 out of the peezness, and now Jack
Cohen has withdrawn his “ nice dry bit.” The
sad-eyed man, who used to yell the odds with one
hand placed against his ear, as though he were
trying to catch the echo, has gone to settle down
in England, whence he came. Are the punters
listening with their hands against their ears? The
tote wouldn’t take any money out of the country,
anyhow.
Several most respectable Australian papers
are quoting The Graphic to the effect that His
Majesty the King, the Duke of York, the Duke
of Saxe Coburg and Gotha, and other royalties
are fond of wearing bangles and bracelets. This
love of bracelets, bangles, and even ankle rings, is
becoming quite common amongst men in high
life, it seems, but the jewellery is usually hidden
under the clothes. This weakness in men is ac-
counted by psychologists one of the strongest evi-
dences of degeneracy.

There exists, Mr. Speaker, a wavy line of thought in
the atmosphere, and the Freetraders mount it, Mr.
Speaker, as a hobby-horse ; but it is one-legged and
cannot budge an inch, Mr. Speaker, and it becomes a
science which needs no verification, and they waltz round
it, Mr. Speaker, and sing hymns of satisfaction, but its
pulse is feeble, Mr. Speaker," and we are going to blow it
out to-night.
Sydney D.T. devoted a first leader last
week to calling on G. H. Reid to arise and save the
Freetrade cause at peril of his living. If G.H.R.
is-wise, instead of being sooled on to monetary
perdition, he will hold tight to his briefs, and
give only his spare time to politics. It seems that
the Freetrade cause depends on the comic states-
man for its very existence. Let it pay for its prop,
as it can well afford to, and make at least one
exception to its common rule of sweating.
Judging by the following speech, delivered
in Maoriland, Hector Macdonald seems to have
been considerably impressed by what he saw in
S’Africa: —
May the dav never come when this beautiful country is
overrun by a "foe, your homes in flames, your women and
children, with anguish in their hearts and despair in
their souls, fleeing to the mountains, homeless wan-
derers, weary and hungry and hopeless, while their pet
possessions, ‘and treasures of childhood and youth, are
trod under foot by ruthless invaders—
In a word, by Australians who boil their billies
with your piano.
The brains of the new W A. Ministry mostly
centre in Attorney-General Moorhead and Lands-
Minister Nanson (assistant-editor of tire Wes-
TRALI.an). Morgans, the Premier, is member for
Coolgardie, and Lord High Keeper of the Eng-
lish mines and “cats” of the Northern fields.
When he visits that dreadful city of the dead wild-
cat—Coolgardie—he is always followed by a pro-
cession of dead-beat cadgers, principally of the
English Johnny sort. Morgans always parts, for,
like his “specs,” his heart is golden. A shrewd
business man of the London city type, Morgans is
no politician ; but in the mixed state of affairs
now existing, may retain office for some time.
“Ah, weel ” —imagine a figure about four

Mr. Coleman Phillips, of note in Maori-
land as a rabbit-destroyer, recently laid before
N.S.W. Govt, a scheme for the repression of
bunny. Details are a secret, but the method is
described as a “natural system of suppression,”
and is to be supplemented by the fostering of
diseases native to the rabbit—such as liver-rot,
bladder-worm, etc. Local rabbit-“ experts,” how-
ever, are pointing out that in the dry N.S.W.
climate these diseases are practically non-existent.
Maoriland has made the pleasing discovery
that, following the policy of expansion, she has
become the possessor of a leper-station —the small
island of Penrhyn. A naval medical officer, who
lately inspected the lepers there, declared the
place to be in a most discreditable state. One
leper boy was being attended by his mother,
herself not then a leper. Govt, proposes to send
down a medical officer every three months.
Argus quotes with great satisfaction from
a London Times item re the war :
We carry on a war and treat our enemies as though we
were at peace with them. We take care of their families,
so that they can fight us without any anxiety as to their
wives and children.
Considering several things, particularly the death-
rate amongst those “wives and children,” the
bland stupidity of that quote is monumental.
The vulgar Boer can fight in peace,
To wife and babes he gives no heed :
Quite well he knows that Bull agrees
To give them all the graves they need.
“Veteran” to The Bulletin
That military “potentate” in Sydney (B. 16/11/’01), who
made the astounding discovery from the “ Army List ’’
that “this man Macdonald” has “ only the regimental
standing of a colonel,” is a truly laughable “soldier.”
No British commander, whatever his other rank, can
possibly have a higher regimental standing than colonel,
and a good many of them are onty lieutenant-colonels.
There have been generals who were, regimentally, only
captains. Further, it is open to question whether any
officer of the British army, outside artillery and engineers,
can have even the “ regimental standing of a colonel.”
If the “ Potentate ” would study his “ Army List ” a little
more he would probably find that all the regimental
colonels are old generals, and that where lieu t.-cols, are
styled “ colonels,” it is only “ army ” rank, and has no re-
lation to “ regimental standing.” Indeed, this question
Of “ regimental ” and “ army ” rank is full of traps for the
unwary student of the “ Army-List ” who has never done
duty in a big garrison, where he would occasionally run
up against a touchy senior, and be hauled-up for failing
in some small observance due to said senior.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —In the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
The latest confidence-trick exposed at
Sydney Sessions would make a cat laugh. An
elderly farmer who had come to Sydney on holiday
made the chance acquaintance of the usual young
man, and, of course, it was not long ere they met
friends, who were casually introduced. Perhaps
Wayback’s funds were not sufficient to make him
a beneficiary of the “ rich Fiji uncle ” —or possibly
he knew that trick—but oneof the friends casually
remarked to the farmer’s young friend, “ What
are you going to do with that?” The friend
replied that if he could not sell it he would pro-
bably make “it” a present to the Art Gallery. The
farmer next day was informed that “it” was a
sun-dial which his friend had been exhibiting in
the Botanical Gardens. It was on exhibit by
agreement for a period of which there was still
a week to run, and at the end of that time,
failing a purchaser, he, as owner, would present it
to the Art Gallery. But he casually suggested
that it would be a very handy thing for the
farmer to possess. A visit was paid to the sun-
dial in the Botanical Gardens, and the farmer was
so satisfied with the article that he agreed to give
for it £3 —£2 down, and the balance in a week,
when he was to take delivery. A week later,
the employees of the Gardens were surprised by
the appearance of an elderly man, with a dray,
who started to remove the sun-dial. The police
were called, and the farmer given in charge. He
gave the police full particulars, including a
description of the young man from the country,
and was then released. The police managed to
secure the “farmer’s friend,” who was rewarded
with “two years’ hard.” Is there any limit to
human gullibility when the hook is baited with a
bargain ?

KIND LADY (to traveller): “I

“ Wyom ” : A few weeks ago an adven-
turous couple (presumably man and wife) sailed,
outward-bound for Walgett, from Narrabri West,
via Namoi River, in two canoe-like affairs, made
of blackbutt bark, and fixed up at the ends with
strips of kerosene-tin caulked with chaff-bags and
tarred over. Steering-gear, a piece of candle-box
nailed to a sapling. Locomotive-power, the cur-
rent. In one canoe were the man and the swags
and camping equipage, and behind came the
other, with the “ Old Dutch ” and dogs, lashed to
the preceding argosy with a piece of fencing-
wire. So far the intrepid voyagers have not been
“ spoken ” by any riverside town, nor has “Port”
Walgett reported their arrival.
* * * *
On Western Queensland roads may be
seen a go-cart drawn by an old man, while his
elderly wife vigorously pushes in the rear. They
reckon to manage about seven miles a day in this
fashion. The old couple confess that they found
themselves rather short of cash, and instead of
waiting for an old age pension they loaded up
their cart with coal-tar soap for sale, and went on
the wallaby. Make a decent thing, too.
“ Jack-a’-Clubs H : In answer to “ H.P.”
(B. 9/11/’01). Trees will carry weights, lifting
them up “on their own,” too. At Mt. Chalmers
(Q.) a growing tree forked under the fork of a
ring-barked tree and rooted it up to the height of
about a foot. Waverley’s “ Natural History
Essays ” (p. 464) has the following :
In the course of the following summer a nut, having
escaped the teeth of the destroyer, sent up its verdant
shoot in the centre of a procumbent millstone. One day
I pointed out this rising tree to a gentleman who was
standing by, and I said, “ If this young plant escape des-
truction, some time or other it will support the millstone
and raise it from the ground." He seemed to doubt this.
In order that the plant might have a fair chance
of success, I directed that it should be defended
from accident and harm by means of a wooden
paling. Year after year it increased in size and beauty,
and when its expansion had entirely filled the hole in the
centre of the millstone, it gradually began to raise up the
millstone itself from the seat of its long repose. This
huge mass is now Bin. above the ground, and is entirely
supported by the stem of the nut-tree, which has risen
to the height of 25ft., and bears excellent fruit.
Also, “ H.P.” is wrong about fences never being
drawn up by trees. I know of a tree that drew
up three posts (wire fence) through the wires
running in the tree when the fence was put up.
On Ganby road, a rail has been raised 7in. by a
tree; and at Yeppoon, about seven years ago, a
Kanaka hammered a nail into a tree as high as he
could reach, and now it is beyond his reach.
“ C.H.R.” : Re “ A.P.R.’s ” remarks
(11/5/’Ol) about ants erecting their business ends.
Have never seen them do this unless they are
disturbed, scent danger, or mean battle. This
more especially applies to the evil-smelling little
black ants which in large armies invade eucalypti
in search of provender. One touch with a stink
on their trail will cause an elevation of tails at
that spot, and in a few moments the movement
will be communicated far up the tree to the top-
most regiments. The most cautious approach to
the trail will often start them (as “ A.P.R,.” calls
it) “saying their prayers,”—but which appears
uncommonly like “ cussing.”
* • • *
Dear Bulletin, — Re W. Malcolmson’s
article, “ A Black Blot on White Aus-
tralia ” (Bulletin, 22/6/’01). I was
arrested in Cue some years ago, and
while on remand, in the look-up, two
niggers were brought in. Neither could
speak or understand a word of English.
I was in the court while their cases
were being heard. One of them was
charged with the larceny of a silver chain
from somebody’s camp. There was no
interpreter in court. The Warden, after
hearing the evidence of prosecutor, who
lost the chain, and the evidence of the
constable who arrested accused and found
the chain in his possession, sentenced
the prisoner to receive a flogging of 15
lashes ! Nigger No. 2, who was next on
the list, was charged with “deserting
his legal employer/* From the evidence
of a police-constable, it appeared that
some nigger was “ assigned ” to some sta-
tion-owner for seven years, but had
“run away” after serving only 18
months of that period. Information had
been given to the police, with the result
that accused was arrested. No evidence
was given in court by anyone connected
with the station on which accused was
supposed to have been employed, and
consequently there was not a shred of
testimony as to accused’s identity with
the absconder. Yet the warden sen-
tenced this poor wretch to receive a
flogging of 25 lashes and also to be
imprisoned for 14 days! I also saw
these men flogged. After vainly trying
to induce any of the white prisoners to officiate
as flagellator, a policeman, “took the job on”
himself; and after it was all over he seemed
quite indignant because the two nigs, took their
gruel without a murmur, although, you may
depend, it was not his fault.— E. J. Sutcliffe.
Cue (W.A.).
“Sackbat”: “H.P. ” (8., 9/11/’01) is
wrong about trees not lifting weights. One of
several instances I’ve known : In the cemetery,
Agra, India, is a tree which carried up a heavy
tombstone with the walls of the grave, and
it is believed there that the remains of the corpse
and coffin are similarly exalted. It is nearly 10
years since I saw it, but as far as I remember, the
tombstone was sticking out of the tree about 14ft.
from the ground.

“Vulcan”: Something, after all, in
“going on the land.” An orchardist in Yass
(N.S.W.) district, from an area of but 25 acres, ob-
tained a net return of £1227 for the season just
past. Of course, “luck” had a deal to do with
it; but—given equal chances —the difference
between this “lucky” man, and the average
unlucky man is mostly a matter of brains.

“ Boko ” : In par re Clarence River
brumbies (B. 16/11/’01) your informant displays as
much ignorance as he attributes to Sleath, M.L.A.
I doubt if you could now find a brumby on either
the Clarence or Richmond. Wire fences and
selectors have put an end to them. A few wild
cattle still exist in isolated spots, notably
Chandler’s Creek, Bonaldo, and other parts of the
Richmond Range, but the rifle has of late years
nearly wiped them out also.

“ Since the beginning of the present month
I have killed 107, and 13 were killed and one
wounded in a single night.” This reads like the
record of a gory cohentingenter, but it’s only the
officer in charge of Mount Kosciusko observatory,
reporting to meteorologist Wragge upon the
remarkable plague of rats which has suddenly
appeared on this loftiest point of Australia.
Dear Bulletin.— Re “ A Bushman’s
Notion ” (B. 13/7/’01). In the par. about Lam-
bert’s “Wool Team,” the word “cogles” should
be “keys.” The latter are thin, flat pieces of
iron, with an eye in the big end, through which
they are secured to the bow with strands of green-
hide. Cogles are merely washers, placed between
the key and the yoke to draw up a slack bow.
My mistake.— E. S. Sorenson.
“Trotter”: Visited Normanton (N.Q.)
recently. Norman ton is on the
Norman River, and sometimes
under it. Nothing will grow
there. Right to the horizon
extends a barren plain with
nothing to break the mono-
tony, and an average 100-
m-the-shade keeps the
inhabitants permanent-
ly tired. There are
eight pubs., but
they don’t flourish
and one large
store has the
monopoly of
the district.
Buy there, or
go without 1
The inhabitants
go to roost early
to dodge the ele-
phantine mos-
quito, and the
streets after
dark are de-
serted. The
nigger here
takes the
place of the
goat, and the
bac k-pre-
mises swarm
with half-
naked lubras
and picka-
ninnies. The
white children have
a peculiar lowering of
the eyebrows (caused
by the eternal glare
from the corrugated
iron), which gives
them a cunning
and old-fashion-
ed appearance.
Also, they de-
velop too early ;
a girl of 13 looks
20, and at 20
looks at least 30.
The eyes are weak
and the lids granu-
lated from the dust and flies. Once
every three weeks the town wakes from
its lethargy. The steamer is coming from
the south, and the whole population seeks the
wharf. For three days the town is in a state of
excitement and whisky—then falls wearily asleep
again till the next steamer. Truly a delightful
spot 1

A couple of swaggies recently struck a
back-blocks pub., and having nothing marketable,
evolved a choice idea. They commandeered a
piece of rope, and, just after dark, one of them
sauntered into the bar, holding one end of the
rope and calling “Whoa, there!” to the
imaginary horse. Then he asked, confidently, for
whisky, but as soon as he got hold of the bottle
his mate, who was at the other end of the rope,
dragged him violently outside the door, and Dy
the time the startled landlady had got round the
counter tl.ore was no man—no horse—no whisky 1

“ Trotter ” : Recently struck Alpha
(should be Omega), on the C.Q. railway, 270 miles
from Rockhampton. Two pubs., 2 white store-
keepers, 2 Syrians, and 5 Chows. Population
comprises every variety and color of humanity.
Wood-carting, water-carting, carriage of goods
and luggage from the railway, grooming, laundry-
work, etc., is all done by aborigines, who swarm
about the hotels and stores picking up a stray six-
pence, which immediately goes to the Chow for
opium. But the Chow is the boss of the place,
and acts it.

“ Corrybar ” on an aspect of the rabbit-
question in Victoria:—
In some “ rabbit cases,” at a country court recently, I
heard several of the defendants pleading
their inability to keep their land clear of
the pest, while Crown lands and reserves
in the vicinity provide harbors for them to
breed undisturbed. It seems that the
Vermin Inspector, being a Govt, servant,
cannot take action against his employer ;
result of which state of things is that, for
all the notice the inspector will take of
them, the rabbits may breed on Crown
lands till they simply swarm-out the
struggling selector adjoining, who will
thereupon be haled before the Crown for
“ not taking reasonable and proper pre-
cautions to rid his land of rabbits.” The
magistrate pointed out that any individual
could prosecute the Board of Land and
Works, and make it take up its responsi-
bilities, but the landholder knows better.
It is more profitable for him to “ poison ”
the reserves himself. The Crown carts a
Melbourne solicitor around the country (at
£3 3s. a day) to conduct its prosecutions,
In case some “bush-lawyer” should find a
hole in the Act; and the private prosecutor
would have to take similar precautions, or run
the risk of “ falling in ” for heavy costs.
t? This year’s S.A. census makes the tally
of the State’s full blacks (aborigines) 1745
males and 1640 females; in the Northern
Territory, 12,331 males and 10,717 females.
The census shows only 349 half-caste males and
341 females. Of the adult males 11,168, and of
the ditto females 9551, are returned as healthy
—how ascertained isn’t stated.

“ Jaok-a’-O. : John Perron, at
Yeppoon, (Q.), in front of a looalpub.,
put an 18-gal. (full) barrel into a dray
by strategy with a rope, doing the

• • •
The M’s.P. who recently visited
the Murray Flats (S.A.) bring back
dismal stories of the condition of
some of the German farmers there.
In one locality where there has been
no harvest for several years the frugal
Teutons live on rabbit. When they
catch the bunnies, which are fairly
plentiful in the scrub, they carefully
skin them and sell the pelts. They
eat the flesh, feed their fowls bn the
entrails, and even keep the bones for
manure.

“Riverina”: “ Kap ” (B.
l/6/’Ol) is right. Horses can swim in
hobbles. I have an old horse that
(hobbled) got into a billabong by-
wash. The current, carrying logs and
snags, was very strong, and the banks,
for 3ft. or 4ft., almost perpendicular.
Nevertheless, the moke swam over,
clambered up the opposite bank, and
was thence tracked over two gates,
and found five miles from the by-
wash with hobbles unbroken. The
enterprising animal was about 20
years old.

A Mud gee (N.S.W.) draper advertises
boldly in tne local paper goods for “the demi-
monde of fashion.” One would have thought (and

special advt. in Mudgee (pop. 2600).
Jacob, the ourang-outang (just deceased)
was not altogether prepossessing in appear-
ance, but was a genial fellow-creature in
a somewhat imperfect state of development.
Many visitors to Sydney Zoo reckoned
him a valuable acquaintance, if not an
actual friend. Jacob would take those whom he
favored out for a stroll round the grounds, walk-
ing sociably with them, hand-in-hand, and, if you
sat on the grass with him, Jacob would go through
your pockets, in a friendly kind of way, for loot.
If Jacob was in his cage and a stranger outside
the bars appeared to have nothing eatable which
he was inclined to give to Jacob, the latter was
prepared to trade, and would pull from some
sacking kept in his cell a string or two and offer
them as an inducement. Jacob’s life was sadly
wasted. Had lie been caught young, and brought
up to business, he might have descended to be
N.S.W. Colonial Treasurer, and built gaols out of
the State’s loan-funds.
The late S. H. Hyam, M.L.C., of Sydney,
held much the same position on Australian waters
as that held by the late Hon. James White on the
turf. Mr. Hyam at one time had in commission
the open boats—Lottie (24ft.), Florrie (22ft.),
Ettie (22ft.), Florrie (19ft.), Ettie (19ft.). All the
boats were manned by professional crews, who
took all prize-moneys, and the hobby must have
cost as much to run as a racing-stable. If writer
recollects aright, Mr. H.’s first racing venture
was the Rica, built by Reynolds, the crack builder
of 30 years ago. Joe Donnelly then came on the
scene with entirely new ideas as to sailing-models,
and to prove which style was the better Mr.
Hyam gave Reynolds and Donnelly an order each
to build a 19ft. and 22ft. skiff. Reynolds’ boats
were the old-fashioned, narrow-tucked craft, and
Donnelly’s the broad-tucked, which from that
day came to stay. The boats were sailed by
the best professionals in the harbour—Mont-
gomery, Ellis, Colebrook, and Fletcher—and no
expense was spared in fitting out. Strange to say,
the 19-footers were very equal in pace, but
Donnelly’s 22-footer proved a veritable sailing-
machine, running rings round everything opposed
to her. Mr. Hyam afterwards raced tne 24-
footers, Lottie and Carlotta, winning races out
of number regattas, until lie pretty well scared
off all opposition, which was, perhaps, the reason
of his going out of the sport. After giving up the
boats, he kept, as a hobby, a very select little
herd of Jersey cattle, running in a paddock near
his residence at Balmain. This hobby came to a
very sudden end. He used to supply a large
number of poor people with milk, which bit of
philanthropy evidently didn’t suit certain people
in the milk-trade, as one morning Mr. Hyam arose
to find his pets all dead. A bale of poisoned
lucerne had been thrown into the paddock during
the night.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.
E. J.: The picked negro, of whom you can see
many examples in Sydney, is a better laborer than the
average white. And he is not economically dangerous—
he sticks to the labor-union and demands full wages all
the time. Of course (apart from the race-mixture aspect
of the question) the black man who is a good trades-
unionist is less objectionable to his fellows than the mean
white who blacklegs on his own order. The white non-
trades-unionist is no good, anyhow ; the cream of the
country’s manhood and industrial skill is unionist to the
core... .E.P.A. : To find the reason why the people
of Queensland, almost unanimous in their opposition
to the Kanaka slave-trade, are not able to have their
views reflected in the local Parliament, it is necessary to
study closely the electoral system of the State, perhaps
the most infamously “faked” in the world. At the
Federal elections, when the people had a chance to speak
at the ballot-box, the verdict against colored labor was
practically unanimous... .Moxy : Can’t say till we’ve seen

Yeria : Question of deportation of all negroes from U.S.A.

portion of the population and the difficulty of finding an
area in the world where they could be settled with any
chance of success. There seems to be only one efficacious
white man’s method of settling a colored problem, and
that is wholesale murder. The B. doesn’t want to have
Australia discussing that method in the future, and so
wishes to see colored immigration stopped now. ..

Mounted Postman Disabled.
The Government mounted mailman who carries
the Golspie and Taralga (N.S.W.) mails—Mr.
Jeremiah G. Webster—has, upon request, given
some of his experiences for publication.
“Few mailmen,” said he, “have undergone
such hardships as I have. 1 have ridden
thousands of miles, night and day, wet and dry,
exposed to all weathers, in a climate which for
severty is, in my opinion, second to none. In ’B2
I first ‘ went under ’ owing to a severe attack of
paralysis, which completely disabled me. Creep-
ing pains came in my limbs, I grew thin and weak,
and for three months was unable to walk. In ’92
my arm became paralysed and useless, and five
years later the rest of my limbs grew paralysed,
and my constitution seemed breaking up. I lost
all strength, my body was swollen, and anything
touching it pained me. My food brought on
fearful attacks of biliousness, and I suffered the
greatest internal agony.”
“ So you were quite helpless ? ”
“Yes,”said Mr. Webster, “I could not even
turn in bed. I was unable to work, and my
misery of mind and body was so great that I
knew not what to do, for all treatments had proved
powerless. Friends then recommended me to try
Dr. Williams’ pink pills for pale people. So some
were procured from a Taralga chemist, and I
began _ taking them. Soon afterwards I felt a
slight improvement. Then, as I continued taking
this medicine, I could feel the in
my paralysed limbs. My appetite returned, the
biliousness left me, and gradually my strength
came back, so that I was able to leave my bed and
walk about. I used Dr. Williams’ pink pills until
I was once more strong and robust, and I am
stronger to-day and more fit to perform my duties
than I have been since 1882. I worked through-
out last winter with all my old activity, and my
health actually improved under these adverse
weather conditions.”
Many others like Mr. Webster who were once
apparently helpless paralytics, have been cured
by Dr. Williams’ pink pills, which make new
rich blood with every dose, aud stimulate the
circulation. They also tone up the nerves and
strengthen the spine. By their action on the
blood and nerves they cure anaemia, debility,
nervous exhaustion, hysteria, neuralgia, rheu-
matism, consumption of the bowels and lungs,
dysentery, liver and kidney troubles, indiges-
fcn, effects of pneumonia, influenza, fevers, etc.
Bold by chemists and storekeepers and the Dr.
Williams’Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney,
and nine per box, post free; six boxes
nfteen and three. Send in full description of

“ How McDougal Topped the Score”—
“My Mate Bill”—“The Billiard Marker’s
Yam” —these and many other famous recitation-
pieces will be found in “The Bulletin Reciter.”
Cloth-bound and illustrated, price ss. post-free
from Bulletin office.
Two important additions to Australian
literature have just been made in “The Poetical
Works,” of Brunton Stephens, and “Joe Wilson
and His Mates,” by Henry Lawson. The first-
mentioned volume contains many new pieces, in-
cluding “Fulfilment,” which appeared in The
Times and several Australian newspapers on Com-
monwealth Day, and which was dedicated by
permission to the late Queen. The volume is
uniform with “The Man from Snowy River.”
Mr. Lawson’s new book is issued by arrangement
with the English publishers, and is obtainable in
cheaper form than the English edition. The
result of Mr. Lawson’s literary work in England
appears for the first time in book-form in this
volume.*
There is one place in Sydney where the
business of money-lending is carried on in an
equitable manner and where those in need of
financial accommodation can relj 7 on being
decently treated. The sensible man who wishes
to get rid of his monetary worries at a minimum
of cost and trouble to himself goes to the offices of
the Australian Equitable Investment Co., Ltd.
The co. has a large capital for investment at low
interest, and is always open to advance money on
risks which the banks would decline; and acts
generally as a medium between the banks and
ordinary money-lenders. Men in business can
obtain assistance for their venture at a figure
which will leave them a fair profit for themselves.
The co. advances money to any extent on deeds of
land, bond warrants, merchandise, stock in trade,
scrip, interests under wills, furniture and all classes
of security. Press notices which have been pub-
lished from time to time express the favorable
opinion which generally exists regarding the co.
Mr, J. E. O’Brien is the Managing Director, and
the co.’s offices are at 45Elizabeth-street, between
King and Hunter-streets.*

A series of remarkable statements concerning
rice in Melbourne were made by Dr. Grattan
Guinness, London medical evangelist, who is i
conducting a mission in this city. He shuddored
when ho thought of the banks of the Yarra, of
the Fitzroy Gardens, of the Exhibition Gardens.
They were a scandal to the Legislature of Vic-
toria. Two things could be done. First, electric-
lights could be put in these places. (Cheers.)
Second, there should be no public gardens open at
night. They were doeed in London, why not
here ? Within the municipal croas of Melbourne
there ought to be left no dark areas and no
v. gardens unclosed at night.

“ White Australia,” educationally. From
a Northern paper :
Miss Wing Wah, teacher of pianoforte.
How easy to prevent these eruptions of
wild abusiveness which occasionally break out in
the Federal and other Parliaments of the Com-
monwealth. If honorable members would but
partake moderately and consistently of D.C.L.
Whisky, and not mix ’em, they would always be
too healthfully happy to be otherwise than
courteous. The glow of absolute serenity com-
bined with the power of achievement produced by
the aforesaid brand has endeared it to the hearts
and stomachs of millions of thinking men. Thus
it is known as “the Whisky of the Wise.”*
It seems to be generally conceded that
when the North Pole is at last discovered a
Scotchman will be found in possession ; but what
he will be doing is a moot point. Will he want
to do a wee bittie in real estate with the new-
comers, or will he have a telescope in position to-
view the Aurora and the Northern stars at sax-
pence a look, or will he be retailing “cool summer
drinks ” and ice-cream ? There wouldn’t be much
of a trade in these, but just think: the drinks,
could be kept quite cool without going to the
expense of an ice-chest, and no freezer would be
needed for the ice-cream. Doing without the
freezer would save him from 13s. Cd. to 305., that
being the price of 2qt. to Bqt. Ice-cream Freezers,
at M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, Ltd., Elizabeth-street,
Melbourne.* .-

POLITICAL
POINTS
The pro-Kanakas of Queensland intend to
crawl at the feet of- Joseph Chamberlain to save
their beloved black : that’s safer than a revolu-
tion.
The Bulletin has a vague idea that it
once heard something about a North Sydney
bridge. Has anybody seen a bridge lying about
recently ?
A year ago the war was over. Roberts knew
it was over; the British Ministry knew it was
over ; the Pro-Gore part}' knew it was over. But,
unluckily, the Boers didn’t know it was over, and
they don’t know it yet. The Boers are an ignorant
lot.
Melbourne Argus has now settled down
to a policy of direct antagonism to the Barton
Govt. In State politics it has been in Opposition
since the days of J. B. Patterson, and Argus
antagonism to an Administration is generally
looked upon as a good sign of its stability..
British Unionist press is fast going into
hysterios. One paper describes the troops in
Africa as “stale,” another says the generals are
no good, a third demands the recall of Ketchener.
In this crisis the only sensible thing Lord
Salisbury ca» do is to send for Major J. Randal
Carey.
The Bulletin is glad to see the Federal
Parliament gradually drifting away from old-
fashioned ideas about “responsible Govt.,” and
dispensing as far as possible with such flummery
as messages from the Crown. The less the
Australian legislature leans on anything but its
own Constitution the less painful will be the
wrench when it comes—for come it must.
Stated that S.A. Council will accept the
deadlock clauses of the Constitution Bill—which
are framed on those in the Commonwealth Act—
rather than risk losing the measure, which the
Tories believe will so reduce city representation
as to make the Labor party a negligeable quan-
tity. Just now both Ministers and Opposition
are anxious to get rid of the Labor members.
“The real cause of the anti-British
agitation in Germany ” (re the S’Africau war)
says the cable, “is attributed to the fact that
many newspapers gladly accept pecuniary
assistance.” The root cause is that the Germans
don’t like the English. Germany is England’s
keen rival and deadly foe —despite the race-link.
“ A man’s enemies shall be of his own household.”
WHEN PEACOCK’S POLITICALLY DEAD.—THE CHIEF MOURNER.
FAT MAN (aa “Joe”): “He was werry good to me, he was.”
The worthlessness of Yic. Opposition was
plainly shown when Premier Peacock announced
that he had dropped the Convention Bill. An
immediate censure motion would have captured at
least a dozen malcontents from the Ministerial
side, but Irvine and the rest hadn’t stamina or
sense enough to seize the opportunity, and
Peacock was given a week to straighten up his
reluctant following. Irvine now submits a hostile
motion, but its chance is not too good.
The Age overreached itself badly just
before telling the Peacock Govt, that it ought to
be ashamed of itself, by ordering its reporters,
who had worked in harmony with the Argus men
on many jobs for years previously, to start a news-
gathering feud. When the Age criticisms became
particularly savage, vengeful Ministers privily
gave away to the hated Argus the secret of their
Convention climb-down, and the Age clean
missed the item.
Should the Council pass the Parliamentary
Economies Bill agreed to by the Assembly, S.A.
Ministry will be reduced from six members to
-*-* 1 18 generally supposed that Education
Minister Brooker and Lands Minister O’Loughlin
will be the lambs led to slaughter, though R.C.
influence may save the latter, in which case
Woiks Minister Foster or Treasurer Butler would
have to go.
No cloud without a silver lining. While
Britain is losing men and money galore, Kynochs,
Ltd. (the ammunition firm in which the Chamber-
lam family is interested), recently doubled its
dividend ; and the Birmingham Small Arms Co.
(Herbert Chamberlain, chairman, brother of
Joseph) reports the largest profit in its history for
the last year with a2O p.c. div. and a 50s. bonus
per share.
Since last N.S.W. election the battle of
Orange and Green has raged fiercely at Wyalong,
m and out of the local papers. One mine-owner—
a Protestant dismissed his R.C. employees,
and a leading R.C. owner reciprocated this kindly
action by turning off the “ Prodistans.” So far
as the employees were concerned it only meant a
change of bosses. Wyalong is a good place to
live out of just now.
That poisonous gang, the Australian mili-
tary caste, now emphasises its existence and
aspirations by loud beat of drum. The Prime
Minister voiced real Australian public opinion
last week by saying that the Commonwealth did
not intend sending any more troops to S’Africa.
But State commandants resent this, and yell
for the despatch of “3000 to 5000” more men.
Who will prevail—the Government or the Caste?
The Lyne-See Government quietly added
19 new deadheads to the list of those en-
joying life-passes over the N.S.W. railways.
Premier See’s defence seems to be that in 1900 the
Assembly rejected a bill to abolish deadheads on
railways. But on that occasion the House was
numerically thin and otherwise unrepresentative ;
15 voted for the Bill and 31 against—altogether 46
out of 125 took part in the discussion. It was
known at the time that in anything like a full
House the Bill would have been carried.
More wonderful Foreign-trade logic :
Mr. Oook (N.S.W.) could not see what reason the Min-
ister had for increasing the protection given to local
manufacturers. The increase must only deplete the
revenue, and he wanted to know why 3d. per lb. duty
should be presented to the manufacturers who had been
doing very well up to the present. ... If the whole of
the tobacco consumed paid the full duty, the difference
to the revenue in Victoria would be £93,572 per annum.
Now the total wages paid was £37,000. For every pound
paid in wages the country paid £2 4s. Bd. In New South
Wales the anomaly was even greater. There the loss to
the revenue was £131,000. For every £1 paid in wages
the State paid £4 14s. 6d. To put it in another way, it
would pay these States to give every man employed
£3 10s. per week in Victoria and £7 per week in N.S W.
to do nothing. This was a system of pensions with a
vengeance.
In other words, if the tobacco industry was
destroyed in Victoria the people would cease to
receive £37,000 a year in wages, and the country
would pay £93,572 more in duties, and Joe Cook
calls that a saving. This way of adding together
the money the country receives, and the money it
doesn’t pay, and calling the result the total loss, is
characteristic of the Shoddy party.

Senator Walker, who topped N.S.W.
Senate poll, has declared for a piebald Australia.
His point is that the magnificent support given
by N. Queensland to the Federal movement
deserves recognition. Yet he must know that
N.Q. saw in Federation its one chance of salvation
from Tommy Tanna, and that it was the persistent
pressure of the N.Q. representatives which
stiffened Barton’s hostility to the Kanaka.
Walker is the sort of man who rewards a valuable
service with a kick in the eye.
THE ANEMIG EMU.
MOTHER EMU : “ What, not well? Ah, you must
take iron, my dear.”
The Peacock idea of allowing Ministers of
one House to appear on the floor of the other in
support of bills of their initiation is the Natal
law. But an inconvenience of the measure was
practically illustrated in N.S.W. Leg. Council
over the Shearers’ Accommodation Bill. Attorney-
General Wise ran down to the Assembly to help
Crick on the Arbitration Bill, and while he was
away the Council divided on the Shearers’ Bill
and cast it out by one vote. Wise would have
neutralised that vote, and the casting-vote would
have sent the Bill into Committee.
Premier Peacock’s proposal to reduce the
Vic. old age pension maximum from 10s. to Bs.
per week has got him the worst possible advertise-
ment, while the amount saved is not worth it. A
loss of two shillings out of a weekly income of ten
will be a terrible blow to the poor pensioner,
while the State will only benefit to the extent of
£465 per week on paper. Considering that the
old age pensioner spends all his money in the
country, and doesn’t go globe-trotting like some
of the gilded Civil Service pensioners, there will
be no real saving worth mentioning.
State Parliaments in Australia must do
one thing or another about clergymen as members.
Why should South Australia discriminate between
an “ordained” clergyman and the common or
garden description of pulpiteer? Every State
Parliament has a bunch of these latter, some of
them being Ministers of the Crown, and while
this is so there is little sense in keeping out “ the
regularly ordained.” Besides, any prohibition of
the sort is undemocratic. The electors should
have full choice, and if they care to return a
Parliament of parsons, it is their business. They
wouldn’t do it twice.
No sooner had Yes-No Reid got away to
Sydney, leaving his lieutenants—M’Millan,
Conroy, and Holy Joe Cook—to do the menial work
of stonewalling the Tariff, than the first-named
blurted out the real sentiments of the Foreign-
trade party. To his piteous pleading that, in
cases where the duty had been lowered or taken
off, the poor importer should be refunded the
difference, Kingston turned a deaf ear. Then
Watson interjected, “ Would you give the refunds
back to the consumer ?” “I don’t care twopence
about the consumer or anybody else.” That’s the
importer’s case exactly ! But catch the tricky
George saying anything so foolish !
S.M. Herald, in an optimistic article on
English Freetrade :
Within the last quarter-century, the wheat-area has
decreased by one-half. . . . What, we are asked, will
the position of England be if, becoming more and more
dependent on foreign supplies for food, she should become
involved in European complications and her fleet should
not prove equal to the defence of her maritime commerce ?
. . . Areas which a few years ago yielded only a small re-
turn of wheat have been used for fruit-growing, the pro-
ducts being converted into jams and jellies. The flower
industry has enormously increased. So that while there
has been a decline in one direction there has been a
marked growth in others.
What would she do ? Why, feed on flowers and
jam !
“ Kiwi,” from Maoriland :
The demand for grants to religious schools has been
killed here, for the present at all events, by the mixed
political position. The great majority of the local R.O.’s
are of the masses, and have long since realised that when
it is a question of getting a rise in wages the average
employer is just about the same, irrespective of his creed ;
so, naturally enough, the heavy-handed son of Mother
Church, so far as politics are concerned, makes up his
mind to follow the labor ticket rather than a church
ticket. Among the wealthier class of R.C.’s Bung is
well represented, and the cold-water people have
threatened Bung so badly that his ranks are solid now,
and proof even against religious squabbles. He is too
earnestly engaged in the protection of the beer-interest
to worry about educational matters. On the denomina-
tional education question the R.O.’s are the only ones
with any sincerity, and their camp being divided, other
religious bodies show no inclination to take up the
campaign.
“The Old Cove” writes on the Curse oF
Cheapness:
Ed. Bulletin. —Cannot go all the way with the Labor
party in their endeavor to put cotton fabrics on the
free list. Sheetings, calicos and prints might pass,
but, if there is a natural industry that might claim
consideration under the Federal tariff, it is the
workinar-up of our staple product, wool, for local
consumption. The free admission of flannelettes,
moleskins, dungarees, &c., is a menace to that
• l . . . . , d rtf TTTArtI
industry, which, with our coarser grades of wool
(so cheap that they might largely supplant these
cotton goods to the advantage both of the 'o-
dustry and the consumer), should not be
tolerated. Peculiar thing that the bush-
worker, living -through wool, uses perhaps
less of that article than any civilised
worker. Week-days—cotton shirt, dungaree
or moleskin pants, and (if any) cotton socks.
For occasions, a triennial 30s. outfit of unquali-
fied shoddy ; whilst the wife and family sel-
dom go beyond prints and flannelettes —
the curse of “ cheapness.”
William Forster’s rejected
scheme of N.S.W. Leg. Council
reform of 40 years ago was a
Council of 30 to be elected for six
years by the whole State voting
as one electorate. The franchise-
was to be the same for both
Houses, but no man under 30
years old was to be eligible for
the Council.
The most wretchedly-paid
N.S.W. Govt, servants are country
post-officers, where the P.O. is run
with a farm or school. They have-
much of the responsibility and worry
of larger offices, have to sell stamps,,
deliver and receive mails, issue postal-
notes, register letters, furnish periodical re-
turns, and be in constant attendance on callers;
the only holidays are Good Friday and Christmas ;
and the salary £lO a-year ! —less than 4s. a-week.
Color-antipathy has been of gradual growth
in Australia. Even such a tribune as John Dun-
more Lang fought to establish a colony of Chinese
on the Northern rivers of N.S.W. Tea-growing
was to be their speciality, and the Doctor was
enthusiastic over the virtues of his proteges.
He oreferential tariffs. W. R. Brock in
the Canadian Commons, on March 21st:
I know of cases where hundreds of pieces of goods nave'
been brought into Yorkshire from Germany partly
manufactured. They were then run through a machine,
weighted up a few ounces to the yard, finished, and sent
out to this county, and 75 per cent, of the preference
went to Germany. In many cases German manufacturers,
at present are sending fancy goods _to London,
Eng., with a small machine to do a little finishing, after
which they are sent out to Canada as British manufac-
tures. It is impossible to get at all the details of the
ways in which this thing is being done, but the door is
wide open for that sort of practice. I know of one case
where a large quantity of granite, supposed to have come
from Aberdeen, really came from Norway, and was then
sent to Aberdeen, where a little work was done on it,
after which it was exported to this country, to the
detriment of the granite industry of New Brunswick and
the eastern townships of the province of Quebec.
“ Malle ” to The Bulletin :
Oondoboliu (N.S.W.) election, upset by Elections and
Qualifications Committee, has been again won by Labor
member Clara by seven votes. His last majority was 14..
Thirteen Labor members stumped the constituency om
Clara’s behalf. This election is a fine example of the
spread of Tammanyism in Australian politics. The Labor
crowd told the electors that any person who voted'
against their nominee was a traitor to his country, his.
home and his children, or the other fellow’s children ;;
but their clincher was this : “ If you vote for Stuart you
have no hope of getting the Bogan Gate railway ; he is
only one man, and the Government is unfavorable-
towards it; and you cannot expect us to vote for it if you
go against our party. If you put our man in we will
support this line. There are 23 of us, and we hold the
balance of power, and we can compel the Govt, to go on
with it.”
Which goes to show, if authentic, that the Labor
crowd is very unscrupulous —just as unscrupulous,
in fact, as the other political crowds.

VITADATIO

READ WHAT MR. ARSOOTT WRITES.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir,—l think Vitadatio worthy of great praise as a
genuine remedy. It has saved me a few pounds of
expenses in doctors and medicine. My wife had a tumor
on her neck, causing her much pain and alarm. The
doctors, when consulted, advised instant operation—there
being danger in delay—but my wife shrinking from this,
we consulted an herbalist, who wanted six guineas per
quarter for an indefinite period to remove the disease.
Vitadatio was then brought under our notice, and we
resolved to try it. By the time the fourth large bottle
was used the tumor had come right away, leaving only a
mark on the neck where it had been. There has been no
return of the trouble, and my wife has enjoyed good
health since. You are welcome to use this as you please,
I am so satisfied with what Vitadatio has done for my
wife.

The struggle for a crust. The Q. State
teacher in the main gets starvation wages and the
toughest treatment, yet there are 1432 candidates
down for next month’s examinations.
The law says that a woman need not give
evidence against her husband, nor a man against
himself, yet* in d N.S.W. court, the other day, a
man was committed, for seven days because of
reluotanoe to give evidence likely to incriminate
his son.
One Vic. mother now argues that Church
bazaar raffles are at least as pernioious as totes or
sweeps. Her own small boy and another, at a
recent bazaar, took tickets in a penny raffle and
won a trophy each—one a bottle of wine, the
other a bottle of pickles. Failing to sell them, or
raffle them again, the boys decided on a picnic
and wolfed their prizes. Found in a state of col-
lapse, they were taken off in the ambulance to the
Children’s Hospital.

LIVER LOGIC
The Liver is the mainspring of the system.
When active it is the great purifier.
When sluggish it easily develops a poisonous
tendency.
The Liver both secretes and excretes.
It secretes about two or three pounds of bile daily.
It excretes impurities and waste materials.
The Liver is the sewage farm of the body.
If the Liver is sluggish it cannot exnel its refuse,
and as it must go somewhere it is absorbed back into
the blood, causing biliousness and often jaundice.
The Liver also makes from the blood a kind of
sugar, which, if healthy, is used up as nourishment.
If the sugar is excessive or inferior it cannot be so
used, it then escapes into the urine, causing diabetes.
A Torpid Liver develops gout and rheumatism in
predisposed subjects.
The absolute, incontestable remedy for all this,
and the one which never fails is Nature’s Great
Restorative, Kutnow’s Improved Effervescent Carls-
bad Powder.
This Powder is made by an ingenious expedient
from the health-giving medicinal springs of the
Continental Spas.
It is improved by removing all drastic or lowering
properties.
It is gentle and pleasant, but the greatest of all
Stimulators of the Liver.

“ Via " : Mention of the moneyed Towers
men (B. 9/11/’01) is surely incomplete without the
name of Harry Paradise. His father, with

Dawn and other good things.
A fruitgrower at Tangmangaroo, near Yass
(N.S.W.), made upon his apple-crop, last year,
£l2OO clear. He has 27 acres of trees, and, but
that tons of fruit were lost through the impossi-
bility of getting it to market quickly enough, the
returns would have been nearly £2OOO. An equally
heavy crop is expected this season.
“ R.A.K.” on an educational matter :
Dotted over Melbourne suburbs are scores of petty
schools, masquerading under hifahiting names, and
occupying mostly little chapels and churches where there
are not the necessary appliances and accommodation.
The teachers are spinsters of uncertain ages and qualifi-
cations, and the main things learnt are worsted work and
snobbery. About June, when practice for Xmas concerts
starts, all else in the way of learning goes by the board,
and parents are pestered for fancy-costumes to be worn
at the “ breaking-up”—a teacher-glorification, or mutual
admiration reunion. You can’t blame the poor teachers j
for trying to keep up such a sickly show of education. |
They have to earn a living somehow, and they think it’s i
a more respectable way than domestic service. The fault '
is with the parents, who are mortally afraid that their !
Cecils and Horaces will become rough and boisterous, and
send them accordingly to these little “ dame-schools,” ,
which turn them ont conceited, namby-pamby, spunk- j
less little prigs, hopelessly out of place amongst healthy- j
spirited youngsters, and liable, consequently, to many a
licking when they drift eventually into the State school.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Big cricket-match, N.S.W. v. Maclaren’s team,
opened disappointingly Friday. On a tip-top wicket, with
weather conditions and light excellent, home side batting
broke down completely, notching 288, where double that
number wouldn t have been extra good. But for the stiff
stand made by the tail end (Kelly, Clarke, and Howell)
the total would barely have topped 200. Cornstalks’
thus failed lamentably where they were expected to shine.
Weak batting more than strong bowling was responsible
Braund wrought the trouble with deliveries that curled
and twisted snake-lilte about batsmen’s legs, and beat
them badly often ; besides, fielding was perfection a
prominent feature being the frequency and precision with
which wickets were thrown down, particularly by Jessop
whose work was startling. Howell was last man in and
his wicket put on 61 in 35min.—34 of which he hit up
himself. Kelly carried his bat for 33. Vic Trumper
secured top figures—67. The whole innings occupied
4hr. 10mm.
• * * *
Bilowell’s great stand for tenth wicket in
N.S.W. first innings recalls that the last time he shaped
for the Cornstalk State against a ’Ome team he accumu-
lated 95 in 63mins.
* » \ * *
The English bowlers may not be tip-toppers ;
they may have not a real first-ranker amongst them ; but
they possess a great advantage—variety. The fastish
Barnes, the tear-away Jessop, the tricky Braund and the
queer, hopp.v Gunn make a confusing quartette. On Friday
Maclaren did not have to draw on his change bowlers,
Jones, Hayward and Blythe. The Englishmen are not
deplorably weak, as the tests will show.
» * * *
First innings of the Englishmen (Saturday) left
neither side with a pull. Visitors were batting prac-
tically the whole of the afternoon and compiled 332,
which gave a lead of 44; of these, 145 went down
to Maclaren. N.S.W. bowling was much better than
anticipated, Clarke particularly shaping excellently
with his leg-break work. Sending down the second
over Noble strained a thigh sinew, and had to be kept
off for the rest of the day, which meant a deal to
N.S.W., for, on the season’s form, he stands out from all
others. In one half-hour during the early part of
Maclaren’s innings only 12 runs were added. Jessop’s ad-
vent met with a great roar. In smin. he got 15 runs (2, 5,
4,4) off 8 balls, but a vigorous dash at one from Clark
made a yorker of it :he was clean bowled. Maclaren
batted 4J hours before O. Gregory caught him on the
edge of the cycling-track, and never till then gave a sem-
blance of a chance; 88 of his total was made up of fours.
Maclaren’s innings (a great one) was completed under the
stiff handicap of his not having quite recovered from that
attack of rheumatism which shut him out of the Vic-
torian match and the fact that only scant support came
from his fellow-batsmen. This month, four years ago,
Maclaren, on the same ground and playing with Stod-
dart’s team, made 142 and 100.
» * » *
Jessop’s early dismissal was a disappointment
to the 30,000 watching Saturday’s play. The taste he
fave of his style showed that the certainty of an hour’s
atting from him would always draw the crowd. He hit
one fiver —an extraordinary stroke. Seeming to chop
down on the ball, he drove it over the fence with appar-
ently no effort at all. A wild, ill-judged swipe at a
breaking ball was fatal to him.
• * * *
Saturday’s play attracted (official figures)
30,000, which looked quite 5000 more, and gate yielded
£I3OO atop of coin from non-members among Friday’s
15,000. Biggest authoritative statement was made four
years ago when Stoddart’s team played their first match.
Turnstiles showed 33,000, with takings exceeding £I4OO.
• * * •
For the most part Monday’s play of N.S.W.
v. England was flat, stale, and crushing. The sprinkling
of cranks among the 12,000 looking on couldn’t squeeze
the faintest approach to a cheer for hours. N.S.W.
second innings was a procession put and back, controlled
principally by Braund until results stood at 7 for 115.
Noble and Poidevin then got together, and thence on the
bat proved superior to the ball. Noble’s strained leg was
a load to carry, but he shaped full of grit. With 43 down
to him Poidevin gave a hard chance, and runs subse-
quently came fast till Barnes clean bowled Noble for 74.
The eighth wicket added 149. Noble’s total occupied 2J
hours, and included nine fours. Later, with Iredale as a
partner, Poidevin notched 13 off Braund in one over.
Wickets down were : 1 for 35, 2 for 63, 3 for 69, 4 for 101,
5 for 106, 6 for 115, 7 for 115, and 8 for 310, all out for 422.
At time of writing the English second innings had com-
menced, and 4 wickets were down for 141. Poidevin
compiled 140 not out against South Australia last season,
and with 77'5 was second to Olem Hill (103'33) in Austra-
lian Sheffield Shield averages. After, taking 5 for 22 in
the early part of the match Braund had 92 scored off
him without getting another wicket. At one stage of
his innings Noble only accumulated 13 in 40mins.
»•*. ■ • ■
Braund is going to be a terribly expensive
bowler in Australia, as indeed he is in England. He is
the delight of the spectators, as his delivery means either
runs or wickets. At Adelaide most of the batsmen were
all at sea with him. But the good batsmen ought to get
his measure in about two matches, and then the fun (for
them) will begin.
* * \ N
Barnes’ performances thus far have raised the
question whether there are many more at ’Ome like him
outside of the county teams. The chap who took 12 Vic.
wickets (six clean bowled) for 99 runs, seems to have been
quite unknown to the people who publish little books
about “famous” English cricketers. An apology for
Barnes was offered by A. O. Jones in a London newspaper
article shortly before Maclaren and co. set out to try their
luck. Wrote Jones: —“ The greatest surprise of all, per-
haps, was the announcement that Barnes, of Burnley,
was to have the last place in the team. Personally, I
have never seen him bowl, but I have heard from a very
good source that he is a really' fine bowler, and would
have played for Lancashire if his League engagements
had permitted. lam sure Maclaren must have given it
ver,v serious consideration, and knowing, as I said before,
Maclaren’s sound judgment, I shall be much surprised if
Barnes does not turn out an acquisition to the team.”
But at that time Jones didn’t expect him to bowl rings
round his colleagues in the second match of the tour.
» • * •
Maclaren is said to hope that his side will win
the toss in some of the test matches, to give his team a
chance of setting their opponents a big score. The bad
tossing luck of visiting English teams is proverbial. The
inter-State captains have won the choice of innings in
each of the first three engagements of the present tour.
Maclaren might take comfort in the good old “ law of
averages.” He must win some tosses, and having lost the
inter-State ones, has a better chance of success in the
test matches.
» * * •
To break the monotony of the main grand-stand
the Sy'dney Cricket Ground trustees have built three
pepper-box minarets on its top. The shadow of the cen-
tre one falls right across the wicket, exactly on the
“ blind spot,” which is a severe handicap to the batting
side. The £IOO spent on these turretty tops was not
only thrown away, but has caused the only grievance ever
urged against the splendidly-appointed Sydney ground.
* * * *
“Mimic” Kelynack, of Melb. Herald, is again
attached to the English cricket team as rep. for Reuter’s,
who “do ” the tour for pretty well all the English
papers.
• * • •
S. A. eleven for inter-State matches, as selected,
was naturally the same as beat Maclaren’s team. Giffen,
however, pleaded inability to get away. He used to do
just the same with regard to English tours 10 or 12 years
ago, and now, as then, opinions differ as to his motives.
The colt who takes his place was captain of Prince Alfred’s
College last season, and an all-round player. Local en-
thusiasts give (besides the absent Darlimr) Hill, Giffen,
Hack, Travers (“ as good as Blythe any day ”), Jones and
McKenzie all a chance for the test-matches!
* * * *
Big cricket-match affected attendance at A. J.O.
November meet (Saturday) considerably, but sport was
good. The bejewelled one had a great turn up, spite of
three well-backed nags landing prizes, for betting was
close enough in each instance to provide him a good mar-
gin. Dunk (Stockbridge Handicap) and Courada (Trial
Stakes) each provided a -complete “ skinner ” for the
Moseses. Dunk was only invested upon to win a com-
parative trifle for fear he might get home ; and so little
was thought of Courada’s prospects that, though his
stable-chum—Barley Bree—carried a fair weight of coin,
not a bean went on the winner. Courada is a well-bred
’un of good shape, by Haut Brion (imp.) from Plevna
(imp.), and looks a racer, but up to Saturday hadn’t
gathered in anj’thing for two years. He is owned by one
of the raucous-voiced—“ Jack ” Samuels. Mr. H. C.
White bred t’other surprise (Dunk), who is by since-dead
Melos from Venapa by Martini Henry from Formosa by
Yattendon.
* * * m
Tyro Stakes (4fur.) for 2yr.-olds at Rand wick,
Sat., seemed soft for Great Scot on his recent Melbourne
running. But when Ceres went out, and money came
along in heaps for similarly-owned Chantress, it looked as
if her chance was rosy, as Ceres shaped particularly well
at Rosehill a fortnight before, the inference being that
the two had had a trial. Great Scot broke away first,
and, carrying some of the starting-gear with him,
appeared to hold the race safe, but Chantress, coming
full of vim, squelched the big fellow easily. Chantress is
a beauty worth watching. She is by Bill of Portland
from Chand Bee Bee (imp.), by Royal Hampton from Miss
Baba, by Springfield from Gunga Jee, by Orlando from
Himalaya, by Middleton from Moodkee, by Venison.
Two fillies mentioned herein (Ceres and Chantress) are
promising champions, and unquestionably the best seen
out this season.
* * * *
Those pars, in the Melb. dailies on Melb. Cup
morning as to the alleged probability of Wakeful being
scratched for the big event were followed by some nause-
ating babble re the noble unselfishness of owner Leslie
M‘Donald in permitting the mare to run for the mere sake
of the public who had backed her. Says “ Terlinga ”
in the Australasian— “ That she (Wakeful) would
have _ won all the w.f.a. races if she had not
run in the Melbourne Cup is very likely. Mr.
M'Donald’s wish was to scratch her once Revenue was
safely landed on the course, but friends persuaded him,
and he ran her, with results that were not satisfactory to
himself or to those who laid odds on her for the Flying
Stakes. . . . That running her in the Melbourne Cup
meant risking the loss of the Flying Stakes was just what
her owner feared. When you come to think of it, the
public did not benefit by getting a run for their money
in the Melbourne Cup. She had no chance of beating
Revenue, and the race probably knocked her for the Fly-
ing Stakes, in which her defeat cost her Melbourne Cup
backers more money.” “ Terlinga’s ” veiled sneers at the.
foolish public who expected a run for their money with
Wakeful are as amusing as the statement that Leslie
M'Donald wanted to scratch the mare on the
morning of the race. In the first place, the
stable must have put some money on the mare at one
time or other. If she was good enough to back for
Caulfield Cup she was good enough to back for the bigger
race beforehand, on the very likely chance of Revenue
going amiss. Having lost the Caulfield by a short head
she was undeniably “ well in ” the Melbourne Cup, and to
say she “ had no chance of beating Revenue ” in a race
where 20 horses run and accidents and ill-luck are possi-
bilities, is to talk nonsense. Mr. M'Donald knows from
his own experience of Nada’s defeat in Patron’s year that
the best of good things runs in danger of bad luck. And
knowing that even Revenue was no cert., except
in a turf way of speaking, what a foolish Leslie
he would have been to deny himself Wakeful’s
chance of winning either of the three stakes—£sooo,
£IOOO, or £SOO ! As for the talk about the mare spoiling
her prospects of winning all the w.f.a. races if she hadn’t
run in the Cup, there were only two w.f.a. races in which
Wakeful could have run—the Flying Stakes, in which she
did run, and got £IOO for being second, and the O. B.
Fisher Plate, on the last day of the meeting, for which
she didn’t start. The prize available for the winner in
the first-named flutter was not more than £4OO, in the
Fisher Plate it was less than £3OO. And in the Melb.
Cup the mare had a rosy chance of earning more oats
than that by finishing second to Revenue. But just
here the “fly-flat” critic has probably winked his
other eye and remarked that the stakes in these w.f.a.
races are a mere bagatelle, and the®befs are what the
stable would have scooped if Wakeful had been reserved
for the minor events. The delusions of the fly-flat are
amusing. To win £IOOO over Wakeful in the Flying
Stakes a man would have needed to risk £4OOO, probably,
which is not the sort of gamble that any M'Donald cares
for. The mare’s success in the F.S. would have necessi-
tated odds of 6tol on her for the Fisher Plate. Put the
Australasian pars in the light of common-sense, and
look at them through spectacles, and you can only
wonder what it all means. Present scribe takes it to be
simply grovel and spinach.
• • • •
The fine attendance at Flemington on each of
the four days of this last Cup meeting was not more
noticeable than the falling off in the business of Ikey Mo.
Every year the “ Carnival ” becomes more of a picnic and
less of a gamble, owing to the educating influence of the
tote in other States. Soft-headed shepherds may con-
tinue to fight for the Mo. monopoly in Vic. and N.S.W.,
but they can’t shut out the Terewth concerning the sinful
shortness of his “prices” and the roguery that is the
natural outcome of the betting-market system. Argu-
ments in favor of the tote have at least revealed to the
tote-less punters the folly of heavy wagering with the
“ books,” and it now seems probable that owners, finding
the “Ring” so reduced in financial capacity, will advo-
cate the introduction of one principal bookmaker—the
machine—for the convenience of all parties.
* * * *
Of £66,082 distributed on placed horses in
“ Tatt.’s” six Cup sweeps this year, no less than £34,875
went to Victoria. Tas. took £13,500 in one lot, S.A.
£7830, N.S.W. £6367, Q. £2025, W.A. £1485. Another in-
stance of the rapacious Victorian wolves robbing the
weaker sister States.!
* v * * *
She’s coming along. At a Queensland race-
meeting the other day a lady owner was suspended for
two years, and her mare disqualified for life for
suspicious practices. Is this the first case of its kind out
here ?
* * * *
Now turns out that the estate of reputed fabu-
lously wealthy late jock, Tom Hales, is only worth £IBBO.
When Hales retired he was undoubtedly well fixed. Some
assessed his accumulations (cash, nags, station-property,
and otherwise) at £40,000. A Riverina station and stud
farm spec, together with other badly-judged or unlucky
investments, however, made a big hole in Hales’ capital.
He also let a fortune slip when he parted with the crack
sire Lochiel.
The little syndicate that bought Revenue from
the late St. Albans estate was engineered by Colin
Templeton, who had been for several years W. R. Wilson’s
confidant and financial agent—a connection that dated
back to the early days of Broken Hill. Though the estate
held its tongue well, Templeton sent some strong and
early advice to old friends still battling on the silver-
fields, and some of them used it before it was too late.
• » • *

two miles in 3.8 4-5. Both times were accomplished from
standing starts. Chase chased a motor-tandem.

* * * *
Man-eating Irishman Denny Moloney went
through his great hurricane act, at Sydney Golden Gate
A.C., Saturday night, and fairly smothered comparatively
ill-conditioned Fraser. Sweltering hundreds cheered
like mad while Denny rushed and ’rastled and sprawled.
Fraser, much the cleverer, dodged well, ducked, and
timed nicely occasionally, but to little purpose ; Denny
was always there or thereabouts, despite his wild work
and frequent fruitless forcing. Towards the end, lack of
fix told on the Melbourne man, and he weakened, while
Moloney punched hard and strong as ever. In the seventh
round Fraser was spread-eagled and counted out. Denny
is looking round for the next man already, and wants to
fight every night in the week if victims can be served up.
* * • •
The daring of it. Sydney Sportsman last week
published a capital picture of pug. Albury Clifford and
called it M'Coll. There isn’t one point of resemblance
between the two.
* « * *
Decadence of Britain is complete. Murkan
middle-weight pug., Jack O’Brien, having grabbed the
heavy-weight championship of England, now deals out
stoush to two in one ring. Lately he backed himself to
knock-out both inside 10 rounds, and neither survived the
third.
