* * * *
The passing of the naked rowing-man at Sydney
regattas is announced—N.S. W. Rowing Ass. having decreed
he shall, in future, be fairly-decently clad—in short-
sleeved guernseys. Hitherto, great lumps were scissored
out of each side of his singlet, leaving nothing but a strip
of stuff down the chest and back, exposing an Esau-like
body that hadn’t much else to recommend it. Why, was
a mystery, as the less seen (uncovered) of the majority of
him the better he was.
V * * •
In England M.L. sculler Tom Sullivan defies
all-comers, bar Towns and Britisher Barry. Harding is
a possible acceptor, A match between Phelps and Sydney
crack, Harry Pearce, is probable. World’s champion
Towns and an “unknown” were matched for £SOO aside
to row on the Thames. Surmised that Eddie Durnan
(Hanlan’s nephew) is the mysterious one.
* * * •
Adelaide has had two bicycle fatalities almost
simultaneously. One was directly due to the free-wheel,
the rider sliding along (on the level, but with a strong
wind behind him) and breaking his face in against the
broadside of a tram car that was crossing. In the other
case, a young fellow was found lying dead by a broken
machine on one of the big hills behind the city.
The destruction by fire of that ancient
timber building known as the Oriental Hotel, in
Wellington, M.L., accompanied by horrible
panic and loss of life, ought to wake up the
Wellingtonians to several facts. First, it
is worse than useless to leave to the City
Council the task of compelling Mrs. Hash or
Mr. Bung to provide proper fire-escapes. Very
often Bung is the Council. Secondly, the time
has come to condemn at least the wooden hotels and
boarding houses, if not all large wooden build
ings, in Wellington. The fact that the city is
built over a fault, which lets the whole place slip
down anything from a fraction to the eighth-of-
an-inch every three or four years, has caused most
of old Wellington to be built of timbei’. Still,
there are a lot of stone, brick and cement buildings
there now, and it is infinitely preferable to
be buried and crushed to death in one act
under a stone or brick building than to be slowly
roasted like a rat in a trap in a burning wooden
one. Last time the writer went to Wellington
he called at that self-same hotel, and was con-
ducted to a room with only a skylight in it.
Asked for one where he could sling out his rope in
case of fire, and the co. ;rcd gent, explained they
hadn’t one—all full up. So this scribe sorrowfully
left his portmanteau and went out for an all-night
stroll.
About the Chinese assault case, which has
occupied the expensive attention of a Sydney
Court for eight sittings:—
There has been much discussion as to the translation
of the circular which convened the alleged illegal meeting
where the row took place. Half-a-dozen expert witnesses
gave different literal interpretations, backing what they
urged with quotations from Chinese classics and diction-
aries. What makes the matter so bewildering is that a
Chinese word has quite a variety of meanings. For
instance, “ kwong” means “to bar a door,” “ to have
connection with,” “ to pass through,” “ a Customs
House,” “a line,” “a consequence,” and so on. The
witness best educated in English made one clause read
“You are requested to attend without guile.” The others
could find no characters to represent the last two words.
And so the case has been dragging along. The lawyers
engaged don’t seem to mind : the Chinese are always
ready with cash for litigation. There is a libel-case to
come on this month with the same crowd. The split
is between two Mongolian parties represented in
Sydney—the Reform party, and the Yin Lik Tong, the
latter a charitable society, and the former an institution
“ for reforming China politically.” The two societies
fight whenever they meet, with bamboo sticks or writs as
the occasion seems to suggest.
A recent Bulletin paragraph mentioned
that books on the S’African war may be obtained
direct from London per medium of Bulletin
Office. We now learn that Will Andrade, the
Melbourne bookseller, has stocked “Peace or
War in S’Africa,” one of the aforementioned
works, and the same can be obtained from William
for Is. 3d., post free. His address is 101 Bourke-
Btreet, Melb.*
Victorian politics are more than mixed.
The Govt, has the confidence of scarcely a dozen
outsidethe Ministry, whilethe.Oppositiomsineven
worse case. Last week, Outtnni — the renegade
Liberal —tried his hand at forming a party, and
was left in the lurch. Irvine has a censure motion
in his pocket, but his hopes of a working majority
are Buckley’s. The only visible combination pos-
sible is one of country against town, but tliere s
no leader in sight who can unite the forces. Only
Peacock’s popularity, backed by his fearsome
laugh, keeps his Government on the front bench.
The Laborites don’t love him, but neither do they
adore Outtrim, while Irvine and Gillies are worse
than poison.
That wonderful body, the N.S.W. Public
Service Board, on 15th last month completed an
investigation into the administration of the old
dead-and-gone Marine Board, abolished by the
Navigation Acts Amendment Act in ’99. The
old Marine Board was responsible for the Atacama
disaster, and for the running of steamers, on
Sydney Harbor with plates so thin that sometimes
the painters shoved their brushes through them
when laying the paint on heavily, and the Public
Service Board has feebly grasped some idea of the
iniquity of the awful thing nearly two years after
the Board is dead and buried.
The present conjunction of the three
large (to the human eye) planets of the solar
system, Venus, Saturn, and Jupiter, is exciting
some interest. This foregathering of our fellow-
worlds is, of course, only an apparent one, and is
simply the result of perspective. But so close a
conjunction is a rare occurrence. Jupiter and
Saturn will be near enough on Nov. 28 to be seen
in the same field of a telescope—a sight not
granted to the human eye since 1683. Many
generations will pass away before such a spectacle
is visible again. So look to the West! Such a
sight will come to you nevermore.

At Poverty Point.
“ Tatt.’s ” announcement that the first prize in one of
his Cup sweeps had gone to a Theatre Royal syndicate,
Melbourne, was followed by a rush of begging letters and
congratulations to the wrong people. Distressed agricul-
turists and impecunious clerks selected Bland Holt for
the position of lucky friend-in-need, and wrote him
urgent requests for £lO £nd upwards by return post.
Also Chris. Simonsens the business manager, and trea-
surer Sproule found themselves pursued by the conven-
tional “ lame dog ” who needs helping over a stile to the
pub. beyond. Likewise, the existence of Baker and
other members of the co. was embittered by the smiles of
many friends and borrowers-elect. It comes hard on
the management and co. to be suspected of unnatural
good luck after losing their bets as usual. Meanwhile,
the actual syndicate of six supers who drew the winning
ticket were content to keep dark and wait for the coin.
An awful mistake to seek the bubble reputation at the
borrower’s mouth.
“The Message from Mars” seems to-have been doing
very well in Tasmania. Miss Winifred Austin now appears
as Horace Parker’s girl, instead of Miss Ruby Ray ; and
Hammond, late of Tyrone-Power’s co., takes the place of
Thos. Holder as the young man who escorts H.P.’s
fiancee to the ball. The change from Holder to Hammond
is a gain to the pretty little show, anyhow, and more
especially in Tasmania, where last-named was a popular
amateur for years ere he went to Melbourne and broke
out in a professional capacity.
Louis Lohr, at latest, was still undecided as to his
choice of a Vic. country hotel (with shooting and fishing
attached), where he can run the show for an unlimited
number of years and develop into a shire councillor, or
something, if the district needs shaking-up. The exact
location of Lohr the Genial ought to be worth an
announcement from every, newspaper office in Australasia
that has had the pleasure of his acquaintance. By-the-
way, Phil Stuart—an old friend and contemporary of
L. J.L. in the show business—has already established him-
self behind a bar. Phil is licensee of the Fawkner Club
Hotel, Toorak-road, Melb., where he draws sundry hogs-
heads per week of a particular beer which has more
virtues than a patent medicine.
The tenors are changing their spots—or the tenor of
their ways. Courtice Pounds, once with “The Firm,” is
playing Shakespearian clowns with Beerbohm Tree in
London, and Joe Taple.v, better known to Australians, is
appearing in melodrama with our old friend, Charles
Cartwright, and has made something of a hit as Gringoire,
the ballad singer in “ The Shadow Dance” —a new version
of Hugo’s “Notre Dame ” that is making money for
Cartwright. The critics all manifest surprise over Tapley’s
ability as a comedian, and his singing is described as a big
factor in the success of the show. Evidently Joseph’s
coat has more colors than we suspected in Australia.
While “Tyrone Power” (Mr. Edith Crane) has been
playing in Australia, another “ Tyrone Power” has been
playing at Dublin Gaiety. The original Tyrone Power,
the great Irish comedian, was lost 60 years ago in the
ship President between England and America. No trace
whatever of the ship was obtained after she started from
port.
A musical prodigy, a pupil of Louis Grist, of Sydney,
has been exciting Maoriland. Miss Kate Nicholls’l
17 years have, according to the chief newspaper of Mas-
terton (no less), found her endowed with a “ rich,
powerful soprano voice of wide range and cultivation."
The audience of the Masterton Town Hall, which gave
Kate her first hearing, was, according to the same organ,
“fashionable and critical,” which in Masterton means a
good deal. They accorded Miss Nicholls “ imperious
encores,” mainly for something that one editor man next
morning described as “Orpheus and His Tante.” Miss
Nicholls is to take a look-in at Australia by and bye,
prior to astonishing London, but the B. wouldn’t advise
her to go until she has got the verdict of Bulli (N.S.W.),
Nhill (Vic.), also of Birdsville (Q.). What a tired place
London must be, with all the geniuses of the Empire con-
verging on it and clamoring at its front door for the foggy,
blase old city to get up out of its bed of ages and be
astonished once more !
Miss Billie Barlow shortly leaves South Africa for
England—not for the Australia where she is so anxiously
awaited by Bulletin Bill.
Osmond Tearle died the other week. Tearle for a long
time ran a co. round the English provincial towns,
playing Shakespeare and the “legitimate,” but he had a
reputation, also, in America, where he was the original
Silver King. Present scribe saw Osmond Tearle play the
part at Wallack’s Theatre, New York, with a co. that was
almost as good as the Firm’s original “ Silver King ”
crowd in Australia. Either of them could give points to
the Wilson Barrett co. who “created” the drama in
London.
Re “Methuselah” and W. H. Williams (8., Nov. 9).
W.H.W. never belonged to the A.B.O.—Amateur Benevo-
lent Comedians—l was the founder, secretary and
manager of this club, and ought to know. Also, Win.
levers didn’t belong to it. In after years W.H.W., W.I.
and myself were members of the Garrick club. I have a
programme in front of me now, a performance of the
“ Poor Gentleman,” May 7, 1866, in aid of a memorial to
G. V. Brooke. The caste included W. levers, D. Martin
(secretary for Agriculture), G. A. Walstab (journalist), and
John Edwards the younger, solicitor. After the comedy
a “ Threnodia,” written bv R. H. Horne, and composed
by J. Summers, Mus. Bac. Oxon. (brother of the sculptor),
was sung by W. H. Williams, Donaldson, Amery and
Angus. Williams’ name is at the foot of that bill as
printer.—F.
Melb. Philharmonic Society has engaged the one and only
Dolores to sing at its next subscription concert, at Melh.
Town Hall, on Saturday (30th). For this joyful occasion
Sullivan’s “ Golden Legend” has been ear-marked, and
Mdlle. Dolores, who has sung the music in America, will
show how very golden the “ Legend ” can be made.

-rom Doctor to Doctor.
A VAIN SEARCH FOR HEALTH.
REWARDED AT LAST.
Th* Case of
Mr. J. FARRELL.
(by a local reporter).
In these nays of C oiniuon wealth festivities,
proclamation 0 , and liolidays, one hesitates
to write a line that has not something about
federation in it. But as many of our fellow-
mortals are physically unfit to push their
way through the crowds who are bent on
seeing everything worth looking at, the
writer waited on Mr John Farrell, of No. 8
Victoria Place, Adelaide, in the hope of get-
ting a word in edgeways which may be read
by those whom >t may interest.
“ When resident in Sydney,” said Mr.
Farr 11, “ I had a close shave of being placed
under the ground through a complication of
diseases which followed an attack of typhoid
fever. My mother took me to nearly all the
doctors in Sydney, but their efforts to relieve
my sufferings wre all in vain One medical
gentleman said my kidneys, and also my
liver,’’were in such a hopelessly diseased
Bt ite that I would be a martyr to the most
dually ailments which aff ct mankind for
the remainder of my life. He could do as
much as it was possible for medical science
to accomplish, lie st-'ted, hut I was no' to
expect to he cured, as I was too far gone for
that. I was inf uiiual that m> life might be
prolong'd a little by the exlii larating effects
of changes of air, sol resolved to travel
about as long as my means permitted me.
Then 1 came to Adelaide, feeling more like
a corpse than a man, and immediately
placed myself under the care of an eminent
physician. The same experience as I had
in Sydney was repeated here. From one
doctor to another I went in quest of relief,
and after d i.sing me up with physic till my
stomach could not take it any longer, 1 was
given up for a had job. The prospects of
my living were just as remote as those of a
man awaiting his do m without the
slightest hope of a reprieve, for my diseases
had set at defiance all the medicines which
had been prescribed. Alth ugh 1 had been
pronounced to be incurable by those who
had treated me, 1 determined not to die
without making a struggle for life, so I pur-
chased many advertised remedies. Would
you believe that those advertised nostrums
only aggravated my sufferings to an extent
which drove me to despair, fo" I was now
convinced that I was going to have a life of
purgatory. With this conviction in my
mind, and the dreadful verdict that I was
doomed to destruction still ringing in my
ears, I made still another att inpt to grapple
with my enemies, and this time my persever-
ance was rewarded with success.”
The listener’s interest now bordered on
exciteme.it, and in answer to the question,
“ How were successful resul ts accomplished ?”
Mr. Farrell proceeded to relate :
“By taking several bottles of Clements
Tonic —a medicine that would have saved
me years of suffering if I had taken it at the
start ofmyillmss. As all pi svious treat-
ment had failed I had not the faintest idea
of getting anything like permanent benefit
from any medicine. No man in this world
has suffered more than I have done, and I
had arrived at such close proximity to death
that the most I could hope for was a little
temporary ease. A strange numbness, like
paralysis, used to strike my hands and pro-
ceed up my arms and into my tongue, and
my throat became so affected by this death-
like sensation that could scarcely breathe.
I had frightful bilious headaches which
made me thoroughly helpless, and at the
commencement of my illness, before I had
to lie up altogether, I used to go quite blind
from a sense of giddiness which overpowered
me, and often had to be led home like a
child from my work. My heart fluttered
and throbbed so painfully that I was afraid
to move lest I might make it worse, and
when I suffered with wind, which always
happened after taking a little food, I
swelled up and could never get any eaoe
until I started belching. I was extremely
nervous and so weak through being unable
to digest any food that I could not stand on
my legs. A heavy sensation across th«
chest served to trouble me greatly, and the
pains between my shoulders were equally
annoying. And as for sleep —well, the most
I got lasted about an hour, and even then I
did nothing but dream, and when I awoke I
felt more weary and worn-out than if I had
been awake all the time. Not only was my
urine cloudy and scanty, but I also had
severe attacks of diarrhoea, followed by long
terms of constipation, and I had such a
dreadful taste in the mouth that I was
always retching. Right across the kidneys
I was so afflicted with pains that I was no
better than a cripple, and the aches in
my loins were agonising in the extreme.
A mist hung before my sight, my eyes feel-
ing hot and swollen, besides being sunk
back in their sockets, and as my skin be-
came yellow and I was very thin and emaci-
ated you may tell that I looked like one
whose days were nearly numbered. My
hearing was so defective that all sounds
were converted into a peculiar rumbling
noise ; but my greatest adversaries had yet
to be met Probably through taking so
many useless medicines, my stomach was so
deranged that my food would not stay down,
and the cramping tortures I endured were
enough to kill any man. It was at this
time—at the last moment, as it were—that

A Jingo daily paper with any sense of
humor would surely avoid publishing in the one
column, from the pen of the one cohentingenter
the following (it appears in Sydney D.T.)
An order wa3 issued that we were each to hand back
one of our blankets. . . . After shivering for a while
under a single blanket, we decided to rush the guard and
get back what was taken from us. . , .
Then—still speaking of the same district—
We came on the convoy unexpectedly at daybreak. . . .
We burned all the waggons, leaving the women and
children on the veldt.
“ J.E.” re Australian “accent”:—
“To speak correctly one must have a good teacher.”
Now, is the average Australian school-teacher qualified to
teach young Australians to speak pure English ? Can he
teach them the correct vowel-sounds ?—how to open their
mouths, use their lips and shape their tongues ? Does the
average A. s.-t. know anything about elocution ? Remem-
ber : he is the person responsible for the Nation’s accent 1
—and hearken to his pupils’ almost unanimous answer 1
The remedy : Make every school-teacher go through a
course of lessons in pronunciation, etc. Make him pass
an examination in elocution, and let him be kept in outer
darkness till he can speak well enough to satisfy his
examiners. Eliminate Euclid, Latin, Algebra, if neces-
sary, and replace them by lessons in Elocution. Let
inspectors frequently visit schools, examine scholars and
see that the teachers are up to the mark. This need not
cost much more money, and it will fill the bill.
[Yes, and fill Australia with sucking Jay Pays and
“ eloquent ” aldermen. Teach Australians to
open their mouths instead of shutting up ! The
man who keeps his mouth shut gets no flies down
his throat, and can pull in the national traces
without wanting to rest every minute to cough up
“a few remarks.” To shape their tongues ! Rats !
Eliminate Algebra and replace Elocution—to
raise up a race of posturers, shallow-pated gab-
blers, prattlers, actors, street-corner politicians?
Angels and ministers of Grace, defend us !]

A Day in the Far West.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One typical day—month, say Eeb. Time, 4 a.m.
The station manager is suddenly startled into
wakefulness by the melancholy howling of dingoes
down by the fast-diminishing tank that supplies
the head station with drinking water, and also
supplies sundry mobs of half-starved sheep.
Muttering a hasty morning prayer, he rolls off his
bedstead—no bed-clothes, too hot—and creeps to
the place where he keeps his gun. On reaching
the tank the dingo has disappeared, but in the
grey, sickly light of morning, he sees a familiar
sight, sheep hopelessly bogged round the sticky
margin of the tank. Another morning prayer
follows, and the man proceeds to extricate those
of them whose eyes have escaped the crows. In
so doing, he gets himself covered with greasy
slime which won’t come off when dry. The
miserable, rescued animals are propped up in a
standing position, and the manager walks back to
the house.
Five horses are mournfully standing as close to
the stable as they can get ; they have long since
given up even looking for grass. The boss opens
the door and feeds them with some rubbishy stuff
called chaff, of a quality which apparently pro-
duce-merchants reserve especially for the back
counti’y, and which costs anything from £7 to £8
per ton delivered, being brought on the final
stage by worn-out, lean bullocks urged to about
seven miles a day by green-hide and oaths. Hav-
ing fed the horses, and made a mental calculation
as to how long his stock of dusty chaff will last,
he returns to the house to wait for breakfast.
In the laundry is the manager’s wife, already at
work trying to get the washing over before the
daily sand-storm begins. The “ married couple,”
after the manner of their kind, have chosen the
most inconvenient time to go on strike, and have
retired to the nearest shanty to knock down their
cheque.
After breakfast, manager and jackeroo proceed
to the stable, and the routine work of a drought
day begins. “You take the old mare, ‘Sally,’
to-day, Tom, and go out to the dry lake and bring
in all the sheep you can find there to the scrub-
cutters.” Upon examination, however, it is found
that “ Sally ” has a puffed back. Also, both Tom’s
dogs are so worn-out and footsore from constant
work' that it is absolutely necessary for them to
have a spell, so a new arrangement has to be
made. Ultimately Tom gets away on another
mare, “Gracey,” taking with him a quart-pot,
tucker, and water-bag, to spend a long, miserable
day under a blazing sun, driving, or rather, kick-
ing along, small mobs of knocked up sheep, on a
knocked-up horse, and with not even a dog to
help him. His faithful little slut, “Grannie,”
makes a feeble effort to follow, and then crawls
under a turpentine bush and lies down panting.
Enter Mick on the scene carrying two milk
buckets each containing about a quart of milk,
which is all he can get from six cows. “Old
Bally don’t give any milk worth speaking of now,
boss, and she’s that poor that what she does give
ain’t healthy-looking.” “ Very well, turn her out
and don’t inilk her any more.” “ What about the
piping for the M'Comas water-lift?—the pump
won’t draw another day without another length
put on.” “I’ll get Kelly to attend to it.”
“What about killing-sheep? Those few in the
ration-paddock ain’t worth killing, and there ain’t
a bit of fat in the kitchen.” “Well, I’ll bring
back a sheep to day from the scrub-cutters’ camp.
I have to go there in the buggy with rations.”
“I see the draught horse has got a sore shoulder
from carrying so much water through the heavy
sand.” “Oh, damnation !—well, pad up the
collar the best way you can. You know if he
goes back on us I shall have to send 300 miles to
where the spelling horses are.”
The manager then goes and interviews Kelly
about the piping, listens to a lot more worries
about tyres getting loose on drays, etc., and then
goes to the stores to put up rations for the scrub-
cutters. He notices grimly that the daily dust-
storm is beginning to rise, but is pleased to see
that his plucky little wife has got the washing
done; and the clothes dry almost as quickly as
they are hung out. By the time he is ready to
start a tornado of sand is whirling past. The
pepper-trees, the only ornamental foliage about
the place, bend to the force of the gale, and every
building resounds with the rattling of iron roofs.
Papers, feathers, leaves, empty kerosene tins,
buckets, and even live fowls go flying past, and are
caught up on the fences. Altogether the aspect
is mean, very mean ; but the manager is used to it,
and goes out to put the horse into the buggy,
stopping to kiss his three little ones playing quite
unconcernedly in the dust-storm under the hot
verandah, their faces thin and wan from the long
hot summer.
Before reaching the spot where the scrub-
cutters are at work—seven or eight men at 255. a
week and their keep—he has to carefully thread
his way through thousands of acres of black fallen
mulga scrub, the only food the sheep have had for
years. He delivers the stores to the cook, who re-
marks, ‘ ‘ I’m about full-up of this blooming caper,
I’m goin’ to leave.” “Very well, come in and
get your cheque,” says the manager sharply,
although he adds to himself, “ I don’t know where
to get another cook, I’m sure.” He then drives
off to visit a tank which has not been looked at
for some days, finds the water all gone, except a
little drop right in the centre, and sticking in the
black stinking mud he ruefully counts 39 sheep,
on the eyes of which the vile crows are hard at
work. There are also four young emus, and one
weak kangaroo, who gazes at him with large mourn-
ful eyes. More slime work. At the end of nearly
two hours he has rescued and propped up about 15
sheep. The rest, and the unfortunate native
animals, have to be left to their ghastly fate. On
his road home another tank is passed, but this has
had wire-netting put round it. There are, how-
ever, about 100 obstinate sheep, which, in spite of
the fact that they have been taken almost daily
to the station tank, where there is water, will
keep coming to the dry one. Manager makes a
mental note that these must be looked after to-
morrow, or they will die,
During the lonely drive home the aspect of
affairs comes before him in all its grim reality.
Tanks dry, and half-filled with sand; the drains
leading to them hopelessly choked with drift-
sand. Fearful loss of sheep ; no lambs last year,
and no chance of any this. No crops for the
last four years ; all horse-feed to be bought at
drought prices ; the face of the country partially
covered by a network of fallen scrub, and cost
of scrub-cutting averaging about £lO a week. It
is not a pleasant picture ; and, when at length he
reaches the stable, his temper is not improved by
the sight of a strange horse standing in one of the
stalls, munching. “Well, old man, I am come
on a begging mission,” says his neighbor, the
nominal owner of Sandy Rises. “ Can you spare
me a half-a-ton of chaff till my teams come?”
“ Oh, certainly,” replies the manager, trying to
look as if he had a haystack or two on hand, and
remembering, at the same time, that his neighbor
had done the same to him last year. Then the
neighbor lets out a flood of miseries: “In a
fearful fix, old fellow ! Something gone wrong
with one of my best wells, and, until it is put
right, I have to muster the sheep every day and
take them to a boggy tank, miles away ; and my
chaff is all done, and the mailman says that the
teams cannot reach the station for two weeks yet.
The bullocks are very poor; two have died, and
they don’t know how they’re going to manage that
dry stretch from Eurugabah tank to my place.”
As they are talking, the sullen red sun sinks
behind the low western hills, and with it sinks the
wind. Tom arrives home red with dust, both
man and horse fairly knocked-up. The three men
go to the house, where a vigorous onslaught is
being made by the wife on the cubic yards of
sand which have found a lodging in every room
during the long weary day. Then comes the
bath —half a kerosene-tin of water to each man—a
nip of whisky, and dinner. In the evening,
whilst smoking outside, the notes of the piano
came from the lamp-lit room, and, for a time,
they strive to forget the miseries of their sur-
roundings.
N.S.W.
BILBY.
This Morning.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sun rose on the Wail Village Settlement as I
passed this morning, and from a clump of gums
that barred the green with long morning shadows
thousands of birds twittered a welcome, self-
assertive magpies’ voices leading the irregular
chorus. To the left, the unbroken reserve un-
dulated steadily, rising gradually in a long,
sweeping, marigolded slope to the darker-green
fringe of timber that showed the beginning of the
sand-belt. The railway-embankment’s whitish-
brown and red relieved the never-ending stretch
of green, and from the vistas of the scrub-belt
peeped here and there the yellow tufts of golden
wattle-bloom. Wreaths of smoke rose straight
from chimneys of every variety of architecture,
and seemed to rest complacently at some 20 feet
or so above the iron-roofed houses, nestling in
their diminutive enclosures. Gardens streaked
with grey pathways intensified the snugness of
each house, and bespoke subtle suggestion of the
combination of Vitality and Beauty.
Poverty was there—the all-enduring, ox-like
stolidity alone making it bearable —but it struck
me that, in His painting of the morning-picture,
the Great Artist had purposely concealed it. Men
whistled cheerily, as the magpies, to the morning
sun, whistled their tribute to the Artist’s cun-
ning—spoke to their teams as to long-tried com-
rades—persuasively, almost gently, as if kindness
were, after all, the better incentive. An old
decrepit man raised his furrowed face and creaked
out “A great marnin’ ” as I hailed him from the
embankment, and a barefooted youngster steering
the rear cow of a short procession by the tail, gave
v ent to the vigor that the beauty of the picture
had unconsciously lent to her. A snatch of song,
a cheery objurgation to “Rosy,” a run to head an
obstinate calf that would wander ! The child’s
mother, carrying a bucket, moved lightly and
sprightly towards some yards a little way from
the house.
The long red road to the township stretched
away to the north through an avenue of dark
green mallee. A lonely gum raised itself here and
there, like some grim old rock amid the sea of
green. Out to the eastward, green plains rose to
the horizon, with their clumps of dark bull-oaks
looking like belated flocks, awakened by the
dawning—one had strayed away for feed —and
another —and another. The white roofs of the
far-off farmhouses gleamed good-morning; vari-
colored herds of cattle, deep to the knees in trefoil
and grasses, dotted the uncropped paddocks. It
was a master-picture of calm content and peaceful-
ness. “Too calm —no relief —nothing of that
contrast which humanity demands ere it grants
its tardy appreciation ” —so thought I as I sat on
the top of the embankment.
Over the hill rode a mounted constable, spick-
and-span, on a prancing grey. He called out
“Do you know where —— lives ? ” I didn’t. I
was a stranger. I watched him ride some distance
down the road, refer to some paper, as if to a
plan, and look around. I watched him ride—the
representative of Law and Order, the visible
emblem of unalterable rigidity, the outcome of
humanity’s desire not to be got-at by its next
neighbour—l saw him dismount and go in at
the gate of the paddock from which the old man
had hailed me. He threw down the reins of the
well-trained grey, and, as he walked to the house,
I saw the coat lifted and a gleam of steel cross
the intervening space. The door opened, and he
entered, to reappear shortly in company with a
tall, ungainly fellow, with whom he walked to the
stable. A horse was led out, and the trooper gave
the man a leg-up. The two, riding, left the house
and re-passed me on the hill. The prisoner looked
quite a lad —about nineteen —and he hung his
head despondently while his fettered hands hung
limply on the horse’s neck. A man in a cart
turned to stare at them, and when they had gone
over the brow of the hill, I hailed him and asked
for information. He reckoned “ young was
nabbed for sheep-stealin’. Time he was, too.”
An ignorant lad in the clutches of a remorseless
law—an old man sobbing in that hut by the road-
side —and on such a morning. I am wondering if
the Artist didn’t surpass Himself.

L.M.D.
“ F.” : Was surprised to find “clinah”
spelt that way in a recent Bulletin. I don’t
know how the more endearing term for “donah ”
is spelt in the Slang Dictionary, but it is clearly
identical in meaning with the German Jcleine, and
is intended to have exactly the same sound,
whereas “donah ” is just as clearly a vulgar per-
version of “donna.”
N.S.W. Hansard staff, during a recent
sitting of the Assembly, was kept continuously at
work for 30 hours in a vile atmosphere, and at an
occupation which calls for the patience of Job,
the arm-muscle of a cricketer, and the brain-
alertness of a cashier. Such a, fact is entirely
discreditable to the Assembly; if it must gabble
for unreasonable hours it 'might make some pro-
vision for the relief of its unfortunate employees.
The Upper House proved that it had some bowels
of mercy by adjourning in a hurry when it heard
the facts, so as to give the miserable shorthand-
writers a rest.

Starry Camp.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the cool night-winds are blowing
And the foot-sore mob’s at rest,
And the camp-fire logs are glowing
Like a sunset in the west;
When the dews are glistening under
And the tufted pines are damp,
Then I lie awake and wonder
If there’s grass on Starry Camp.
The stretching plains seem silent friends,
The clouds take shape of trees,
And surely yonder starry bends
Are bluegrass to the knees !
And when the red lights twinkle
You can catch the moan and stamp
Of the cattle, and the tinkle
Of the bells on Starry Camp.
Here, behind, the creeks are drying,
And the water’s scarce ahead,
And two hundred steers are lying
Back between our camp-fires dead ;
But away up there, high over,
Every star’s a golden lamp,
Lit to light the road-sick drover
To his rest on Starry Camp.
Don’t you hear the night-winds sighing
Through the cloud-bush, lute and lyre?
Can’t you see the drovers lying
Head to saddle, foot to fire ?
Hear them sing the soft words suited
To the tune the soft winds, vamp—
Careless singers, spurred and booted,
Round the fires on Starry Camp.
Yet it’s not all rest up yonder,
I suppose there’s work to do ;
How d’ they hold the wings, I wonder,
When the gray mists veil the blue ?
For whenever rain comes stinging
And the roaring storm-winds tramp,
You can hear the cattle ringing
Round and round on Starry Camp.
I am getting tired of roving
Round those hornies in the lead,
And I’m tired, sick tired, of droving
On the stock-routes bare of feed ;
But I’d chance another battle,
With no chain-wide lanes to cramp,
Idling down with mud-fat cattle
Round the bends to Starry Camp.
N.B.W.
WILL H. OGILVIE.
Magda in the South.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The level, lonely plains, treeless, brown from
the drouth of Australian summer, stretched far
into the distance on all sides. The railway, its
metal 3 gleaming hotly white beneath the afternoon
sun, ran through in a curving line that took its
apparently endless way as far as eye could reach.
It was broken in one place only, in all those im-
merise spaces, by a platform and crossing, beside
which crouched a low-browed, blue-stone cottage.
It was the dwelling of the station-mistress of
“ Digger’s Road ” ; and past it ran the high-road
which was the excuse for this stopping-place,
separated .from the surrounding plains by low,
roughly-heaped walls of the dark stones that lay
scattered thickly over the ground in all directions.
The train from Melbourne had stopped, set
down a solitary passenger, and rushed away up-
country. The solitary was a young woman, tall
and full-figured, with a clear pale face, scarlet lips,
and red hair waving softly above her delicate
brows and creamy neck.. She was dressed
luxuriously, yet with a careful simplicity, in
faintly-tinted thin silk and lace. As she stood on
the platform an elderly woman came up to her,
looked into her face inquiringly, and kissed her
in a sudden, unemotional way. She was tall and
spare, dressed in a dark linen gown with a coarse
hat tied under her square chin. Plain and even
common as her clothing was, there was an air of
dignity in her free, strong- movements. As the
girl stooped to take up the portmanteau that the
guard had put down beside her, the elder woman
prevented her, saying as she lifted it herself,
“I’ll take it. It would spoil your gloves.” She
spoke in char, unhurried tones that accentuated
the independence and dignity of her appearance.
In a few minutes the two women were sitting
in the shady front room of the stone cottage. It
was a plain room, the only noticeable furniture
being a shelf of books, solid-looking and well-worn.
“Well, well, to think you’re actually here ! ”
said the elder woman. “Doesn’t it seem
strange ? ”
“It does indeed,” assented the girl. She
seemed a little ill at ease, and her beautiful eyes
rested anywhere but on her companion’s. “You
always managed to keep it cool in here, mother,”
she said, irrelevantly; “that is, you can keep it
cool for the first few days of the heat, but it gets
dreadful when the stone is heated through, I
remember. You must come away, mother, as
I’ve so often asked you to; now that I’ve come
back to Australia to fetch you.”
“I don’t think so,” the elder woman said. “I
like my own ways, my, own work and my own
earnings too well. But it’s good to see you ; I’m
lonely here sometimes. I never was one to have
many friends—you. remember that ? Not that
there’s anyone within reach, anyhow.”
“Lonely ! ” said the girl. “ It’s heart-breaking.
I’d go mad if I lived here like you. Surely you’ll
pay me a visit while I’m in Melbourne, mother,
even if you don’t give this up altogether. You
could get leave, I suppose ? But I’d like you to
come Sack to Europe with me.”
“Would you?” said the mother, quietly. “I
might come to Melbourne while you’re there. You
mustn’t think, child, that I’m not delighted at
your success. It fills me with pride to hear of
your wonderful voice, and of people all over the
world bowing down to your singing. Yes, and I’m
pleased at your wanting to share it all with me,
even though I can’t possibly take what you offer.”
“Why can’t you, mother’ ” asked the girl, with
some irritation. “It is so ridiculous, your living
on alone here, working hard, when I have ever so
much more money than I know what to do with.
Parents oughtn’t to be proud about taking things
from their children, any more than children ought
to be proud towards parents.”
“The cases are quite different,” said the elder
woman. “ Or, anyhow, I feel them to be dif-
ferent. Besides, you are in a world that is utterly
strange to me. I should be awkward in it.”
“You would soon beat home in it,” said the
girl, eagerly. “You speak and move with more dig-
nity, as it is. than most of the women one meets.”
“ I don’t know that I want to be at home in
that world,” said the elder woman, slowly, ignor-
ing the compliment. After a moment, she went
on : “ You did not answer the question I wrote to
you, Dorothea.”
“ What question ? ” asked the girl.
“I think you know,’’said her mother; and waited.
“ I suppose,” said the singer, unwillingly, “you
mean your question about the truth of what people
say about my private affairs ? ”
“ Yes,” assented the other.
“Howl hate those newspapers!” the singer
burst out. “If it were not for them ——”
“ if it’s untrue,” interrupted her mother,
“ you’ve only got to say so. What do I care what
they say ? ”
“They exaggerate so,” said the singer, im-
patiently.
“ Exaggerate ? ” repeated the elder woman, in
her measured tones. “What do you mean ? A
thing of that kind is true or untrue. That’s all
that matters. They led one to believe that you
were living almost openly with a man who is not
your husband.”
The singer threw out her hands despairingly.
The struggle had to come, apparently.
“ Was it true ?” asked the mother.
“Yes,” said the singer, moodily.
Her mother looked at her with a kind of hard
curiosity. One would say that the word just
uttered had swept all memory of her motherhood
out of her heart. “ Why do you do it? ” she asked.
“ What is the use of my telling you about it ? ”
said the singer. “ You would never be willing to
understand.”
“ Perhaps not,” replied her mother. “ Yet you
had better try me. If you can’t make me under-
stand, as you call it, and if I can’t make you see
your wickedness ” ——
“ Mother,” broke in the girl, with almost pas-
sionate pleading in her voice, “Why won’t you
let me love you and take care of you as I want
to ? Why won’t you take the good that’s in me,
and—love me as any other mother would ?”
The elder woman ignored the tone and the
words altogether. “ Are you still living with this
man ? ” she asked.
The daughter’s face set into hard lines like her
mother’s. “ Yes,” she replied.
“ Won’t he marry you ?” her mother asked.
“I don’t know,” the singer replied, with a
cynical smile. “ I never asked him ; I daresay he
would, if I said he must.”
“Don’t you want to maery, then?” asked the
other.
“No, I don’t.”
“ Don’t you care for this man ? ”
“Yes, I care for him.” The daughter looked
at the inscrutable face opposite to her for a
moment or two, and then burst out, “What’s
the use of my answering you ? You can’t under-
stand. Why should I marry and bind myself
down like other women—like ordinary women?
I have a great gift—l have a voice that makes a
queen of me; and as well as that I am an artist —
I am a musician—l love my art. Oh, the two
things don’t always go together, I can tell you.
If you’d only come with me you’d see what
it all means, you who have suoh a splendid
mind, a hundred times quicker and stronger
than mine. Won’t you come?” She paused,
but the sombre face she gazed at gave no
stir of sympathy. She went on: “Well, I sup-
pose you will say that if I care so much for my
work —if I won’t marry—l ought to have nothing
to do with men. But I don’t choose to sacrifice
so much of the sweetness of life just because I
want to work. Why should a woman have to
choose between love and work? I have both. I
want both. Love is actually necessary to make
one understand and interpret great works. I
should be a half-developed, powerless creature
unless I knew what it means —this loving and
being loved. I choose to enjoy life and to know
life to the full—and to remain free.”
She stopped, and the elder woman repeated
after her —“ To know life to the full. Have you
a child, then ?” _ .
A rush of red came up over the singer’s fair
face. “No,” she said.
The mother smiled slowly, “lts
you who can’t understand,” she said. “ Why,
here in my loneliness, I know more of the realities
of things than you. Great joys and great sorrows,
delight and despair and death, these make a
a woman live to the full, and these come to wives
and mothers with a glory and an agony that such
as you can never know. You live for art ; what’s
art but a glorified plaything ! Live for realities,
and then you may speak of living to the full.”
There was a silence. Presently the singer said,
in a low voice, “ I do understand. Come with
me, mother.” '
“I’ve said nothing of the sin you are commit-
ting,” went on the mother, inexorably. “ And it
is that which hurts me most. If I came with you
would you promise to make an end of it now from
this moment ? ”
The singer’s face paled. “ No,” she said ;“ it
takes different people to make a world. It takes
my kind, as well as yours. I can admire your
kind, but I can’t alter myself. I will not.”
Her mother rose, as if there was nothing more
to say. “I must give you some tea, Dorothea,”
she remarked ; “you must be wanting it.” As
she went about preparing it, she continued, in
matter-of-faot tones, “ How long did you think of
staying with me ? ”
I have to be back in Melbourne very soon, ” said
the warbler. “lam to sing there, you know. I
thought I would stay while you get ready to come
with me.” She stopped, but the elder woman
said nothing. The singer continued, hesitatingly,
“If you would not stay at my hotel because of—
others who might be there, you could stay where
I could often come and see you ; and you could
come and hear me sing. It will be a great night.”
“I shall not come with you to Melbourne,” said
the other.
The singer rose to her feet with an angry ex-
clamation : “In that case, I see no use in my
staying at all,” she said. “Tell me what time the
next traintoMelbournestopshere, and I’llcatchit.”
“As you please,” said her mother, steadily.
“But there is plenty of time for you to take
something to eat and drink first. I can’t let you
go without that.”
They sat and talked commonplaces for the
remainder of their time together, and the mother
brought her daughter to the station in time for
the evening train to town. As it drew up to the
platform the singer leaned forward and kissed hei
mother’s firm, unbending lips. Then she got into
the carriage, and was carried back to the city, to
the passionate arms of her lover, to the adoring
admiration of her audiences, to soft living and
luxurious surroundings. But none of these will
ever deaden her vivid memory of that forlorn rail-
way platform, lonely and bare, with the brown
plains stretching from it on all sides, and the little
blue-stone cottage crouching beside it ; and,
before all, standing there in the red light of the
setting sun, the tall figure of her mother, dignified,
steadfast and alone.

WHY WE ADVERTISE!
Because Advertising* is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people,
to acquaint each other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large sums of money in studying that Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for him not to announce
the fact, and disseminate the knowledge universally, We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our' help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of your medical advisers, our method of treatment
may be, the fact still remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands of testimonials received from
grateful patientsi We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only claim to have utilised in a common-sense
way the result of scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
jealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if you allow the disease to run riot in your system, you
are guilty .o£ culpable neglect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.
